History of CCB status 
This is the first version for seeking validation under both the Climate 
Community, and Biodiversity Standard, as well as the Verified Carbon 
Standard. 
Gold Level criteria 
The project aims to achieve the Triple Gold Level criteria for Climate 
Change Adaptation Benefits, Exceptional Benefits for Community (GL2) and 
Biodiversity. 
Climate Change Adaptation Benefits: KPCP will implement activities to 
mitigate climate change’s impacts on the local communities and 
biodiversity within the project area. Detailed information is provided in 
section 3.4. 
Exceptional Benefits for Community: Around 50% of the households in the 
project area is below national poverty line. KPCP aims to deliver both short-
term and long-term benefits to all communities. Detailed information is 
provided in section 4.5. 
Exceptional Benefits for Biodiversity: The project area is home to 45 
species recorded within the project area that are classified as threatened 
under the IUCN Red List. KPCP will protect and enhance the local habitat 
for these species. Detailed information is provided in section 5.5.  
Expected verification 
schedule

Canopy Carbon

1.2 Standardized Benefit Metrics 
 
1
 Land with woody vegetation that meets an internationally accepted definition (e.g., UNFCCC, FAO, or IPCC) of what constitutes a forest, 
which includes threshold parameters, such as minimum forest area, tree height and level of crown cover, and may include mature, 
secondary, degraded and wetland forests (VCS Program Definitions) 
2
 Reduced emissions from deforestation and forest degradation (REDD) - Activities that reduce GHG emissions by slowing or stopping 
conversion of forests to non-forest land and/or reduce the degradation of forest land where forest biomass is lost (VCS Program 
Definitions) 
3
 Afforestation, reforestation and revegetation (ARR) - Activities that increase carbon stocks in woody biomass (and in some cases soils) by 
establishing, increasing and/or restoring vegetative cover through the planting, sowing and/or human-assisted natural regeneration of 
woody vegetation (VCS Program Definitions) 
4
 Improved forest management (IFM) - Activities that change forest management practices and increase carbon stock on forest lands 
managed for wood products such as saw timber, pulpwood, and fuelwood (VCS Program Definitions)

2 PROJECT DETAILS 
2.1 Project Goals, Design and Long-Term Viability  
2.1.1 Summary Description of the Project (VCS, 3.2, 3.6, 3.10, 3.11, 3.13, 3.14; CCB, 
G1.2) 
Tropical forests are crucial carbon pools due to their remarkable capacity to store vast amounts of 
carbon in their dense biomass and soils. These ecosystems act as natural carbon sinks, absorbing 
substantial  amounts  of  atmospheric  carbon  dioxide.  However,  their  capacity  to  mitigate  climate 
change  is  increasingly  threatened  by  deforestation  and  land  degradation,  which  release  stored 
carbon back into the atmosphere.  
Indonesia has one of the highest deforestation rates globally. National forest cover has declined 
sharply over the decades, dropping from 78.3% of the country's total land area in 1950 to 63.8% 
in 1982, and further to 46.8% by 2017. The regions most affected are Sumatra and Kalimantan, 
with  average  annual  deforestation  rates  of  approximately  356,100  hectares  and  303,360 
hectares, respectively
12
.The primary drivers of deforestation in the region include the expansion of 
industrial  oil  palm  and  pulpwood  plantations,  along  with  agricultural  development.  Timber 
plantations contributed  significantly,  accounting  for  14%  of  deforestation  between  2001  and 
2016
13
. 
The Kapuas Pisau Conservation Project (KPCP) aims to protect and restore 13,580 ha of secondary 
swamp forest located situated across two districts Pulang Pisau and Kapuas in Central Kalimantan.  
The  area  is rich  in  biodiversity  and  home  to many  vulnerable  and  critically  endangered  species 
including Bornean Orangutan  (Pongo  pygmaeus),  Bornean  white-bearded  gibbon  (Hylobates 
Albibarbis) and Sun Bear (Helarctos malayanus). 
The project is located entirely within a state-designated production forest area, for which the forest 
management  company  PT Ramang Agro Lestari (PT RAL) held a concession permit. PT RAL was 
legally authorized to convert the existing natural forest into an Industrial Timber Plantation under 
a Forest Utilization Business License, issued by the Ministry of Environment and Forestry through 
Decree No. SK.7310/MenLHK-PHPL/UHP/HPL.1/9/2019. 
In 2023, the concession was transferred to PT Indo Hutan Ekosistem, which reoriented the project 
toward  forest  conservation  and  ecological  restoration.  To  formalize  this  shift  in  management 
objectives, an amendment to the Forest Utilization Business License was submitted and approved 
by Ministry of Environment and Forestry: Decree No. SK.13046/MenLHK-
HPL/PUPH/HPL.1/12/2023, reflecting the adoption of environmentally sustainable direction. 
 
12
 Antonio Santoro, Francesco Piras, and Qingyi Yu, “Spatial Analysis of Deforestation in Indonesia in the Period 1950–2017 and the Role 
of Protected Areas,” Biodiversity and Conservation, published July 2, 2023, https://doi.org/10.1007/s10531-023-02679-8 
13
 Kemen G. Austin, Amanda Schwantes, Yaofeng Gu, and Prasad S. Kasibhatla, “What Causes Deforestation in Indonesia?,” 
Environmental Research Letters 14, no. 2 (February 1, 2019): 024007, https://doi.org/10.1088/1748-9326/aaf6db. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
11 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
In the absence of the Kapuas Pisau Conservation Project (KPCP), the secondary swamp forest in the 
area would be at high risk of deforestation and conversion into industrial timber plantations, primarily 
using fast-growing species such as Acacia and Eucalyptus. Additionally, parts of the land would likely 
be used for community cultivation of Elaeis guineensis (oil palm). 
KPCP  aims  to  establish  and  implement  a  sustainable  land-use  model  focused  on  reducing 
emissions from deforestation, enhancing carbon sequestration through the restoration of native 
forest species, conserving biodiversity, and improving the livelihoods of local communities within 
the project area. Specific objectives focused on climate, biodiversity and community are outlined 
as follows: 
Climate objectives 
• Reduce  CO₂ emissions  by  preventing  planned  deforestation,  illegal  logging,  and  forest 
fires. 
• Restore degraded areas within the ecosystem through the reintroduction of native species 
and by supporting natural forest regeneration processes. 
• Conduct   regular   training   and   outreach   programs   with   local   communities   to   raise 
awareness   about   the   importance   of   ecosystem   conservation   and   sustainable   land 
management. 
Community objectives 
• Improve   local community’s   economy by   introducing   sustainable   income-generating 
opportunities and providing capacity-building training. 
• Enhance ecosystem health within the project area to support the long-term well-being of 
local communities. 
• Support community development through targeted strategic initiatives. 
• Ensure continued access to natural resources and promote their sustainable use by local 
communities. 
Biodiversity objectives 
• Conserve and protect ecosystems to support the natural habitats of flora and fauna within 
the project area. 
• Address and mitigate external drivers of deforestation and degradation that threaten the 
survival of rare, threatened, and endangered (RTE) species. 
• Collaborate with relevant institutions to develop and implement initiatives that enhance 
biodiversity and strengthen ecosystem integrity.

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The  project  will  generate  net  Green  House  Gas  (GHG)  emission  reduction  and removals  of 
2,449,708 tCO2e over a 30-year crediting period, with an average annual reduction of 81,657 
tCO2e. 
KPCP is an independent project and not located within any jurisdictional REDD+ program.

2.1.4 Project Eligibility (VCS, 3.1, 3.6, 3.8, 3.18, 4.1; CCB Program Rules, 4.2.4, 4.6.4) 
The  project  is eligible  to  participate  in  both the  VCS  and  CCB programs due  to  the  following 
criteria: 
• The project falls under VCS Scope 14, covering Agriculture, Forestry, and Other Land Use 
(AFOLU), and includes activities under three AFOLU project categories in accordance with 
VCS Section 3.2.1: 
o 
REDD
 (Reducing Emissions from Deforestation and Forest Degradation), involving 
conservation of secondary forests through preventing industrial timber plantation 
and illegal logging. 
 
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
13 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
o 
ARR
 (Afforestation,   Reforestation,   and   Revegetation),   through agroforestry, 
assisted   natural   regeneration,   and planting   of   native   species   to   reforest 
shrubland and bare land areas. 
o 
WRC
 (Wetlands  Restoration  and  Conservation),  through  the protection  of  intact 
wetland areas from drainage and industrial plantation activities. 
• The project applies the latest versions of the VCS methodologies VM0007 and VM0047. 
Both  methodologies  are  approved  under  the  VCS  Program  and are  appropriate for the 
project context and activities, in accordance with VCS Sections 3.1.2 and 3.1.3. Further 
elaboration on the choice of methodology and baseline scenario can be found in Section 
3.1.2. 
• In compliance with VCS Section 3.1.4, the implementation of project activities does not 
result in a violation of any applicable local, national, or international law. 
• The  project  area  is  not  situated  within  a  jurisdiction  that  is  covered  by  a  jurisdictional 
REDD+ program, as described in VCS Section 3.2.2. 
• The project start date is 1 March 2023, and the pipeline listing and validation processes 
are expected to be completed by the end of 2025. This timeline aligns with the validation 
and listing deadlines for AFOLU projects outlined in VCS Section 3.8. 
• As  this  is  a  new  project,  it  is  currently  seeking registration/validation  approval. In 
accordance  with  VCS  Section  3.18.9, a 30-day public  comment  period  will  be  initiated 
once the project is listed on the project pipeline. Subsequently, the validation process will 
be conducted in accordance with VCS Section 4.1, and the validation report and validation 
statement will be submitted within one year of the initiation of the public comment period 
as  stated under CCB  Program  Rules  Sections  4.2.4  and  4.6.4.  Once  validation  is 
completed, the project will proceed to registration under the VCS Program. 
2.1.5 Transfer Project Eligibility (VCS, 3.23, Appendix 2) 
Not applicable. The project is not a part of any other GHG program, nor is it intended to be part 
of other GHG programs.

PT Indo Hutan Ekosistem (PT IHE) is the rightful and legitimate owner of the project, holding full 
authority, responsibilities, and rights over its implementation and management. PT Ramang Agro 
Lestari, a subsidiary of PT IHE, holds the forest concession rights granted under the Decree of 
the  Minister  of  Environment  and  Forestry  No.  SK.13046/MenLHKHPL/PUPH/HPL.1/12/2023. 
Official proof of ownership and concession rights can be provided upon request 
2.1.10 Project Start Date (VCS, 3.8) 
Project start date 
 
01-March-2023 
Justification 
 
The project start date is defined as 01 March 2023. This date reflects 
the transition period during which PT Indo Hutan Ekosistem acquired 
PT  Ramang  Agro  Lestari and  reoriented  the  project  from  its  original 
baseline  as  an  industrial  timber  plantation  to  a  fully  conservation-
focused  initiative.  The acquisition was completed in March 2023 and 
conservation activities within the project area commenced immediately 
thereafter.

and community well-being will be continuously monitored. 
2.1.12 Differences in Assessment/Project Crediting Periods (CCB, G1.9) 
There are no differences between the assessment periods for GHG emissions accounting, 
climate adaptive capacity and resilience, community, and biodiversity.

2.1.14 Physical Parameters (CCB, G1.3) 
2.1.14.1 General condition of the project area 
The  project  is  located  within  the  concession  area  of  PT Ramang Agro  Lestari  (PT  RAL).  It covers 
approximately 13,580 hectares, located within the jurisdiction of the Kapuas Forest Management 
Unit (KPHP Kapuas) in Central Kalimantan Province, Indonesia. Historically, there has been little 
change in  the  landcover,  the area is characterized  by  a  mix  of  natural  swamp  forests  and 
anthropogenically  influenced  land,  including small-scale oil  palm  plantations (in  the west  of  the 
concession area) and bare lands resulting from previous land clearing.  
The  concession area is accessible  through  a  network  of  local  roads. Administratively, it spans 
across  two  districts:  Pulang  Pisau  and  Kapuas,  and  directly  borders  eight  villages,  including 
Humbang Raya, Hanua, Hurung, Lawang Uru, Manen Kaleka, Bereng Ramang, Parahangan, and 
Manen Paduran (
Figure 1
). 
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
18 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Figure 1: Project area map 
 
The physical parameters of the KPP area are described in the sub-paragraphs below. 
2.1.14.2 Topography 
The project area exhibits a varied topographic profile, though it is largely characterized by flat to 
gently sloping terrain (
Figure 2
). Based on slope gradient classification using FAO standards, the 
area spans across seven slope categories (
Table 1
). 
Flat terrain, with slopes less than 0.2%, constitutes a very small portion of the concession, covering 
approximately 72 hectares or 0.53% of the total area. Level areas (0.2%–1%) make up about 473 
hectares or 3.49%, while nearly level zones (1%–2%) account for 1,544 hectares or 11.36% of the 
area. 
Very  gently  sloping  land  (2%–5%)  is  the  most  dominant  slope  class,  covering  6,265 hectares, 
equivalent  to  46.14%  of  the  project  area.  Gently  sloping  terrain  (5%–10%)  follows,  with  4,336 
hectares or 31.93% of the area. 
Strongly  sloping  areas  (10%–15%)  comprise  774  hectares,  or  5.68%  of  the  concession,  while 
moderately steep land (15%–30%) accounts for 116 hectares or 0.85% of the area. 
This  distribution  reflects  a  landscape  predominantly  suitable  for  ecosystem  restoration  and 
sustainable  forest  management, with  only  a  small  portion  of  the area  requiring  targeted  soil 
conservation and technical mitigation to minimize land degradation risks.

2.1.14.3 Geology 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

The concession area is predominantly located on a relatively uniform geological formation. Most 
of it is underlain by the Dahor Formation
15
 (
Figure 3
), a sedimentary rock unit from the Quaternary 
to  younger  Tertiary  period.  This  formation  contributes  to  the  area’s  flat  and  slightly  undulating 
landscape.  
In  the  southeastern  part  of  the  concession,  particularly  near  Humbang  Raya  village,  there  are 
alluvial deposits (
Figure 3
), formed from the natural sedimentation processes of the river system. 
These deposits consist of sand, silt, and clay, indicative of lowland areas with relatively higher soil 
fertility. 
Figure 3: Geology map of PT RAL

variety  of  soil  types  across  five  suborders: Typic  Fluvaquents  (Entisols),  Typic  Haplohemists 
(Histosols),     Typic     Haplosaprists     (Histosols),     Typic     Hapludults     (Ultisols),     and     Typic

2.1.14.5 Climate 
The climate in the PT RAL project area is classified as tropical rainforest (climate type Af; Köppen-
Geiger climate classification),  with  consistently  high  rainfall  and  no  significant  dry  season.  The 
annual  average  rainfall  ranges  from  3,093  to  3,300  mm,  while that  of  central to  southern  part, 
including  the areas  around  Humbang Raya Village, can range  from 3,300  to 3,387  mm. The 
monthly rainfall data was collected for the year of 2024 with a total annual rainfall of 3,305.36 mm

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
(
Table 3
). The rainy season typically occurs from November to April, and the dry season from May 
to October, though rainfall remains substantial year-round.

2.1.14.6 Hydrology 
The project area falls within two major watersheds: the Barito River Basin and the Kahayan River 
Basin (
Figure 7
). The  Barito  River  system  dominates  the  western  portion  of  the  concession, 
flowing  toward  the  southern  coast  and  supporting  ecosystems  along  its route.  Meanwhile, the 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
24 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Kahayan  River  Basin  covers  the  eastern  segment,  flowing  northward  and  carrying  water  from 
upstream areas to the coastal regions. Both rivers are critical to the region’s hydrological balance, 
supporting  local  communities  and  maintaining  ecological  processes  such  as  groundwater 
recharge, peat moisture retention, and flood mitigation. 
Within the project zone, three main rivers serve as key ecological features: the Kahayan River to 
the west, the Mangkutub River to the east, and the Kaliwang River, which flows diagonally across 
the  concession  from  northwest  to  southeast.  These  rivers  are vital  for  flood  regulation,  water 
quality maintenance, and soil stabilization. 
However, extensive gold and zircon mining along the Kahayan River has led to severe degradation 
of its riparian zones, resulting in increased soil erosion and water pollution. Similarly, the riparian 
areas along the Kaliwang and Mangkutub Rivers have been heavily impacted by illegal logging 
and deforestation, causing a significant loss of critical ecosystem services. 
Figure 7: Watershed Map of PT RAL 
 
2.1.14.7 Land cover 
Land cover classification was conducted based on Sentinel-2A satellite imagery (acquired during 
January – July  2023)  and  ground-truthing  through  field  surveys.  The  classification  used  both 
supervised and on-screen digitization methods, achieving an overall accuracy of 93% and a Kappa 
statistic of 0.81, indicating high reliability.

significance for ecological restoration and carbon sequestration.

2.1.15 Social Parameters (VCS, 3.18; CCB, G1.3) 
2.1.15.1 Main settlements 
The  project  PT Ramang Agro  Lestari  operates across  two  administrative  districts  in  Central 
Kalimantan  Province:  Kapuas  Regency  and  Pulang  Pisau  Regency.  Within  these  regions,  the 
company’s  operational  landscape  intersects  with  eight  key  villages  that  form  the  primary  social 
units,  located  within  or  adjacent  to  the  project’s  concession  boundary  (
Figure 10
).  In  Kapuas 
Regency,  RAL’s  area  includes  Humbang  Raya  Village,  located  within  Mantangai  Subdistrict.  In 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4

2.1.15.2 Population and Socio-cultural Information 
The  total  population  across  the  eight  study  villages  is  6,036  individuals,  residing  in  1,509 
households, consiting of 3,161 male (52.4%) and 2,875 fenale (47.6%)
16
. 
The  communities  across  these  eight  villages  are  predominantly  composed  of  indigenous  Dayak 
Ngaju  ethnic  group  members.  A  defining  characteristic  of  the  Dayak  community  is  their  strong 
connection with nature and a high dependency on natural resources. While Dayak Ngaju are the 
majority, some villages also host residents from other ethnic groups, such as Javanese, Madurese, 
and Banjar, who are migrants but have integrated closely with the Dayak culture and values over 
time.  
The local population practices a variety of religion, including Islam, Roman Catholicism, Protestant 
Christianity, and Hinduism. Among these, Protestant Christianity is the most widely adhered to. This 
 
16
 https://www.bps.go.id/id 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
28 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
presence of multiple religions reflects a high level of religious tolerance and respect for diversity 
within  the  Dayak  Ngaju  community.  Beyond  formal  religions,  traditional  beliefs,  particularly 
Kaharingan,  remain  preserved  by  some  community  members  and  are  passed  down  through 
generations – including traditional death ritual (Tiwah), gratitude ceremony to God (Pakanan Sahur 
Lewu),  misfortune  cleansing  ritual  (Mamapas  Lewu),  and  healing  ritual  for  supernatural  forces 
(Badewa).  
2.1.15.3 Land Use 
The land use patterns and economic well-being of the population across the eight villages surveyed 
by PT Ramang Agro Lestari—Humbang Raya, Hanua, Hurung, Lawang Uru, Manen Kaleka, Manen 
Paduran,  Bereng Ramang,  and  Parahangan—are  deeply  interconnected,  reflecting  a  complex 
interplay of traditional practices, development pressures, and socio-economic vulnerabilities. The 
land  use patterns  have undergone  significant  transformations,  moving  away  from  traditional 
practices due to various socio-economic pressures, government regulations, and the emergence of 
new  economic  activities,  which  include  the  introduction  of  large-scale  plantations  and  informal 
mining. 
Traditionally,  the  Dayak  Ngaju  community's  livelihoods  included  shifting  cultivation,  hunting,  and 
gathering forest products. However, these traditional land use practices have undergone significant 
changes  due  to  development  dynamics  in  Central  Kalimantan,  particularly  the  expansion  of  the 
forestry,  plantation,  and  mining  sectors.  Government  regulations  prohibiting  land  clearing  by 
burning  have  led  to  the  cessation  of  traditional  shifting  cultivation.  The  expansion  of  plantation 
areas has reduced traditional hunting grounds, altering the community's way of life and economic 
activities. Over  the  past  decade,  gold  mining  has  emerged  as  a  significant  source  of  income  for 
local communities, especially in the areas surrounding the Kahayan River. This activity has rapidly 
gained popularity due to its relatively low entry barriers and the potential for quick financial returns. 
However, the expansion of gold mining has brought with it several risks to environment. Gold mining 
operations  have  led  to  artificial  widening  of  the  river,  which  increases  the  risk  of  flooding  and 
contributes to the pollution of river water. 
Figure 11: Community gold mining near Kahayan River 
     
Many local communities have recently turned to oil palm cultivation and community-owned rubber 
plantation, hoping it will provide steady income. These plantations, mostly established within the 
last five years, were chosen for their ease of growth, market access, and available land. However, 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
29 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
most  lack  economic  comparisons  with  other  crops.  Alongside  oil  palm  and  rubber,  communities 
also grow fruit and small-scale vegetables on their own land for household use. 
Additionally,  there  has  been  a  relocation  of  settlements  from  the  Kahayan  River  basin  to  inland 
areas  closer  to  the  Trans-Kalimantan  Road  access,  driven  by  a  desire  for  improved  access  to 
infrastructure and public services such as electricity. 
2.1.15.4 Employment and Economic Well-being 
The primary source of income for most household heads is gold mining, an informal and seasonal 
sector.  Specifically, according  to  social  baseline  study  conducted  in  June  2025, 67.9%  of 
household heads identify gold mining as their primary occupation, indicating a significant reliance 
on   artisanal   and   small-scale   mining   activities   as   the   main   economic   pillar   among   male 
breadwinners. Villages with particularly high dependence on gold mining include Bereng Ramang 
(55%),  Parahangan  (47%),  and  Hanua  (36%).  This  heavy  dependence  on  gold  mining  is  noted 
across all villages. It was reported this shift to gold mining began in the early 1990s, prompted by 
declining  rubber  prices  after  1994,  which  made  traditional  rubber  tapping  and  smallholder 
agriculture unstable. Local testimonies suggest that gold mining initially offered substantial short-
term financial gains. While economically attractive, this reliance exposes households to long-term 
risks such as income volatility, environmental degradation, unsafe labor conditions, and limited job 
security.  This  also  reflects  a  narrow  economic  base  and  makes  local  economies  vulnerable  to 
depletion cycles as gold reserves diminish. 
Understanding their dependency on gold mining and the associated risks, some household heads 
pursue   secondary   occupations.   These   additional   activities   commonly   include   farming   and 
gardening  (for  consumption  or  local  sales),  fishing,  small-scale  animal  husbandry  (like  raising 
poultry or pigs), and operating small-scale trade businesses such as kiosks or food stalls. These 
alternative  activities  help  stabilize  household  income  during  uncertain  periods  and  demonstrate 
the community's continued reliance on natural resources and traditional livelihood skills. However, 
despite  these  efforts,  the  overall  employment  structure  remains  largely  informal  and  highly 
economically vulnerable, lacking stable income or long-term job security.  
The  overall  employment  siutation  is  one  of  economic  precarity,  where  communities  are  heavily 
reliant on volatile, non-renewable, and informal sectors like gold mining. This, at the same time, 
also  contributes  to  varying  levels  of  unemployment  and  underemployment.  A  notable  portion of 
villagers reported no primary occupation or "Unemployed", in which Manen Kaleka Village records 
the  highest  unemployment  rate  at  27%. This  suggests  that  even  within  the  dominant  informal 
economy,  opportunities  are  not  limitless,  and  the  skills  of  the  available  workforce  may  not  align 
with the demands of the emerging or declining sectors. 
Female population, spouses (wives), posed a limited economic contribution, with 69.2% from our 
survey serving as full-time housewives not involved in income-generating activities. This imbalance 
places  a  heavy  reliance  on  the  household  head's  income,  narrowing  the  family’s  economic 
adaptability when the primary income is disrupted. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
30 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Overall, the economic well-being across the eight villages is marked by significant vulnerability and 
a heavy reliance on informal and extractive sectors. This structural fragility in employment of the 
eight villages highlights the urgent need for livelihood diversification strategies that can improve 
household resilience and reduce socio-economic vulnerability over time.  
2.1.15.5 Health 
Most villages have access to integrated health services for toddlers (Posyandu Balita), integrated 
health services for the elderly (Posyandu Lansia), auxiliary health centers (PUSTU), and community 
health  centers  (Puskesmas).  Accordingly  to  the  villagers, easy  access  is  found  in  Lawang  Uru, 
Humbang Raya, Bereng Ramang, Hanua, and Manen Paduran villages; moderate access is noted 
in Parahangan Village; and difficult access is primarily experienced by Manen Kaleka Village. The 
most reported diseases across the eight villages are high blood pressure (35% of cases), diarrhea 
(31%), back pain/osteoporosis (11%), asthma (7%), upper respiratory tract infection (7%), and skin 
diseases  (3%).  For  children  aged  7  to  21,  diarrhea  is  the  most  serious  health  concern,  affecting 
75% of the total number of children recorded. 
2.1.15.6 Education  
The majority of the community has completed junior high or senior high school education. Senior 
high school graduation is the most common level achieved, with 19.48% of men and women in our 
survey reported completing it, followed by junior secondary school, and elementary school. Higher 
education is very restricted and even a small portion of respondents (particularly in Manen Kaleka, 
Parahangan, and Hanua) have never received any formal education. The limitation in education is 
partly due to the inaccessibility to education facilities; though elementary schools are available in 
all villages, junior high schools are only present in Hanua, Lawang Uru, and Humbang Raya, and 
only  Hanua  Village  has  a  senior  high  school.  The  other  social-economic  reasons  include  limited 
financial capacity, early marriage, and especially when gold-mining offers a quick gain with minimal 
entry requirements which encourages dropouts. 
2.1.16 Project Zone Map and Project Location (VCS, 3.11, 3.18; CCB, G1.4-7, G1.13, 
CM1.2, B1.2) 
2.1.16.1 Project Area 
The  project  is  situated  across  two  districts—Pulang  Pisau  and  Kapuas—in  Central  Kalimantan, 
Republic of Indonesia. It encompasses a total area of 13,580 hectares, as defined by the PBPH 
license, and overlaps with the territories of eight villages.

2.1.16.2 Project Zone 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
32 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The project zone is defined as the broader area encompassing the project site(s) where activities 
directly impacting land and natural resources are implemented. These activities include, but are 
not limited to, the provision of alternative livelihoods and community development initiatives. The 
zone extends beyond the project area to include the full administrative boundaries of the eight 
affected villages, as well as the surrounding ecological landscapes connected to the project. The 
total size of the project zone is 142,123 ha. 
 
Figure 13: Project Zone Map 
 
For  broader  landscape  analysis  and  contextual  understanding,  a  5-kilometer  buffer  around  the 
project area was applied to assess High Conservation Value (HCV) areas. Multiple locations within 
the project have been identified as containing HCVs (section 4.1.3 and section 5.1.2). The project's 
activities  are  designed  to  generate  positive  impacts  across  climate,  community,  and  biodiversity 
dimensions, thereby contributing to the conservation and enhancement of these critical HCV areas. 
The project is designed to deliver wide-ranging and long-lasting impacts extending well beyond the 
immediate  boundaries  of  the  project  area.  Through  a  combination  of  targeted  interventions, 
including forest protection, wetland conservation, and reforestation, the project aims to restore and 
maintain  essential  ecological  functions  that  are  critical  to  the  resilience  of  the  surrounding 
landscape. 
One of the key environmental benefits lies in the prevention of soil erosion and the preservation of 
healthy soils, which are vital for sustaining ecosystem health. Reforestation and the conservation 
of  peatlands  and  swamp  forests  also  contribute  significantly  to  stabilizing  the  local  hydrological 
cycle. These activities help regulate water flow, reduce surface runoff, and enhance groundwater 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
33 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
recharge, ultimately  maintaining  water  availability  during  dry  periods  and  reducing  the  risk  and 
severity of seasonal droughts. 
The project area forms part of a larger, interconnected forest ecosystem, and its protection plays a 
crucial role in conserving HCV areas. Swamp forests within the project zone serve as vital habitats 
for  numerous  endemic  and  threatened  species.  By  safeguarding  these  habitats,  the  project 
supports biodiversity conservation, species recovery, and the overall integrity of the ecosystem. 
In  addition  to  environmental  outcomes,  the  project  incorporates  a  strong  social  component  that 
seeks to uplift the well-being of communities living in and around the project zone. Activities such 
as  improving  access  to  education,  providing  healthcare  support,  and  developing  alternative  and 
sustainable livelihood opportunities are central to the project's community engagement strategy. 
These  initiatives  not  only  address  immediate  social  needs  but  also  contribute  to  long-term 
community resilience and sustainable development. 
2.1.17 Project Activities and Theory of Change (VCS, 3.6; CCB, G1.8) 
The KPCP is designed to promote a sustainable land management model that minimizes emissions 
from  deforestation,  increases  carbon  storage  through  the  regeneration  of  native  forests, and 
protects biological diversity. Currently, the project area, which includes secondary swamp forests, 
wet  shrubland,  and  degraded  lands,  faces  ongoing  threats from forest degradation,  drainage-
induced hydrological changes, recurring fires, and habitat fragmentation resulted from industrial 
timber plantation activities and historical land clearing. Meanwhile, local communities continue to 
face  a  range  of  development  challenges,  including  limited access  to  sustainable  livelihoods, 
education, healthcare, infrastructure, and agricultural markets. These constraints are particularly 
acute  in  remote  villages,  where  opportunities  for  economic  advancement  and  social  services 
remain scarce. Therefore,  while  restoring ecosystem  function, safeguarding key  conservation 
values, and contributing  to climate  stability,  the  project  also  seeks  to  generate  long-term social 
benefits for local communities in line with VCS and CCB program objectives. 
The  project’s  activities  were  developed  through  a  systematic  process  that  integrates  scientific 
analysis,  local  knowledge,  and  participatory  planning. This process began  with  a  comprehensive 
assessment  of  the biodiversity and  social  conditions  within  the  project  area,  allowing  the 
identification of key risks, pressures, and opportunities across the landscape. Activities were then 
designed  to  respond  directly  to  these  baseline  findings,  ensuring  that  each  intervention  is both 
evidence-based and contextually appropriate. The  theory  of  change  model shown in 
Figure 14
 
below describes our project interventions and demonstrates how they can help to meet our project 
objectives.
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
34 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Figure 14: KPCP’s Theory of Change 
 
To  ensure  coherence  and  effectiveness,  activities are designed  to  create  impacts  across  3 key 
domains  aligned  with  the  project’s  core  objectives:  climate  mitigation,  biodiversity  conservation, 
and  community  development. Each  activity  is  structured  to  contribute  to  clear,  measurable 
outcomes and to form part of a broader pathway of change (See Appendix 1: Project Activities and 
Theory  of  Change  Table for  more  details). Overall, the design  process  emphasizes  sustainability, 
community  ownership,  and  institutional  alignment,  ensuring  that  interventions  are  feasible  to 
implement, socially legitimate, and aligned with the principles of the VCS and CCB Standards. 
Below are 8 key activities covered in our project: 
Avoiding deforestation and degradation 
To prevent further deforestation and degradation, the project secured a legal license for ecosystem 
restoration, formally designating the area for long-term forest conservation. This legal protection 
will safeguard secondary swamp forests from conversion into industrial timber plantations, which 
poses a major threat to ecology and biodiversity. In parallel, satellite imagery and remote sensing 
analysis will be used to monitor land cover changes within and around the project area, enabling 
early detection of deforestation risks and providing verifiable data for MRV. Community workshops 
and  outreach  sessions  will  also  be  conducted  to  raise  awareness  about  the  purpose  and 
regulations of these conservation areas. By combining legal protection, community engagement, 
and  satellite-based  monitoring,  the  project  will  maintain  intact forest  cover,  reduce  emissions, 
preserve biodiversity, and protect critical ecosystem services. 
Forest patrolling 
To deter illegal logging, hunting, and unauthorized land use, the project will establish a community-
based  patrol  system  integrated  with  the  Spatial  Monitoring  and  Reporting  Tool  (SMART).  Patrol 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
35 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
teams comprising trained local residents will be equipped with GPS units, fire detection tools, and 
SMART  Mobile  devices  to  collect  georeferenced  field  data  on  threats,  patrol  routes,  and  wildlife 
observations.  Using  SMART’s  standardized  data  collection  and  centralized  reporting  system, 
information  from  the  field  will  be  quickly  processed  into  maps,  charts,  and  summaries  to  inform 
adaptive patrol planning and enforcement actions. Patrols will be conducted on foot, bicycle, boat, 
or by drone, depending on terrain accessibility, and coordinated with forestry agencies to ensure 
legal backing for action taken.  
Fire prevention and monitoring 
To mitigate the risk of fire in fire-prone wet shrubland and degraded forest edges, the project will 
establish  a  comprehensive  fire  prevention  and  monitoring  system.  This  system  will  combine 
satellite-based early warning mechanisms, such as NASA’s FIRMS hotspot detection, with on-the-
ground  patrols  and  drone  surveillance  to  ensure  rapid  identification  of  fire  risks  in  high-priority 
zones. Patrol activities will be intensified during the dry season, when vegetation becomes highly 
flammable and fire incidence typically increases. Local communities will be trained and equipped 
to form Fire Response Units (FRU), capable not only of fire suppression but also of implementing 
preventive  measures  such  as  creating  fire  breaks  and  conducting  controlled  burning  where 
necessary. By  integrating  technology,  patrols,  and  community  preparedness,  the  project  aims  to 
significantly reduce fire occurrence and minimize its impact on carbon emissions, biodiversity, and 
local livelihoods. 
Restoration and agroforestry programs 
To  reverse  the  legacy  effects  of  land  clearing  and  logging,  the  project  will  implement  two  main 
restoration strategies. Firstly, direct planting of native tree species will be carried out in shrubland 
and bare land areas that are accessible for active restoration. Secondly, in remote or difficult-to-
access areas, assisted natural regeneration (ANR) techniques will be applied by protecting naturally 
occurring  seedlings,  preventing  wildfires,  and  reducing  human  disturbance  to  accelerate  natural 
forest recovery. In parallel, the project will develop an agroforestry program near residential and 
non-wetland  areas,  integrating  native  tree  species  with  locally  preferred  crops  such  as  durian, 
rattan, mango,  etc. Together,  these  interventions  will  stabilize  forest  structure,  enhance  carbon 
sequestration, improve habitat quality, and strengthen community engagement in sustainable land 
use, particularly in areas previously affected by land clearing and illegal logging. 
Diversifying livelihoods for local communities 
To reduce the reliance on unsustainable economic activities, the project will promote alternative 
incomes through   agroforestry,   skills   development,   and employment. As   mentioned   above, 
agroforestry program will   be   implemented   to   create   long-term and   sustainable income 
opportunities. At  the  same  time,  the  project  will  also  provide  training  on  sustainable  non-timber 
forest products (NTFPs), such as honey, rattan, and medicinal plants, to further diversify community 
livelihoods while maintaining forest integrity. Lastly, local villagers will be prioritized for employment 
in  restoration,  patrolling,  and  monitoring  activities,  ensuring  that  conservation  efforts  generate 
direct economic benefits. These interventions aim to address the limited availability of sustainable 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
36 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
livelihoods,  particularly  for  communities  currently  dependent  on  mining,  logging,  and  declining 
rubber production. 
Community support grants 
The  project  will offer financial grants  to  support  community-led  initiatives  that  improve  shared 
infrastructure  and  preserve  cultural  traditions.  These  grants  will  focus  on  projects  such  as  the 
construction  or  renovation  of  schools,  community  halls,  or  places  of  worship,  ensuring  that 
resources are invested in facilities with long-term collective benefits. Support may also extend to 
traditional  festivals  and  customs  that  play  a  vital  role  in  maintaining  cultural  identity  and 
strengthening  social  cohesion.  By  prioritizing  community-driven initiatives,  the  project  seeks  to 
enhance well-being, reinforce cultural values, and foster stronger, more resilient villages. 
Empowering youth through education 
To  address  persistent  educational  challenges  in  the  project  area,  particularly  high  dropout  rates 
after high school, the project will implement targeted interventions aimed at improving access to 
and  continuity  in  education.  Scholarship  programs  will  be developed with  a  special  focus  on 
supporting girls and students from low-income households. Additionally, the project will support the 
integration of forest ecology and biodiversity awareness into school curricula and establish school-
forest partnerships through student eco-clubs to build environmental stewardship among youth. 
Healthcare outreach program 
The project will implement a community healthcare outreach program to address the prevalence 
of preventable illnesses and the limited access to formal medical services within the project area. 
Doctors  and  trained  health  professionals  will  be  invited  to  conduct  workshops  on  key  topics, 
including disease prevention, nutrition, maternal and child health, basic emergency care, and first 
aid. These sessions will strengthen community knowledge of preventive healthcare practices and 
provide practical skills for responding to medical emergencies in remote villages. By improving local 
capacity  and  awareness in  preventive  healthcare,  this  activity  contributes  to  overall  community 
well-being and resilience. 
2.1.18 Sustainable Development Contributions (VCS, 3.17) 
The KPCP is aligned with several sustainable development and environmental protection targets 
set  by  the  Government  of  Indonesia,  including  the  National  Medium-Term  Development  Plan 
2025-2029 (RPJMN). The following goals align directly with our project core objectives, including 
climate mitigation, biodiversity conservation, and community development. 
 
Indonesia’s RPJMN 2025–2029 is the country’s official medium-term development plan, guiding 
national  priorities  across  all  sectors.  It  marks  the  first  phase  of  the  “Indonesia  Emas  2045” 
vision, with a strong focus on sustainable, inclusive growth. The plan integrates climate action, 
community  empowerment,  and  biodiversity  protection  into  national  policy,  emphasizing  a  just 
energy transition, ecosystem restoration, and rural development to build long-term resilience and 
prosperity.

2.1.21 Benefit Permanence (CCB, G1.11) 
KPCP   has   been   deliberately   structured   to   secure   long-lasting   climate,   community,   and 
biodiversity benefits that extend well beyond the project’s formal crediting period of 30 years. In 
line with Indonesian government regulations on PBPH concessions, the current license provides 
long-term  management  rights,  with  options  for  renewal,  thereby  ensuring  regulatory  continuity. 
Beyond that, the benefit permanence of the project also comes from resilient systems for land 
stewardship.  The  project’s  ecosystem  restoration  and  REDD+  interventions  are  designed  to 
rehabilitate degraded areas, protect high conservation value forests, and improve carbon storage 
in  a  way  that  creates  self-reinforcing ecological  stability.  As  restored  forests  mature,  natural 
regeneration   processes,   strengthened   fire-prevention   measures,   and   enhanced   habitat 
connectivity  will  continue  to  safeguard  biodiversity  and  climate  benefits  long  after  the  project 
ends. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
43 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Equally  important,  the  project’s  community-focused  strategy  is  centered  on  fostering  local 
ownership  and  embedding  sustainable  practices  within  village  livelihoods.  Through  ongoing 
training,  support  for  agroforestry  and  non-timber  forest  product (NTFPs) enterprises,  and 
participatory  land-use  planning,  the  project  equips  communities  with  practical  skills  and 
economic alternatives that reduce pressure on forests. These measures are complemented by 
the establishment of village-level committees, long-term partnerships with government agencies, 
and  integration  with  district  and  provincial  development  agendas.  By  institutionalizing  local 
governance mechanisms and aligning with broader policy frameworks, the project ensures that 
the social and ecological gains achieved will remain embedded within community systems and 
formal structures, thereby maintaining and enhancing the benefits beyond the project’s lifetime. 
2.1.22 Financial Sustainability (CCB, G1.12) 
The project’s financial sustainability is underpinned by upfront private investment, which covers 
key development costs such as project design, stakeholder engagement, and initial restoration 
activities.  Once  operational,  the  project  will  generate  consistent  revenue  through  the  sale  of 
verified carbon credits over its lifetime. In addition, the senior management team brings extensive 
expertise in financial management, ensuring the effective and sustainable deployment of capital. 
Financial modeling has been conducted to assess feasibility, projected returns, and breakeven 
points,   demonstrating   that   the   project   remains   viable   under   conservative   market   price 
assumptions.  Supporting  documentation  for  these  analyses,  including  investment  records  and 
financial  projections,  can  be  made  available  to  the  project’s  independent  validation  and 
verification body (VVB) upon request. Together, these mechanisms demonstrate that the project 
has a clear and credible pathway to long-term financial sustainability. 
2.2 Without-project Land Use Scenario and Additionality  
2.2.1 Conditions Prior to Project Initiation and Land Use Scenarios without the Project 
(VCS, 3.13; CCB, G2.1) 
Conditions Before Project Implementation 
Land use within the PT RAL’s project zone has experienced significant transformation over time, 
influenced by cultural practices, immigration patterns, economic developments, and changes in 
governance. 
The  area  is  predominantly  inhabited  by  indigenous  communities,  particularly  the  Dayak  Ngaju 
ethnic  group.  Traditionally,  their  livelihoods  have  centered  around  shifting  cultivation,  hunting, 
and  gathering  of  forest  products.  Historically,  shifting  cultivation  involved  slash-and-burn 
techniques for land clearing. However, such practices have been discontinued in compliance with 
current government regulations. 
The APD (Avoided Planned Deforestation) project area is currently forested, though some parts 
of it have been degraded due to past logging operations and agricultural expansion. These areas 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
44 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
are now undergoing natural regeneration. Meanwhile, the ARR (Afforestation, Reforestation, and 
Revegetation)  zone  comprises  largely  of shrubland and  estate  crops,  resulting  from  earlier 
agricultural  activities  and  forest  clearing  through  fire,  primarily  in  preparation  for  palm  oil 
plantation development. 
Prior  to  project  initiation,  the  land  was  officially  designated  for  conversion  into  an  Industrial 
Timber Plantation (HTI). In 2017, a Business Permit for the Utilization of Timber Forest Products 
in Industrial Plantation Forests was issued under Decree Number 2/1/IUPHHK-HTI/PMDN/2017. 
And  in  2019,  Ministry  of  Forestry  has  approved PHHK-HTI  Business  Work  Plan  for  a  10-Year 
Period,  2019-2028 granting  the  permission  for  PT  RAL  to clear  the  land  and  conduct  timber 
cultivation with Eucalyptus  and  Acacia species. A  portion  of  the  land  was  designated  for 
deforestation  to  establish  community  agricultural  projects,  including  palm  oil  plantations. 
Preparatory work commenced on-site; however, operations were paused at the end of 2019 due 
to the COVID-19 pandemic, and no further developments occurred through the end of 2021. 
Transition to a Conser vation-Oriented Approach 
A  significant  shift  occurred  in  2023 when  the  concession  was  acquired  by  PT  Indo  Hutan 
Ekosistem, which introduced a new management plan. The company transitioned the concession 
from an extractive forestry model to a conservation-oriented approach, with a primary focus on 
avoiding planned deforestation. As part of this shift, all deforestation activities were suspended. 
This  new  direction  was  formally  recognized  through  the  approval  of  an  amended  PHHK-HTI 
Business   Work   Plan,   as   outlined   in   Ministry   of   Environment   and   Forestry Decree   No. 
SK.13046/MenLHK-HPL/PUPH/HPL.1/12/2023. 
Without this transition, the most likely land-use trajectory would involve full-scale conversion into 
industrial  timber  plantations,  with  no  initiatives  to  reduce  or  remove  greenhouse  gas  (GHG) 
emissions. Alternative land-use scenarios in the area could include community-based agriculture, 
smallholder palm oil cultivation, or social forestry initiatives. 
Under  the management of  PT  Indo  Hutan  Ekosistem,  the  project  area  is  now  being  actively 
protected and restored through a sustainable, conservation-driven management approach. 
2.2.2 Most-Likely Scenario Justification (CCB, G2.1) 
The  most  likely  without-project  land-use  scenario  for  the  concession  would  have  been  the 
conversion  of  forest  areas  into  an  industrial  timber  plantation  dominated  by  Acacia  and 
Eucalyptus, in line with the concession’s original 2017 Business Permit and the 2019 approved 
10-year Work Plan (see section 2.2.1 and section 3.1.4). Preparatory works for such conversion 
had  already  commenced  prior  to  the  COVID-19  pandemic,  and  industrial  timber  plantations 
remain  common  in  the  surrounding  landscape,  as  evidenced  by  the  activities  of  PT  Industrial 
Forest  Plantation  to  the  northeast of  the  project  area.  Remote  sensing  analyses  and  land-use 
trends  across  Central  Kalimantan  further  demonstrate  that  secondary  swamp  forests  are 
typically  cleared  and  replanted  with  fast-growing  timber  species  when  designated  as  HTI 
concession. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
45 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Alternative  scenarios  such  as  smallholder  agriculture,  palm  oil  development,  or  commercial 
logging  were  assessed  but  determined  less  likely.  As  discussed  in section 3.1.4,  palm  oil 
expansion  is  largely  restricted  to  Conversion  Production  Forest  (HPK)  or  non-forest  land  (APL), 
neither  of  which  applies  to  this  concession.  Commercial  logging  is  also  unlikely,  given  that  the 
standing stock in secondary swamp forest is below the economically viable diameter class, and 
“unexploited forest” scenarios rarely persist in Central Kalimantan due to high pressures for land 
conversion. While community-driven agricultural expansion and artisanal mining are present in 
parts of the concession, these would likely have coexisted alongside, rather than replaced, the 
industrial timber plantation model, reinforcing the conclusion that the primary land-use trajectory 
without the project would have been full-scale Acacia and Eucalyptus development. 
Therefore,  based  on  the  regulatory  approvals  already  in  place,  regional  land-use  patterns,  and 
the absence of viable alternatives, the industrial timber plantation scenario represents the most 
credible  and  well-documented  baseline.  In  the  absence  of  the  project’s  conservation-oriented 
management  shift  in  2023,  large-scale  deforestation  followed  by  monoculture  plantation 
establishment would almost certainly have taken place, leading to the permanent loss of natural 
forest cover and associated carbon stocks, biodiversity, and ecosystem services. 
2.2.3 Community and Biodiversity Additionality (CCB, G2.2) 
In the absence of the project, the positive impacts on community and biodiversity would not occur. 
The  most  likely  baseline  scenario  suggests  that  the  forested  area  within  the  PT  RAL  concession 
would  have  been  fully  converted  into  an  Industrial  Timber  Plantation  (HTI)  under  the  legally 
approved  business  plan  (IUPHHK-HTI  Decree  No.  2/1/IUPHHK-HTI/PMDN/2017  and  PHHK-HTI 
Work  Plan  2019–2028).  This  scenario  would  entail  large-scale  land  clearing  and  monoculture 
planting  of  fast-growing  commercial  species  such  as  Eucalyptus  and  Acacia along with  palm  oil 
plantations  for  community  livelihood,  resulting  in  a  loss  of  natural  regeneration  processes, 
ecosystem  services,  and  biodiversity  habitats.  Likewise,  community  benefits  beyond  direct 
plantation employment would have remained limited and unevenly distributed. 
Community additionality 
Under the without-project scenario, community engagement would have been narrowly confined to 
roles in plantation operations, such as land clearing and plantation maintenance. These jobs, while 
potentially  generating  short-term  income,  typically  offer  minimal  skills  development  and  limited 
long-term benefits. Furthermore, such benefits are often restricted to select villages located near 
operational  zones,  exacerbating  existing  inequalities.  Importantly,  no  provisions  were  outlined  in 
the  original Industrial  Plantation management  plan  for  addressing  critical  issues  faced  by  local 
communities, such as limited higher education attainment or lack of alternative and sustainable 
incomes. 
Conversely,  the  project  introduces  a  conservation-based  management  model  that  proactively 
invests  in  community  development.  Planned  activities  include  improving  access  to  education 
through  scholarships (particularly  for  marginalized  groups  such  as girls and students  from  low-
income households), establishing community support grants, developing health outreach program, 
and enabling sustainable  livelihoods through agroforestry,  employment,  and  skills  development. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
46 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
These interventions would not have been feasible under the original Industrial Plantation model, 
which prioritizes cost-efficiency and timber yield over social investment. Financial and institutional 
barriers, such  as  limited  return  on  social  programs  and  lack  of  technical  capacity  within 
conventional Industrial Plantation operations, would have precluded such community benefits in 
the baseline scenario. 
Biodiversity additionality 
In the without-project scenario, the conversion of forests into monoculture plantations would have 
led  to  the  near-total  loss  of  structurally  diverse  forest  habitat. Despite  Indonesian  regulations
17
 
requiring that concession maps include both “Areal Budi Daya” (productive areas) and “Kawasan 
Lindung”  (protected  areas),  such areas are  generally  not  aligned  with  actual  High  Conservation 
Value  (HCV)  areas  unless  the  concession  voluntarily  seeks Forest  Stewardship  Council (FSC) or 
equivalent certification, which PT RAL did not. Thus, many ecologically important habitats, including 
those undergoing natural regeneration, would likely have been degraded or lost. 
The project scenario halts this planned deforestation and instead implements active protection and 
restoration  across  the  concession.  Through  its REDD,  ARR,  and  WRC components,  the  project 
conserves secondary forests by preventing illegal logging and industrial timber expansion, restores 
degraded shrubland and bare land through planting native species, agroforestry, assisted natural 
regeneration and safeguards intact wetlands from drainage and plantation activity. These efforts 
will eventually expand habitat for flora and fauna, reduce fragmentation, and increase ecological 
integrity across the landscape. 
Biodiversity conservation under the project is further reinforced by the introduction of forest patrols, 
community awareness programs, and local stewardship mechanisms. These interventions address 
not only habitat degradation but also indirect threats such as illegal hunting and fire, both of which 
are observed and documented in the field survey. Without the project, such targeted interventions 
would not have been implemented due to the lack of financial incentive and operational focus on 
biodiversity in the industrial plantation model. 
The identified without project scenario for both communities and biodiversity is described in more 
detail in section 4.1.4 and 5.1.3 respectively. 
Legal and institutional context 
While  Indonesian  forestry  regulations  provide  a  legal  basis  for  forest  management  and  minimal 
environmental  safeguards,  enforcement  remains  weak,  particularly  in  remote  areas.  Approval  of 
business   work   plans under   the Industrial   Plantation scenario does   not   necessitate   the 
implementation  of  biodiversity  or  social  programs  beyond  those  required  for  basic  licensing 
compliance.  There  is  no  binding  legal  obligation  to  conduct  HCV  assessments  or  community 
consultations unless the concession is voluntarily pursuing certification. Therefore, in the without-

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
47 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
project scenario, the implementation of conservation and community development activities would 
have faced substantial institutional and legal gaps. 
Additionally, the financial model underlying the Industrial Plantation system poses a fundamental 
barrier to the implementation of non-revenue generating activities such as ecosystem restoration 
or  social  services.  Forest  protection  and  ARR  activities  are  capital-intensive  and  lack  short-term 
financial returns, making them unviable under the standard plantation approach. The shift toward 
conservation, enabled by the acquisition of the concession by PT Indo Hutan Ekosistem in 2023 
and the subsequent approval of the amended work plans in December 2023, represents a clear 
departure from business-as-usual practices. 
2.2.4 Benefits to be used as Offsets (CCB, G2.2) 
The project does not intend to generate any distinct community or biodiversity benefits to be 
used as offsets. 
2.3 Safeguards and Stakeholder Engagement   
2.3.1 Stakeholder Identification (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB G1.5) 
Stakeholders likely to be impacted by the project were identified using five main methods: 
•
 
Desktop Study
, which enabled the identification of villages and settlements within the project’s 
area of influence, mapped potential areas of environmental and social impact, and provided 
an initial list of potentially affected stakeholders. 
•
 
Initial Socialization
,  conducted  at  the  village  level  to  introduce  the  project  concept  and 
objectives, which helped to build early trust with the community and served as an entry point 
to identify relevant local stakeholders. 
•
 
Focus  Group  Discussions  (FGDs)
,  which  facilitated  the  cross-verification  of  information  and 
captured diverse perspectives, including indigenous knowledge and local historical context. 
•
 
In-depth   Interviews
 with   key individuals,   such as   company   representatives,   workers, 
community leaders, and local authorities, to obtain detailed insights into land use, governance 
structures, and stakeholder interests. 
•
 
Participatory  Mapping  and  Ground  Checks
,  which  involved  local  community  members  in 
delineating customary land use areas and verifying forest access and tenure claims, thereby 
ensuring the spatial accuracy and legitimacy of identified stakeholders. 
This multi-method approach ensured comprehensive and inclusive identification of all stakeholders 
with potential claims, rights, or interests in the project area.

2.3.3 Stakeholder Access to Project Documents (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G3.1) 
To  ensure  stakeholder  access  to  project  documents,  hard  copies  of  the  Project  Description  (PD) 
and other relevant documents (in Bahasa Indonesia) will be made available at village offices within 
and  around  the  project  area.  Executive  summary  of  the  PD  will be  shared  during  stakeholder 
meetings or with community leaders to disseminate the information among the local communities. 
All official documents (in English) will also be accessible through the Verra Registry and the project 
website (to be developed). 
2.3.4 Dissemination of Summary Project Documents (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G3.1) 
The summary of the project documentation, including key elements required under G1.1–G1.9, 
have  been  proactively  disseminated  to  local  communities  through  meetings  conducted  during 
the FPIC process and follow-up stakeholder engagements. Specifically, translated summaries in 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
54 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Bahasa Indonesia are made available at village offices, shared with community leaders during 
village meetings, participatory forums, and workshops.  
In the future, monitoring results will be summarized and shared after each verification cycle using 
the same channels, with facilitated meetings or focus group discussions organized to explain key 
findings and gather feedback. All official summary documents will also be uploaded to the Verra 
Registry and the project website to ensure transparency and broader accessibility. 
2.3.5 Informational Meetings with Stakeholders (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G3.1) 
KPCP has carried out various informational meetings with communities and local stakeholders 
as part of its stakeholder engagement and FPIC process. Between July 2023 and March 2025, 
the  Initial  Socialization  stage  involved  engagements with  regency  and  sub-district  government 
offices, village heads, customary leaders, women’s group representatives, and local communities 
across eight villages. These  meetings helped build  trust,  introduce the project’s concept  and 
objectives, present planned REDD and ARR activities, and provide opportunities for communities 
to share their perspectives. They also served to clarify misunderstandings and address circulating 
rumors, such as confusion with palm oil operations. 
Following these initial engagements, the project team obtained permission from village heads to 
carry  out a  series  of  formal  community  consultations between  12  and  25  June  2025.  These 
included a social baseline survey  with  villagers,  in-depth  interviews  with  key individuals, and 
focus group  discussions  (FGDs)  with  various  community  representatives. The  objective was  to 
gain a deeper understanding of community contexts, gather input to inform project design, and 
secure local agreement for project implementation. In villages where consensus was achieved, 
FPIC meetings were subsequently held to formalize agreements through the signing of consent 
documents. Details about those activities are provided in 
Table 9
 below. 
All  informational  meetings  were  publicized  in  advance  through  formal  letters  to  village  heads, 
announcements   at   community   events and postings   on   village noticeboards. To ensure 
transparency  and  provide a  reference  for  follow-up  engagement,  they were also documented 
through attendance records, notes, and photographs.

2.3.6 Risks from the Project and No Net Harm (VCS, 3.18, 3.19) 
In  line  with  the  precautionary  principle  and  the  VCS  no  net  harm  requirement,  the  project  has 
assessed   potential   natural   and   human-induced   risks   across   environmental   and   social 
dimensions. 
Table 10
 below outlines  the  identified  risks,  their  potential  impacts,  and  the 
corresponding mitigation or preventive measures to ensure that project activities do not cause 
net harm to stakeholders, biodiversity, or ecosystem services.

18

Indonesian labor law.

benefit    positively    from    the

2.3.7 Community Costs, Risks, and Benefits (CCB, G3.2) 
Since July 2023, KPCP has engaged stakeholders through a combination of formal consultations 
and informal village meetings, as described in section 2.3.5. During these consultations, we have 
actively  shared  project  activity  information  and  gathered  community  inputs  regarding  the 
potential benefits, risks, and costs associated with the project. Further details will be outlined in 
section 4.2.1.  
2.3.8 Information to Stakeholders on Validation and Verification Process (VCS, 3.18.6, 
3.19; CCB, G3.3) 
KPCP  has  introduced  the  VCS  and  CCB  validation  and  verification  processes  to  communities 
through  village  meetings  and  informal  discussions. Easy-to-understand  summaries  of  the 
concepts and requirements have been shared to help stakeholders grasp the purpose and steps 
involved. Besides, printed brochures summarizing the process will be made available at village 
offices for public access. 
Going forward, the project team will continue to use village meetings and notes at village board 
to  communicate future validation  and  verification  activities,  including  the  timing  of  VVB  field 
visits.  Communities  will  be  informed  in  advance  if  their  involvement  is  required  during  these 
processes. 
2.3.9 Site Visit Information and Opportunities to Communicate with Auditor (VCS, 
3.18.6; CCB, G3.3) 
KPCP will notify communities and other stakeholders of the auditor’s site visit at least 2 weeks 
in advance through village government, and announcements at village office. Prior to the visit, a 
formal letter will be sent to the village heads requesting permission to conduct the visit, including 
relevant  information  and  the  contact  details  of  all  personnel  involved.  Additionally, the  project 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
68 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
team will explain the audit process and prepare relevant stakeholders to ensure they understand 
their  role  and  can  participate  meaningfully. Finally, KPCP  will  facilitate  direct  and  independent 
meetings between the auditor and community members, without project staff present, to allow 
open  and  honest  dialogue.  A  tailored  schedule  will  be  proposed  to  ensure  all  relevant 
stakeholders have sufficient opportunity to engage with the auditor. 
2.3.10 Stakeholder Consultations (VCS, 3.18; CCB, G3.4) 
Date of stakeholder 
consultation 
July 2023 – March 2025 
Stakeholder 
engagement process  
During this period, KPCP conducted a series of formal and informal 
engagements with  local  stakeholders  across the  8 villages  and 
relevant sub-district institutions, as described in section 2.3.5. The 
engagement  process  was  carried  out  through  regular  field  visits, 
informal   discussions,   and   formal   village   meetings   following 
traditional customs and local communication practices. The project 
prioritized   transparency   in   explaining   its   operational   activities, 
including nursery establishment, forest protection, land 
preparation,  and  pilot  planting  of  native  and  fruit-bearing  species. 
Additionally,  the  project  also clarified the  legal  status  under  the 
Forest Utilization Business License (PBPH) and outlined community 
participation opportunities. In each engagement, the project’s field 
and  social  teams  adopted  a  participatory  approach,  encouraging 
open  dialogue  and  documenting  every  interaction  in  the  social 
activity log. Special attention was given to maintaining inclusivity by 
engaging both male and female community members, ensuring the 
use of local language (Dayak Ngaju dialect) during discussions, and 
accommodating  meetings  at  times  and  locations  convenient  to 
villagers. 
Apart  from  local  communities,  the  project also engaged relevant 
regencies  and sub-district  institutions (e.g. Pulang  Pisau  Regency, 
Mentangai  Sub-District, Kahayan  Tengah  Sub-District, and Koramil 
Kahayan   Tengah)   to   introduce   project   activities,   obtain   their 
support, and ensure their feedback is well accounted for. 
All   outcomes of   the   stakeholder   consultation were   formally 
documented through meeting minutes and attendance lists, which 
were retained as part of the project’s consultation records. 
Consultation outcome 
Across all engagement activities, stakeholders generally expressed 
broad  support  for  the  project’s  reforestation  and  forest  protection 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
69 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
objectives, recognizing its potential to restore degraded lands and 
create new livelihood opportunities. 
A  key  outcome  of  the  consultations  was  increased  community 
understanding  and  acceptance  of  the  project’s  legal  status  and 
purpose. Initial   concerns   regarding   land   boundaries,   company 
legitimacy,  and  rumors  of  oil-palm  operations  were  effectively 
resolved   through   direct   dialogue,   transparency,   and   repeated 
village-level  socialization.  Stakeholders  also  gained  confidence  in 
the  project’s  transparency  after  observing  field  evidence,  such  as 
nursery establishment, pilot planting plots, and the involvement of 
local labor. 
Stakeholder input 
Feedback and input from stakeholders throughout this period were 
diverse  but  largely  constructive,  reflecting  community  interest  in 
participating  in  project  activities  while  seeking  greater  clarity  on 
certain aspects of implementation.  
Firstly, the  project considered community feedback  by  integrating 
fruit-bearing  species  (e.g. durian,  jengkol,  petai,  jackfruit,  and 
pineapple)  into  the  planting  design  to  enhance  economic  co-
benefits   for   surrounding   villages. Additionally,   the   project will 
coordinate  with  relevant  partners  to  establish  market  linkages  for 
community-based  forest  and  fruit  products  during  later  project 
stages.  
Secondly, many villagers requested early, broad-based socialization 
to  all  community  members,  emphasizing  the  need  for  transparent 
communication  on  project  boundaries,  partnership  mechanisms, 
and benefit-sharing arrangements. KPCP responded by coordinating 
with The   Forest   Area   and   Environmental   Management   Center 
(BPKHTL) to  schedule  official  boundary  demarcation  only  after 
comprehensive socialization sessions and by preparing 
informational materials in simple, accessible language. 
 
 
Date of stakeholder 
consultation 
12 June 2025 – 22 June 2025 
Stakeholder 
engagement process  
During   this   period,   the stakeholder   engagement process was 
formally conducted  in  each  of  the 8 villages  located  within  the 
project  zone  (see section 2.3.5 for  the full list  of  meetings and 
activities). One week  prior  to the  engagement,  formal  letters  were 
sent to the village heads to request permission to conduct a series 
of formal community consultations. These letters included details of 
the engagement objectives,  a  list  of  personnel  involved,  and  the 
proposed schedule. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
70 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Engagement  activities  began  with  a  social  baseline  survey,  which 
included  a  questionnaire  survey  and  in-depth  interviews  with  key 
individuals possessing extensive knowledge of the communities and 
local context. Subsequently, this was followed by a series of Focus 
Group Discussions (FGDs) in each village. In total, 271 participants 
took  part,  representing  diverse  groups  such  as  traditional  leaders, 
village   governments,   village   representative   bodies,   customary 
institutions, farmer representatives, youth, and women. 
Prior  to  each  FGD,  public  announcements  were  made  by  village 
leaders 3-5 days in advance. Discussions were conducted in village 
halls using both Bahasa Indonesia and the local Dayak Ngaju dialect 
to ensure accessibility for all participants. 
During the FGDs, the project team: 
• Explained the objectives and planned activities of PT Ramang 
Agro Lestari’s ecosystem restoration program in each village. 
• Collected  information  on  village  resources  and  potential  to 
ensure  that  proposed  activities  would  deliver  positive  social 
and environmental outcomes. 
• Conducted  stakeholder  mapping  to  identify  individuals  and 
groups  who  could  be  positively  or  negatively  impacted  by  the 
project. 
• Carried  out  participatory  mapping  to  document  land  use 
patterns, including residential areas, plantations, gold mining 
sites,  logging  areas,  fishing  grounds,  and  other  significant 
areas. 
• Where  the  village  head  and  the  community  expressed  full 
support  for  the  project,  a  Free,  Prior,  and  Informed  Consent 
(FPIC) agreement was signed, confirming that the community 
fully understood and endorsed the planned activities. 
All  outcomes  from  the  FGDs  and  FPIC  process  were  formally 
documented through meeting minutes and attendance lists, which 
were retained as part of the project’s consultation records. 
Consultation outcome 
The  consultations  demonstrated  a  varied  response  across  the  8 
villages. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
71 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
In four villages (Humbang Raya, Parahangan, Manen Paduran, and 
Hurung),  community  representatives  expressed  broad  consent  for 
the   proposed   ecosystem   restoration   activities,   acknowledging 
potential   benefits   such   as   livelihood   opportunities, climate 
mitigation,  and  biodiversity protection. In  these  villages,  the  FPIC 
meeting process reached consensus, and formal FPIC agreements 
were signed. 
In  three  villages (Bereng  Rambang, Manen  Kaleka,  and  Hanua), 
however,  consensus  was  not  reached  during  the  FPIC  meetings. 
Concerns raised included potential restrictions on traditional forest 
use and uncertainty over future access to land. In these instances, 
community  representatives  indicated  the  need  for  more  time  to 
conduct wider consultations with residents and to address or clarify 
circulating information within the village. 
In one remaining village (Lawang Uru), no FPIC meeting process was 
conducted  during  the  consultation  period.  The  absence  of  this 
meeting   was   due   to   the   community’s   decision   to   postpone 
engagement until preliminary concerns were addressed, and further 
information was provided. 
As  follow-up,  KPCP  will  continue  engagement  with  all  villages, 
including  additional  socialization  sessions  to  share  more  detailed 
project  information,  address  outstanding  concerns,  and  collect 
further community feedback. This input will be used to refine and, 
where necessary, revise the project plan to ensure it reflects local 
priorities and perspectives. 
Stakeholder input 
Following the FPIC consultations, communities provided a range of 
feedback.  Supportive  villages emphasized interest  in  participating 
in   restoration   activities,   co-management   of   forest   areas,   and 
livelihood  programs  such  as demonstration  gardens. In  villages 
where  consensus  was  not  reached,  participants  raised  concerns 
about potential loss of access to customary lands and restrictions 
on resource use. 
Across   both   supportive   and   undecided   villages,   stakeholders 
stressed   the   importance   of equitable   benefit   sharing and 
integration of local knowledge into project design. These inputs will 
guide  adjustments  to  land-use  planning,  community  engagement, 
and livelihood support activities in KPCP.
