2.3.11 Continued Consultation and Adaptive Management (VCS, 3.18; CCB, G3.4) 
To  ensure  sustained  communication  and  inclusive  stakeholder  par ticipation  throughout  the 
project lifecycle, KPCP has established a structured plan for ongoing consultation and adaptive 
management.  This  plan  is  designed  to  foster  transparency,  build  trust, and  ensure  the  project 
remains responsive to the evolving needs and concerns of communities and stakeholders. 
Continued Consultation 
KPCP will hold regular village-level meetings, scheduled annually or as needed, to share project 
updates,  discuss  implementation  progress,  and  solicit  community  input.  These  meetings  will 
provide  a  consistent  platform  for  open  dialogue  and  enable  the  project  team  to  address  any 
emerging concerns promptly. 
To strengthen continuous engagement, KPCP has appointed community facilitators who ser ve as 
liaisons between the project team and local stakeholders. These facilitators, familiar with local 
languages and customs, will maintain regular contact with community members and help ensure 
that feedback is communicated effectively to the project management. 
Additionally,  KPCP  will  maintain  active online communication  channels that  include  village 
leaders,  patrol  team  members,  women’s  group  representatives,  and  other  key  stakeholders. 
These channels enable real-time sharing of information and allow stakeholders to voice concerns 
or suggestions at any time. 
An  annual  stakeholder  workshop  will  be  organized  to  bring  together  a  broader  group  of 
community members and relevant stakeholders. This forum will serve as an opportunity to review 
project   performance,   identify   challenges,   and   collaboratively   plan   improvements   for   the 
upcoming year. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
75 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Adaptive Management 
KPCP  is  committed  to  an  adaptive  management  approach  that  uses  regular  monitoring  and 
evaluation  to  inform  decision-making  and  improve  project  implementation  over  time.  Project 
plans,  methods,  and  objectives  will  be  periodically  reviewed  to  incorporate  lessons  learned, 
stakeholder input, and evolving field conditions. 
Community  feedback,  alongside  monitoring  results,  will  be  reviewed  as  part  of  the  annual 
planning  cycle  to  inform  strategic  adjustments  to  project  activities.  For  instance,  patrol  routes 
may be revised based on local observations, or education and livelihood support may be tailored 
to address newly identified needs. 
Furthermore, local stakeholders will be directly involved in planning future project phases—such 
as  identifying  new  areas  for reforestation or  proposing  community  development  initiatives—
ensuring  that  the  project  remains  aligned  with  community  priorities  and  social  dynamics  over 
time. 
2.3.12 Stakeholder Consultation Channels (CCB, G3.5) 
KPCP   has   employed   multiple   consultation   channels   to   ensure   inclusive   and   meaningful 
engagement  with  communities  and  other  stakeholders.  Consultations  have  been  carried  out 
directly through initial socialization, in-person village meetings, focus group discussions (FGDs), 
and in-depth interviews with individuals or key figures who possess extensive knowledge about 
the desired topic. These were conducted during the FPIC process and follow-up consultations on 
project design, potential impacts, and planned activities. Participatory approaches such as field 
surveys  and  mapping  were  also  used  to  strengthen  stakeholder  input. Where  appropriate,  the 
project  has  consulted  legitimate  community  representatives,  such  as  village  heads  or  local 
government officials. In addition, online communication channels have been established to share 
project updates, gather input, and maintain continuous communication. 
To   suppor t   transparency,   all   consultation   sessions   were   documented   through   attendance 
records, meeting notes, and photos, and key updates were translated into Bahasa Indonesia. The 
insights gained through these channels have been used to refine project activities and ensure 
alignment with community needs. Further details on our stakeholder consultation processes can 
be found in section 2.3.10. 
2.3.13 Stakeholder Participation in Decision-Making and Implementation (VCS, 3.18, 
3.19; CCB, G3.6) 
KPCP has established inclusive and culturally sensitive processes to enable effective community 
participation  in  both  decision-making  and  project  implementation.  Village-level  meetings  are 
coordinated through local government and customary leaders, with invitations sent in advance 
to ensure full and fair representation. These meetings are open to all community members and 
structured  to  actively  encourage  participation  from  women,  youth,  and  other  marginalized 
groups.  Where  appropriate,  separate  women-led  sessions  are  facilitated  to  ensure  their 
perspectives are clearly heard and considered. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
76 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
All consultations are conducted in the local language, and facilitators are instructed to adhere to 
local customs and   cultural protocols,   including   involving   traditional   leaders   in   opening 
discussions. This approach helps build trust and ensures that the messages are understood and 
respected by all participants.  
2.3.14 Anti-Discrimination Assurance (VCS 3.19; CCB, G3.7) 
KPCP  upholds  a  zero-tolerance  policy  for  all  forms  of  discrimination  and  sexual  harassment  in 
the design and implementation of its activities. All project staff, partners, and affiliated personnel 
are required to act in accordance with this policy, which prohibits discrimination based on gender, 
race,  ethnicity,  religion,  sexual  orientation,  social  class,  or  any  other  individual  attributes.  This 
commitment  extends  to  all  community  engagements  and  implementation  activities  under  the 
project. 
The  project  strictly  complies  with  relevant  Indonesian  laws,  including  the  1945  Constitution 
(Article  27),  Law  No.  40/2008  on  the  elimination  of  racial  and  ethnic  discrimination,  Law  No. 
1/2023  (Criminal  Code),  and  Law  No.  1/1970  on  Occupational  Safety.  KPCP’s  internal  HR 
policies  incorporate  these  legal  standards  and  mandate  equal  opportunity  practices  and  safe, 
respectful working environments for all. 
To  safeguard  against  misconduct,  the  project  ensures  that  all  employees  and  stakeholders  are 
informed  of  their  rights  and  responsibilities  through  training  and  orientation.  Furthermore,  a 
formal  grievance  redress  mechanism  (see section 2.3.15)  has  been  established  to  allow  staff 
and  community  members  to  confidentially  report  any  concerns  related  to  discrimination  or 
harassment.  All  grievances  will  be  handled  promptly  and  fairly  to  ensure  accountability  and 
protection for all parties involved. 
2.3.15 Feedback and Grievance Redress Procedure (VCS, 3.18.4; CCB, G3.8) 
Development process
 
The  development  of  the  project’s  grievance  redress  mechanism 
(GRM) has been a collaborative effort involving the community and 
potentially affected stakeholders. The project’s grievance procedure 
includes the following key steps: registering the grievance, working 
with  the  complainant  to  identify  potential  solutions,  implementing 
appropriate actions to address the issue, executing the resolutions, 
and   documenting   the   process   for   feedback   and   learning.   All 
grievances and disputes are expected to be resolved transparently 
and responsibly, typically within 5 working days.
 
Grievance redress 
procedure
 
Key roles of the feedback and grievance redress procedure of KPCP 
involve the Camp Manager, who is the main community liaison and 
addresses concerns; the Field Assistant, who handles logistics; and 
the  Social  Manager,  who  develops  strategy  for  complex  situations 
and makes higher-level decisions. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
77 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Local  villagers  are  asked  to raise their  concerns  either  through  a 
grievance  form  or direct  conversation  with  KPCP’s  field  team;  All 
complaints  are  directed  first  to  the Camp Manager,  and  upon 
receipt,  the  grievance  is  registered  in  the  project's  grievance 
database.   The   grievance   resolution   process   follows   a   staged 
approach.  
• Stage   1   begins   with   the Camp Manager   reviewing   and 
analyzing  the  complaint  and  sets  up  a  meeting  with  the 
concerned    villagers.    If    the    issue    can    be    addressed 
immediately,  meeting  participants  sign  the  minutes,  and  the 
grievance is marked 'Resolved' in the database. If the issue is 
complicated   and   requires   a   higher-level   decision,   it   is 
escalated  to  the  headquarter  of  social  management  team. 
Meeting minutes are recorded as part of documentation. 
• In Stage 2, the Social Manager from headquarters initiates the 
grievance resolution process by arranging a meeting with the 
Camp Manager  to  gain  a  clear  understanding  of  the  issues. 
Following  the  assessment,  the  Social  Manager  collaborates 
with the project’s management team to analyze the situation 
and jointly develop a resolution plan. 
• In  Stage  3,  the Camp Manager,  supported  by  relevant  staff, 
leads the implementation of the resolution plan. Actions may 
involve  operational  adjustments  or  direct  engagement  with 
affected  parties.  If  the  proposed  plan  does  not  resolve  the 
issue,  the  management  team  reviews  and revises  it,  then  re-
executes the updated approach. 
• If the grievance remains unresolved after these internal steps 
and the objection period ends without mutual agreement, the 
case moves to Formal Mediation and Arbitration. At this stage, 
the matter is referred to an impartial third party for resolution, 
following the applicable Indonesian laws and regulations. Key 
documentation  throughout  the  process  includes  grievance 
submissions,    meeting    records,    resolution    plans,    and 
photographic evidence.  
• Feedback  and  Learning:  After  implementation,  the  outcome, 
feedback,   and   lessons   learned   are   documented   in   the 
grievance database. This record helps inform future grievance 
handling   and   project   decisions   and   as   well   supports 
continuous  improvement  of  the  overall  grievance  redress 
mechanism. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
78 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
 
2.3.16 Accessibility of the Feedback and Grievance Redress Procedure (VCS, 3.19; 
CCB, G3.8) 
The  project  ensures  that  the  feedback  and  grievance  redress  procedure  described  in  Section 
2.3.15 is  well  publicized,  accessible,  and  transparent  to  all  stakeholders.  The  procedure  is 
introduced   to   communities   through   village   meetings,   information   sessions,   and   ongoing 
socialization activities carried out by the project team. Printed grievance forms are available at 
the project office and village centers, while complaints may also be submitted verbally, through 
the village government, or directly to the Camp Manager. Community liaison staff regularly visit 
villages to remind local residents about the procedure and to ensure access for individuals who 
may not be literate or digitally connected. 
All grievances and project responses are documented in the project’s grievance database, which 
includes records of submission, meeting minutes, resolution plans, and outcomes. Summaries 
of  grievances  and  their  resolutions  are  periodically  shared  with  village  leaders  and  can  be 
reviewed  by  stakeholders  upon  request.  To  support  transparency,  the  project  makes  this 
documentation available for validation and verification processes, and anonymized summaries 
may be included in public reporting to demonstrate accountability. 
2.3.17 Worker Training (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G3.9) 
KPCP places strong emphasis on equipping its workforce and community partners with the skills 
needed to actively contribute to project outcomes while also enhancing their long-term livelihood 
prospects.  Before  beginning  any  duties,  all  new  personnel will participate  in  an  onboarding 
session  introducing  the project’s  objectives,  Standard  Operating  Procedures  (SOPs),  expected 
work  standards,  and  field  safety  requirements.  Orientation  sessions  also  cover  rights  and 
responsibilities,  ethical  conduct,  and  the  project’s  commitment  to  a  safe  and  respectful 
workplace.

Additionally, to safeguard knowledge against turnover, the project applies a peer-to-peer learning

2.3.18 Community Employment Opportunities (VCS, 3.19.13; CCB, G3.10) 
KPCP  is  committed  to  providing  equal  employment  opportunities  for  all  qualified  community 
members,  including  positions  at  the  management  level.  Recruitment  is  guided  by  a  non-
discrimination policy ensuring equal pay for equal work regardless of gender, ethnicity, religion, 
or  social  background.  Preference  is  given  to  applicants  from  villages  within  or  adjacent  to  the 
project  area where qualifications  are  equivalent,  to  maximize  local  benefits  and  community 
ownership. 
The project actively promotes gender equity by encouraging women to apply for a range of roles, 
including field positions traditionally held by men. Training is offered to women, youth, and other 
marginalized groups to equip them with the skills needed to qualify for available positions. 
Employment   opportunities   are   communicated   through   village   notice   boards,   community 
meetings,  and conversation with  local  leaders.  Information  sessions  are  held  in  stakeholder 
villages  to  explain  job  roles,  requirements,  and  application  procedures.  Selection  is  based  on 
relevant skills, experience, and willingness to undertake training, with interviews conducted in a 
transparent manner. 
By  combining  fair  hiring  practices,  targeted  outreach,  and  capacity  building,  KPCP will ensure 
that  all  community  members  have  a  fair  chance  to  contribute  to  and  benefit  from  project 
implementation. 
2.3.19 Occupational Safety Assessment (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G3.12) 
KPCP  is  committed  to  maintaining  a  safe  and  healthy  working  environment  for  all  staff  and 
community members involved in project activities, in compliance with Indonesian labor laws and 
occupational safety regulations.  
Work in the project’s forests presents several potential risks, including: 
• Heat stress from strenuous activity in hot, humid conditions 
• Exposure to smoke and burns during fire suppression 
• Injuries from tools or heavy equipment 
• Encounters with dangerous wildlife 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
81 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
• Mosquito-borne diseases 
• Navigation hazards during patrols 
• Possible confrontations with individuals engaged in illegal activities. 
To  address  these  risks,  KPCP  has  developed  a  comprehensive Occupational Health and Safety 
(OHS) Plan (Appendix 2: Occupational Health and Safety PLAN) that is introduced to all personnel 
during orientation and reinforced through regular training. Before any field activity, team leaders 
conduct  safety  briefings  to  review  specific  hazards  and  prevention  measures.  Field  teams  are 
equipped  with  appropriate personal  protective  equipment  (PPE),  navigation  tools,  and  first  aid 
kits, and operate under a buddy system to enhance safety. 
Safety   procedures   are   communicated   in   Bahasa   Indonesia   and,   where   necessary,   local 
languages to ensure understanding. All incidents are recorded through a formal reporting system, 
with corrective actions implemented to prevent recurrence. This proactive approach ensures that 
occupational safety is embedded in all stages of project planning and implementation. 
2.4 Management Capacity  
2.4.1 Project Governance Structures (CCB, G4.1) 
KPCP is a collaborative initiative between PT. Indo Hutan Ekosistem (PT IHE) and Canopy Carbon. 
PT  IHE is the project proponent  and  is  responsible  for  overseeing  project  implementation  and 
coordination. Canopy Carbon acts as a technical development partner, contributing specialized 
expertise in nature-based solutions, carbon project standards, and project MRV. 
PT  IHE  is in  charge  of comprehensive  project  management  and  on-the-ground  operations. The 
team comprises professionals with deep experience in managing forestry and land-use projects 
in Indonesia. Key responsibilities include: 
• 
Development of Operational Protocols
: Establishing Standard Operating Procedures (SOPs) 
to guide all field-based activities. 
• 
Implementation   of   Field   Activities
:   Overseeing   stakeholder   engagement,   nursery 
operations, and reforestation initiatives. 
• 
Regulatory  Compliance
:  Ensuring  all  project  activities  comply  with  applicable  local  and 
national regulations. 
• 
Monitoring and Data Collection
: Executing the project’s monitoring plan and maintaining 
records of field data. 
• 
Financial  Planning  and  Management
:  Preparing  the  project’s  financial  budget  and 
managing operational expenditures.

2.4.3 Management Team Experience (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G4.2) 
Siswantoro - Director 
Siswantoro  is  the  director  of PT  IHE and  has  extensive  experience  in  forestry  operations  and 
project  management,  with  a  career  spanning  30  years  in  Kalimantan.  He  has  overseen 
operational planning, implementation, monitoring for large scale forestry concessions, ensuring 
compliance with regulatory requirements and sustainable management principles. His expertise 
includes coordinating multi-stakeholder activities, from technical teams in the field to corporate 
and government liaisons. With his proven leadership in both technical forestry and operational 
oversight,  he  brings  strong  capacity  to  manage  the  daily  implementation  of  land-based carbon 
projects. 
Sri Utoro - General Manager 
Sri  is  the  General  Manager  for  Legal  team  in PT  IHE.  He  has  nearly  30  years  of  experience  in 
forestry, plantation management, and corporate governance. His background combines technical 
forestry  expertise  with  legal  and  compliance  oversight,  including  preparing  SOPs,  structuring 
organizations, and ensuring operations follow Indonesian forestry regulations. His experience in 
managing   social   responsibility   programs   and   maintaining   zero-fire   policies   adds   strong 
governance and risk-management value to the project. 
Udi Rudiana - Camp Manager 
Udi is a forestry professional with more than 10 years of hands-on experience in forest planning, 
harvesting  operations,  and  camp  management.  He  has  served  in  multiple  leadership  roles, 
including  Head  of  Planning,  Camp  Manager,  and  Production  Manager  across  different  forestry 
concessions  and  industrial  operations  in  Kalimantan.  His  skills  include  forest  licensing  and 
compliance (RKU, RKT, IHMB), budgeting, and operational planning, as well as technical mapping 
using   GPS,   drones,   AutoCAD,   and   GIS   systems.   With   his   strong   background   in   field 
implementation and logistics management, he ensures efficient, compliant, and safe operations 
at the project’s base camps. 
Meri Yulizarni - Social Relations Associate 
Meri  has  strong  experience  in  community  engagement  and  social  facilitation,  particularly  in 
building  relationships  with  local  stakeholders  around  forestry  and  land-based  projects.  She 
specializes  in  conducting  consultations,  supporting  Free,  Prior  and  Informed  Consent  (FPIC) 
processes,  and  developing  community  partnership  programs  that  align  with  both  project 
objectives  and  local  needs.  Her  skills  include  conflict  resolution,  participatory  rural  appraisal, 
and  managing  livelihood  support  activities  that  enhance  project  co-benefits.  Through  her  role,

to manage field teams and conduct rigorous data collection provides valuable technical support 
for the project’s forest monitoring and management activities. 
2.4.4 Project Management Partnerships and Team Development (VCS, 3.19; CCB, 
G4.2) 
The  project  recognizes  the  importance  of  supplementing  its  expertise  to  ensure  long-term 
success. To address any potential gaps in knowledge or experience, the project has developed a 
multi-pronged strategy focused on partnerships, recruitment, and capacity building. 
Collaboration with local governments 
The project will establish formal collaborations with local government agencies to leverage their 
administrative experience, technical resources, and established networks. These collaborations 
will help align the project with regional development priorities, ensure regulatory compliance, and 
provide  access  to  valuable  local  knowledge  and  data.  Importantly,  the  project’s  conservation 
activities will be designed to align with existing government-led environmental and climate action 
strategies, ensuring coherence with national and regional frameworks. 
Partnership with institutions and organizations 
To  fur ther  strengthen  technical  capacity,  the  project  will  remain  open  to  par tnerships  with 
academic institutions, research centers, and specialized NGOs. These partnerships will enable 
access  to  cutting-edge  research,  technical  expertise  in  areas  and  innovative  solutions  for  land 
management and biodiversity conservation. 
Recruitment and capacity building 
The project places strong emphasis on local recruitment to support community livelihoods and 
foster a sense of ownership. Community members will be hired and trained to take on operational 
roles, ensuring that essential knowledge and skills remain within the local area. To complement 
this  approach,  the  project  will  engage  short-term  specialists—such  as  experts  in  forestry 
management,  biodiversity  monitoring and  hydrology  management — who  will  provide  targeted 
training  and  capacity-building  programs annually.  This  strategy  not  only  meets  immediate 
technical requirements but also empowers local communities to sustain project outcomes over 
the long term. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
86 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
2.4.5 Financial Health of Implementing Organization(s) (CCB, G4.3) 
The  project  proponent,  PT IHE,  is  a  financially  reliable  and  well-established  company  with 
sufficient resources committed to supporting the project throughout its lifetime. Their financial 
statements are audited every year in accordance with Indonesian regulations, and these audited 
statements will be made available to the project’s independent validation and verification body 
(VVB) upon request. 
2.4.6 Avoidance of Corruption and Other Unethical Behavior (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G4.3) 
The project proponent and all entities involved in project design and implementation affirm that 
they  are  not,  and  will  not  be,  engaged  in  any  form  of  corruption  or  unethical behavior.  This 
includes,  but  is  not  limited  to,  bribery,  embezzlement,  fraud,  favoritism,  cronyism,  nepotism, 
extortion,  and  collusion.  All  project  activities  are  conducted  in  compliance  with  applicable 
national  anti-corruption  legislation,  the  U.S.  Foreign  Corrupt  Practices  Act  (FCPA),  and  relevant 
international standards such as the United Nations Convention Against Corruption. 
To provide assurance of compliance, the project has established measures that promote integrity, 
transparency, and accountability across its operations. A grievance and redress mechanism is in 
place  to  allow  staff,  community  members,  and  other  stakeholders to  raise  concerns  regarding 
potential unethical behavior. All submissions are treated confidentially and addressed in a fair 
and timely manner, with appropriate corrective actions taken where necessary. 
Through these safeguards, the project ensures that risks of corruption and unethical practices 
are minimized while maintaining the trust of stakeholders and the integrity of project outcomes. 
2.4.7 Commercially Sensitive Information (VCS, 3.5.2 – 3.5.4; CCB Rules, 3.5.13 – 3.5.14)  
The project proponent is committed to transparency and has included all information necessary 
for  stakeholders,  auditors,  and  the  public  to  evaluate  compliance  with  the  CCB  Standards. 
Nevertheless, certain information is considered commercially sensitive and therefore is not made 
publicly available, in order to protect intellectual property, safeguard competitive interests, and 
maintain confidentiality agreements with stakeholders. Such information will, however, be made 
available  in  full  to  the  accredited validator/verifier  and  relevant  regulatory  authorities  upon 
request, under conditions of confidentiality.

•

:  This  includes  spatial  data  defining

2.5.3 Human Rights (VCS, 3.19)  
The  KPCP  demonstrates  a  comprehensive  approach  to  recognizing,  respecting,  and  promoting 
the protection of the rights of Indigenous Peoples (IPs), Local Communities (LCs), and customary 
rights  holders,  aligning  with  international  human  rights  law,  including  the  United  Nations 
Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples (UNDRIP) and ILO Convention 169 on Indigenous 
and  Tribal  Peoples.  This  commitment  is  evident  through  its  explicit  adherence  to  ethical 
frameworks, its robust Free, Prior, and Informed Consent (FPIC) process, inclusive participatory 
methodologies, and proactive strategies for social impact management. In particular, the project: 
Upholds Free, Prior and Informed Consent (FPIC): 
The FPIC process is a fundamental safeguard 
to uphold the rights of Indigenous Peoples and local communities affected by project activities. 
It guarantees their right to freely give or withhold consent regarding initiatives that may impact 
their  lands,  territories,  or  natural  resources.  The  process  engages  all  communities  within  and 
around  the  project  area,  along  with  village  administrations, customary  institutions,  and  local 
organizations.  Their  participation  is  vital  for  protecting  the  area,  fostering  transparency,  and 
ensuring collaboration and support for project implementation. 
Recognizes  and  Respect  for  Customary  Land  Rights  and  Cultural  Identity: 
The project explicitly 
recognizes, respects, and seeks to protect the cultural heritage of Indigenous Peoples (IPs) and 
Local  Communities  (LCs)  within  its  operational  area.  This  includes  acknowledging  Indigenous 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
93 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
identity, cultural ties to nature, and the significance of traditional practices and sacred sites. All 
activities  are  guided  by  principles  of  cultural  sensitivity,  ensuring  alignment  with  local  norms, 
values, and customs. The project also anticipates potential risks—such as violations of taboo or 
restricted areas and concerns over limitations on traditional activities—and commits to avoiding 
such impacts while upholding cultural integrity.  
Practices  Inclusivity  in  Participation  and  Stakeholder  Engagement: 
Local  enumerators  were 
engaged during social baseline study and social impact assessment to ensure culturally sensitive 
data collection and build stronger community trust. The assessment involved participation from 
traditional leaders, village authorities, farmers, youth, and women. Particular attention was given 
to  amplifying  the  voices  of  women, as well as supporting vulnerable groups—including widows, 
the  elderly,  persons  with  disabilities,  and  families  living  below  the  poverty  line—so  that  project 
benefits are more inclusive and equitable. 
Builds Capacity and Sharing Benefits Equitably: 
The project is committed to delivering inclusive 
and  tangible  benefits  by  maximizing  community  gains  and  minimizing  losses,  ensuring  all 
activities are carried out ethically, inclusively, and sustainably. It prioritizes positive impacts for 
women, vulnerable groups such as widows, the elderly, people with disabilities, and low-income 
families. Strategies include involving women in education and decision-making, enhancing their 
skills, and supporting alternative livelihoods such as weaving or traditional product making. The 
project also generates employment opportunities through restoration, surveys, facilitation, forest 
patrols,  and  rehabilitation,  while  fostering  local  businesses  by  engaging  residents  in  providing 
goods  and  services  like  food  and  transportation.  To  ensure  lasting  impact,  it  strengthens 
community   and   institutional   capacity   through   training,   counseling,   seminars,   and   direct 
involvement in project activities. 
2.5.4 Indigenous Peoples and Cultural Heritage (VCS, 3.18, 3.19) 
KPCP is committed to preserving and protecting cultural heritage through integrated and context-
sensitive  approaches.  Central  to  this  is  the  recognition  that  the  Dayak  Ngaju  indigenous 
community  predominantly  inhabits  the  eight  villages  within  the  project area.  The  project 
acknowledges  their  deep  cultural  and  spiritual  connection  to  the  forest,  which  underpins  both 
livelihood  and  identity,  alongside  other  traditional  practices  and  sacred  sites.  A  precautionary 
approach is applied, taking into account local customs, cultural norms, capacities, and education 
levels, given the community’s sensitivity to cultural differences. To strengthen relationships, the 
project  fosters  critical  thinking  and  open  communication,  promoting  harmonization  between 
companies,   communities,   and   government.   Local   knowledge,   cultural   values,   and   socio-
economic   conditions   are   explicitly   integrated   into   project   planning   and   implementation, 
positioning  Dayak  traditions  and  cultural  heritage  sites  as  foundations  for  community-based 
development.   Customary   institutions   are   recognized   as   key   stakeholders,   whose   active 
involvement is essential for program design, institutional strengthening, and long-term  cultural 
preservation. In addition, the project provides grants to support traditional festivals and customs, 
ensuring that cultural practices such as rituals, ceremonies, and communal gatherings are not 
only preserved but also revitalized as living traditions that strengthen identity, social cohesion, 
and intergenerational knowledge transfer.

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
2.5.5 Statutory and Customary Property Rights (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.1) 
Statutory rights 
The project area falls within the legally granted concession of PT Ramang Agro Lestari (PT RAL), 
with  all  relevant  permits  and  approvals  detailed  in section 2.5.12.  Since  2023,  under  the 
management of PT Indo Hutan Ekosistem, the concession has been formally transitioned from 
industrial  timber  production  to  a  conservation-oriented  model,  with  amended  work  plans 
approved  by  the  Ministry  of  Forestry  authorizing conservation  and  restoration activities. The 
project therefore  holds  full  statutory  rights  to  manage  the  concession  for  conservation  and 
ecosystem service–based activities. 
Customary rights 
The  project  zone  is  predominantly  inhabited  by  indigenous  Dayak  Ngaju  communities,  who 
maintain customary ties to land and natural resources through gold mining, small-scale farming, 
gathering  of  forest  products,  and  river  use.  While  these  practices  are  not  formally  recognized 
under statutory law, the project respects customary rights and ensures that community access 
to subsistence resources is maintained in line with conservation goals. Continuous consultation, 
participatory  planning,  and  benefit-sharing mechanisms  have  been  established  to  safeguard 
customary use and promote inclusive participation in project activities. 
2.5.6 Recognition of Property Rights (VCS, 3.7, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.1) 
All statutory and customary property rights within the project area are recognized and respected. 
The  project holds  valid  concession  rights  as discussed in section 2.5.5 above,  and  these  are 
exercised in compliance with national regulations. The project also acknowledges customary use 
by  Dayak  Ngaju  communities  and  has  established  mechanisms  for  participatory  planning, 
benefit-sharing, and grievance redress to safeguard their rights and interests. More importantly, 
project activities do not result in the displacement of rights holders from their lands, nor do they 
restrict access to culturally or economically significant practices. Through these measures, the 
project actively supports both statutory security and customary recognition, fostering equitable 
and lawful management of the concession. 
2.5.7 Free, Prior and Informed Consent (VCS, 3.18; CCB, G5.2) 
The  FPIC  process  is  a  continuous,  iterative  process  of  dialogue,  negotiation,  and  consensus-
building conducted before any project intervention takes place and throughout the entire project 
lifecycle. Information regarding the use, access, or management of Indigenous and community 
lands must be conveyed in an open, transparent, and easily understood manner. For KPCP, FPIC 
is  an  integral  part  of  ensuring  its  ecosystem  restoration  project  is  implemented  inclusively, 
respects  indigenous  rights,  and  delivers  tangible  social  and  environmental  benefits  within  its 
approximately 13,580-hectare concession in Central Kalimantan. 
The implementation of FPIC, consists of seven stages described in the table below: 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
95 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Description of process 
for obtaining consent 
Step 1: Identifying rights holders and their rights
 
This process begins with identifying individuals or community groups 
that  hold  rights  to  territories,  resources,  or  decision-making,  and 
understanding  how  these  rights  are  recognized  under  customary, 
national,  or  international  law.  Indigenous  Peoples  (IPs) and  Local 
Communities (LCs) have long-standing connections to their ancestral 
lands,  guided  by  customary  laws  and  institutions  that  shape  their 
cultural   and   social   life   while   ensuring   stewardship   of   natural 
resources.  They  share  a  distinct  cultural  identity  expressed  through 
language,   beliefs,   traditions,   and   rich   systems   of   traditional 
knowledge.  
KPCP attempted this  approach  in  all eight villages  where  FPIC  was 
initiated  (Humbang  Raya,  Hanua,  Hurung,  Manen  Kaleka,  Manen 
Paduran, Bereng Rambang, Lawang Uru and Parahangan). To ensure 
culturally sensitive engagement and build trust, the assessment team 
worked with local enumerators throughout the process. The objective 
was   to   document   the   socio-economic   conditions,   resource   use 
patterns,  and  cultural  practices  of  the  Dayak  Ngaju  indigenous 
community,  who  are  the  primary  inhabitants  of  these  villages  and 
whose  deep  ties  to  the  forest  remain  central  to  their  identity  and 
livelihoods. 
Understanding land ownership within the project area is a vital part of 
identifying    rights.    It    ensures    Indigenous    Peoples    and    local 
communities    are    recognized    as    legitimate    rights    holders, 
safeguarding  their  ability  to  give  or  withhold  consent  over  activities 
that  may  affect  their  lands  and  resources.  It  also  helps  capture  the 
complex  cultural,  livelihood,  and  legal  dimensions  of  land  tenure, 
ensuring  that  project  implementation  is  inclusive,  respectful,  and 
sustainable.  In  the project area,  land  ownership is  complex  and 
includes several legal classifications:  
•
 
Right to Cultivate (HGU) – Typically held by oil palm companies, 
granting   them  rights  to  manage  and  utilize   the   land  for 
plantation purposes. 
•
 
Private  Ownership  (Hak  Milik – SHM) – Held  by  individual 
community members, often acquired through inheritance, first-
use   claims,   village   land   allocations,   or   purchase.   Many 
residential plots received SHM titles under the PRONA Program 
in 2014. 
•
 
Customary  Land  Rights – Governed  by  traditional  law,  these 
rights  include  opening  land  for  use  and  collecting  forest 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
96 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
products.  The  Dayak  Misik  Farmers  Group  has  been  actively 
campaigning  for  the  allocation  of  5  hectares  of  land  per 
household,  highlighting  ongoing  challenges  where  claims  are 
made  without  fully  considering  the  official  land  status  (e.g., 
forest areas versus Other Use Areas/APL). 
•
 
Communal   and   Open   Access   Rights – In   some   areas, 
community   members   hold   shared   rights   to   use   common 
resources. In others, where no specific rights are granted, land 
and resources remain freely accessible. 
Step  2:  Preparing  for  follow-up  engagement  and  agreeing  on  the 
scope
 
After    identifying    the    rights    holders,    the    project    initiated 
communication  to  build  trust,  present  the  objectives,  and  agree  on 
the  scope  of  the  participatory  consultation  process.  Within  the 
premises of KPCP, the Social Impact Assessment (SIA) was conducted 
from 14–25 July 2025, beginning with a baseline social data survey 
through questionnaires, followed by focus group discussions (FGDs). 
The  baseline  stage  covered  eight  villages  with  349  respondents, 
providing an overview of community conditions, the Forest Utilization 
Business Permit (PBPH) area of KPCP, and the SIA plan itself. These 
initial  engagements  created  a  foundation  for  relationship-building, 
emphasizing     mutual     respect,     transparency,     and     inclusive 
participation. 
Step 3: Participatory Mapping and Assessment
 
The project carried out participatory area mapping and socio-cultural 
assessments  with  community  members  to  better  understand  local 
contexts, values, and the potential impacts of planned activities. This 
process  aimed  to  identify  individuals  and  groups  who could  be 
positively or negatively affected by the project’s operations.  
The  process uses Global  Positioning  System  (GPS)  devices  to  allow 
community   members   to   actively   map   customary   land   rights, 
boundaries,  and  land  use  patterns  efficiently  and  cost-effectively. 
Before  the  GPS  mapping,  the  project  team  consulted  with  local 
villagers   to   gather in-depth   information   and   confirm   land   use 
locations  on  the  site,  ensuring  that  technical  mapping  aligned  with 
community  knowledge  and  realities.  These  community-generated 
maps enhance the spatial accuracy and legitimacy of tenure claims, 
while  also  serving  as  tools  for  future  monitoring,  verification,  and 
compliance. Beyond technical outputs, participatory mapping fosters 
transparency,    empowers    local    stakeholders,    and    integrates 
traditional  knowledge  into  land-use  planning  and  decision-making. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
97 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
This step was completed in seven villages except Lawang Uru where 
community   members   request   additional information   to better 
understand the project before sharing their customary land claims.  
Step 4: Provide information to affected rights holders
 
A  core  step  in  the  FPIC  process  is  to  provide  complete,  clear,  and 
easily understood information to affected rights holders regarding the 
planned  activity.  This  information  includes the  potential  benefits  of 
the project, possible risks, and both short- and long-term impacts. The 
objective of this stage is to ensure that Indigenous Peoples and local 
communities have a comprehensive understanding of the proposed 
initiatives before making any decisions. By establishing transparency 
at  this  stage,  the  process  safeguards  the  community’s  right  to 
informed    consent    and    lays    the    foundation    for    meaningful 
participation. 
KCPC successfully completed this information dissemination step in 
all eight  villages.  The  information  was  delivered  primarily  through 
focus  group  discussions  (FGDs),  during  which  the  project  team 
explained its objectives and ecosystem restoration plans. At the same 
time,  the  team  gathered  input  on  the  villages’  potential  to design 
programs  that  generate  tangible  benefits  for  the  community  and 
surrounding environment. These sessions emphasized collaboration, 
cultural    sensitivity,    and    openness,    highlighting    the    project’s 
conservation-oriented   approach   and   commitment   to   working in 
genuine partnership with Indigenous Peoples and local communities. 
The activities related to this step were carried out on various dates in 
June 2025 across the different villages: 
•
 
Parahangan Village: Information dissemination was conducted 
during  two  meetings,  with  the  first  meeting  held  on  June  7, 
2025, and a subsequent meeting on June 22, 2025. FGDs in 
Parahangan were done on June 17, 2025. 
•
 
Bereng  Rambang  Village:   Information  regarding   PT   RAL's 
planned  activities  was  received  by  the  community  during  an 
FGD on June 17, 2025. 
•
 
Lawang Uru Village: An FGD was conducted on June 17, 2025, 
during    which    the    information    gathering    process    was 
successfully carried out. 
•
 
Hurung   Village:   Information   gathering   through   an   FGD 
proceeded on June 19, 2025. 
•
 
Hanua Village: The information gathering process through  an 
FGD occurred on June 19, 2025.

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
•
 
Manen  Paduran  Village:  FGDs,  which  included  information

Step 5: Affected rights holders discuss and decide
 
This  step  ensures  that  affected  rights  holders  are  given  sufficient 
space  and  time  for  internal  discussions,  free  from  pressure,  to 
independently  decide  whether  to  approve  or  reject  the  planned 
activity. In line with this principle, the KPCP project team carried out 
activities in seven of the eight villages. Activities were not conducted 
in  Lawang  Uru,  as  the  villagers  requested  additional  information  to 
clarify that the company is not a palm oil plantation. Activities typically 
involved  community  deliberation  sessions,  often  following  Focus 
Group  Discussions  (FGDs)  where  information  had  been  shared.  The 
outcomes varied across the villages: in some, consensus was reached 
and agreements were signed, while in others discussions took place 
but no formal agreement was concluded. Where agreements were not 
finalized, the FPIC process did not advance beyond Step 5. 
Villages where consensus was reached and agreements were signed 
(FPIC completed through Step 7): 
• Humbang  Raya  Village:  Community  members   engaged  in 
deliberations during FGDs and appointed five representatives 
to  ratify  the  FPIC  document.  The  community  expressed  full 
support   for   PT   RAL’s   ecosystem   restoration   plan,   with 
conditions  to  develop  a  village demonstration  garden  and 
ensure community involvement. 
•
 
Parahangan  Village:  The  FPIC  process  involved  two  FGDs 
where  the  community,  with  strong  backing  from  the  Village 
Head,  agreed  to  accept  PT  Raman  Agro  Lestari’s  presence. 
Eight representatives ratified the FPIC document on behalf of 
37 participants. 
•
 
Manen Paduran Village: The community actively discussed and 
verified   information,   showing   strong   understanding   and 
engagement. Five representatives ratified the FPIC document, 
which was endorsed by 34 participants. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
99 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Villages where discussions were held, but no formal agreement was 
signed: 
• Hurung   Village:   Information   gathering   went   seamlessly; 
however,  it  is  still  pending  between  PT  RAL  and  the  village 
government to reach a mutual agreement on the operation of 
KPCP. 
• Bereng Rambang Village: The FGD process was complicated by 
varied interests in PT RAL’s permit area. While information was 
exchanged, further steps were needed to resolve disputes and 
reach consensus. 
• Manen Kaleka Village: Concerns over possible land conversion 
to oil palm plantations and past negative experiences led the 
Village  Head  to  delay  the  FPIC  agreement  pending  broader 
community discussions. 
• Hanua  Village:  Although  the  community  recognized  that  PT 
RAL’s  operations  posed  no  direct  harm,  unresolved  land 
ownership conflicts created obstacles. Landowners requested 
additional  meetings,  resulting  in  a  decision  to  postpone  the 
FPIC process. 
Step 6: Verify and formalize the FPIC Agreement
 
This  step  requires  that  documentation  of  consent  or  rejection  be 
carried out in a transparent, legal, and mutually agreed-upon manner. 
The  formal  agreement  must  unequivocally  reflect  a  decision  that  is 
genuinely  free  and  informed,  following  the  internal  deliberations 
conducted in Step 5. To ensure legitimacy, such agreements must be 
officially  recorded  and  recognized  by  all  relevant  parties.  The  KPCP 
project  team  facilitated  this  process  in three of  the  eight  villages. 
While outcomes varied—with some villages reaching consensus and 
formalizing   agreements,   and   others   remaining   without   formal 
documentation—this stage was crucial in confirming the integrity and 
accountability of the FPIC process. 
Villages  where  consensus  was  reached  and  FPIC  agreements  were 
formalized (Step 6 completed): 
•
 
Humbang  Raya  Village:  The  Minutes  of  the  FPIC  Agreement 
were officially signed on 20 June 2025. 
•
 
Parahangan Village: The Minutes of the FPIC Agreement were 
signed on 17 June 2025, with community endorsement.

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
•
 
Manen  Paduran  Village:  The  Minutes  of  the  FPIC  Agreement 
were signed on 23 June 2025, following thorough community 
discussions. 
Step 7: Implement and monitor the FPIC Agreement
 
Implement and monitor the FPIC Agreement is the final stage in the 
Free, Prior, and Informed Consent (FPIC) process, where the agreed-
upon activities are carried out, and a system for continuous oversight 
and feedback is established. This step involves:  
• Implementing   activities    in    accordance    with    the    FPIC 
agreement,  
• Ensuring a joint monitoring mechanism,  
• Establishing a feedback system that is responsive to changes 
and community concerns. 
For Parahangan, Manen Paduran, and Humbang Raya, the next step 
is to follow through on the commitments made during FGDs by turning 
agreements   into   clear   action   plans,   involving   communities   in 
implementation, and setting up joint monitoring to show transparency 
and  accountability.  Regular  updates  will  help  strengthen  trust  and 
confirm that the agreements bring tangible benefits. 
For  the five villages  without  agreements,  the  priority  is  to  deepen 
engagement through more frequent visits, targeted discussions with 
key  individuals,  and  open  forums  to  address  concerns.  Tackling 
issues  such  as  land  disputes,  skepticism,  and  negative  perceptions 
with patience  and  transparency  will  be  essential  to  gradually  build 
trust and move these communities closer to consensus.
 
Outcome of FPIC 
process 
The FPIC process was initiated in all eight target villages. However, 
Lawang  Uru  Village  was  the  sole  exception  where  no  formal  FPIC 
meeting  was  held,  as  additional  information  was  requested  by  the 
community.   Among   the   seven   villages   that   participated,   FPIC 
agreements   were   successfully   implemented   and   formalized   in 
three—Humbang  Raya,  Parahangan,  and  Manen  Paduran.  In  the 
remaining four villages—Hurung, Bereng Rambang, Manen Kaleka, 
and  Hanua—the  process  either  did  not  reach  consensus  or  was 
postponed  due  to  unresolved  issues  and  community  concerns. 
These  mixed  results  reflect  both  the  progress  achieved  in  certain 
communities   and   PT   RAL’s   ongoing   efforts   to   engage   with 
stakeholders and address outstanding matters. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
101 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
PT   RAL will ensure   that   conflict   resolution   mechanisms   are 
accessible  and  impartial,  while  promoting  participatory  decision-
making  processes  that  allow  communities  to  voice  concerns  and 
shape project activities. These steps will not only reinforce PT RAL’s 
credibility  but  also  provide  a  pathway  toward  more  inclusive  and 
sustainable FPIC outcomes. 
2.5.8 Benefit Sharing Mechanisms (VCS, 3.18, 3.19;) 
Process used to 
design the benefit 
sharing plan  
The  design  of  KPCP’s  benefit-sharing  plan  is  embedded  within  the 
broader  Social  Impact  Assessment  (SIA)  and  Free,  Prior,  and  Informed 
Consent  (FPIC)  processes.  This  ensures  that  the  perspectives  of  all 
affected stakeholder groups are meaningfully included from the outset. 
A  series  of  community  consultations,  focus  group  discussions,  and 
bilateral meetings with customary leaders, women’s groups, and youth 
representatives   were   held   to   introduce   project   objectives,   explain 
potential streams of benefits, and gather community aspirations.  
As a result of those meetings, a general benefit-sharing framework was 
co-designed  together  with  the  community  at  project  initiation,  with  an 
understanding that details will be continuously refined over the project 
lifecycle.  This  adaptive  approach  is  reasonable  because  the  timing, 
volume,  and  type  of  benefits  depend  on  ecological  performance  and 
market  conditions,  both  of  which  require  an  iterative  and  participatory 
adjustment process. By embedding benefit-sharing design in an ongoing 
dialogue  process,  the project aims to  strengthen  transparency,  local 
ownership, and adaptive management. 
Summary of the 
benefit sharing 
plan 
The general benefit-sharing plan covers the following aspects: 
• 
Employment  and  wages:
 Local  residents  will  receive  priority 
employment  and  fair  wages for  roles  in  tree  planting,  seedling 
nursery   management,   forest   patrols,   fire   response   units, 
biodiversity monitoring, and other project activities. 
• 
Agroforestry and livelihood enhancement:
 Farmers participating 
in  agroforestry  schemes  will  benefit  from  training,  inputs,  and 
market access support. Over time, they will retain ownership of 
harvests  and  receive  assistance  in  identifying  off-takers  for 
commodities such as fruit or non-timber forest products. 
• 
Community   development   support:
 The   project   will   provide 
support  for  community  development  initiatives  identified  as 
local priorities through participatory planning. This includes the 
offering of education   scholarships,   community grants for 
infrastructure improvement projects, and resources to preserve 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
102 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
traditional   customs   and   cultural   practices.   This   approach 
ensures  that  benefits  extend  beyond  individual  households  to 
collective  well-being,  reinforcing  long-term  social  cohesion  and 
resilience. 
• 
Non-financial   benefits:
 Beyond   monetary   contributions,   the 
project supports capacity building, training in sustainable land 
management, agroforestry,  NTFPs  enterprises, and  technical 
assistance   for   diversified   livelihoods.   These   will   eventually 
strengthen  household  resilience  and  reduce  dependency  on 
extractive practices. 
This balanced mix ensures both household-level benefits and collective 
gains,  while  aligning  with  the  long-term  sustainability  goals  of  the 
project. 
Approval and 
dissemination of 
benefit sharing 
plan 
The  general  benefit-sharing  plan  has  been  formally  discussed  and 
validated through the FPIC process, with minutes of meetings and signed 
consent documents retained as evidence of community approval. While 
further details will be decided in the future, the principles of equitable 
distribution,  transparency,  and  inclusivity  have  been  widely  endorsed. 
The  plan will  be disseminated  in  culturally  appropriate  ways,  including 
through village assemblies, women’s group meetings, illustrated leaflets 
in local language, and workshops  
2.5.9 Property Rights Protection (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.3) 
KPCP is committed to upholding and promoting the property rights of Indigenous Peoples (IPs) 
and Local Communities (LCs) within and surrounding its ecosystem restoration concession area. 
The project adopts a rights-based approach that prioritizes the protection of these property rights 
through a rigorous Free, Prior, and Informed Consent (FPIC) process, ensuring that communities 
have  the  genuine  opportunity  to  give  or  withhold  consent  for  any  proposed  interventions. 
Importantly, the project does not entail the removal or relocation of property rights from lands 
belonging to local communities. 
2.5.10 Illegal Activity Identification (VCS, 3.19; CCB, G5.4) 
Illegal   activities   represent   a   significant   threat   to   the   project’s   climate,   community,   and 
biodiversity outcomes. Within the project zone, the most common risks are associated with illegal 
logging, hunting, and unpermitted land clearing for agriculture. These practices not only lead to 
forest degradation and biodiversity loss but also directly undermine carbon emission reduction 
goals. While national and regional regulations prohibit land clearing and cultivation by burning, 
such practices remain a recurring challenge in rural forest-edge communities where enforcement 
is often limited. Encroachment into concession areas by smallholders for subsistence agriculture 
or cash crop expansion poses an additional risk that, if left unmanaged, could erode the integrity 
of the project’s conservation and restoration objectives. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
103 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Beyond   forest   degradation,   other   potential   risks   include   the   establishment   of   informal 
settlements within concession boundaries, which may create long-term land tenure disputes and 
accelerate  forest  conversion.  Moreover,  weak  governance  in  frontier  landscapes  can  create 
space for corruption or the issuance of illegal permits that overlap with existing concession rights. 
Such actions could enable extractive activities within the project area, further jeopardizing both 
the ecological integrity of the concession and the social benefits intended for local communities. 
These  risks  underscore  the  importance  of  proactive  monitoring,  transparent  governance,  and 
multi-stakeholder coordination to safeguard the project’s impacts. 
To   reduce   and   prevent   these   illegal   activities,   the   project   will   implement   a   series   of 
complementary measures: 
• Forest monitoring  and patrols: Deploy  community  patrol  teams  and  project  rangers, 
supported  by  satellite  hotspot  detection  (NASA  FIRMS),  drones,  and  intensified  ground 
surveillance during high-risk periods. 
• Alternative employment opportunities: Recruit local residents into project activities such 
as reforestation, nursery operations, and fire management, thereby reducing reliance on 
destructive and illegal practices. 
• NTFP training and agroforestry program: Support the development of non-timber forest 
product  (NTFP)  enterprises  and  sustainable  agroforestry  to  create  incentives  for  long-
term forest stewardship. 
• Partnership with authorities: Collaborate with government agencies and law enforcement 
to report and act on illegal logging, encroachment, and unpermitted activities. 
• Awareness  and education campaigns: Conduct  regular  community  outreach  to  build 
understanding  of  forest  laws,  fire  risks,  and  the  ecological  and  cultural  value  of 
conservation. 
• Multi-stakeholder coordination: Establish    dialogue    forums    that    bring    together 
communities, authorities, and concession holders to align land management and prevent 
conflicting activities. 
• Grievance redress  and conflict resolution: Provide  accessible  grievance  channels  and 
mediation  mechanisms  to  resolve  land-use  disputes  before  they  escalate  into  illegal 
activities. 
The project also demonstrates full compliance with labor safeguards to ensure that benefits are 
not  derived  from  exploitative  practices.  In  line  with  Indonesian  labor  law  and  international 
standards, the project explicitly prohibits the use of child labor, forced labor, or trafficked persons 
in  all  its  activities.  Recruitment  follows  transparent  procedures,  contracts  are  provided  to  all 
employees,  and  wages  are  paid  fairly.  Regular  internal  oversight  and  an  open  grievance 
mechanism   ensure   that   all   workers   are   protected,   while   monitoring   systems   safeguard 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
104 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
compliance  with  International  Labour  Organization  (ILO)  conventions.  These  measures  ensure 
that the project’s outcomes are achieved ethically and responsibly. 
2.5.11 Ongoing Disputes (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, G5.5) 
While there is not yet a final agreement on the administrative boundaries between some villages, 
the process of definitive boundary determination is ongoing through government facilitation, and 
the project will adapt once these are formally agreed. Meanwhile, all project activities are being 
implemented  in  accordance  with  national  laws  and  with  respect  for  customary  land  tenure 
arrangements,  and  no  actions will  be undertaken  that  could  prejudice  the  outcome  of  any 
dispute. 
The  project  acknowledges  that  maintaining  transparent  communication  and  trust  with  local 
stakeholders is essential to prevent the emergence of new disputes. To this end, the project has 
established  mechanisms  for  continuous  engagement  with  communities,  including  regular 
consultation meetings, participatory land-use planning, and the involvement of local leaders in 
decision-making   processes.   Dedicated   community   development   staff,   with   experience   in 
mediation  and  conflict  resolution,  are  tasked  with  monitoring  potential  sources  of  tension  and 
addressing  issues  proactively.  In  the  event  that  disputes  do  arise,  the  project  will  rely  on  its 
established  grievance  redress  mechanism  (detailed  in section 2.3.15),  which  provides  a 
transparent and fair process for resolution, ensuring that all parties are heard, and outcomes are 
agreed upon in a collaborative manner.

2.6 Additional Information Relevant to the Project  
2.6.1 Leakage Management (VCS, 3.11, 3.15) 
REDD+ Leakage 
Based on the assessment in section 3.2.3.1, both activity shifting leakage, market leakage and 
ecological  leakage for  this  project  have  been  assessed  as  zero,  and  therefore  no  mitigation 
measures  are  required.  Activity  shifting  leakage  is  considered  zero  because  PT  RAL — the 
identified agent of deforestation — holds no other active concessions. Furthermore, PT RAL's has 
been  fully acquired by  PT  IHE which  has  a conservation  commitment  and  has  no  intention  for 
deforestation. Market leakage is also deemed zero, as the project does not affect national-level 
production  of  timber,  fuelwood,  or  charcoal. Ecological  leakage  is  not  expected  either,  as  the 
project is designed to conserve undrained peat areas and project activities will not cause change 
to local hydrology. However, the project will continue to systematically monitor ecological leakage 
throughout  the  crediting  period  by  observing  water  levels  and  tracking  land-use  changes  to 
ensure that this remains the case. 
ARR Leakage 
Analysis  in  section 3.2.3.2 shows  that  leakage  from  ARR  activities  is  considered  zero  as  no 
agricultural  displacement  is  expected  to  occur. Participatory  mapping  and  satellite  imagery 
analysis   confirmed   that   palm   oil   is   the   only   agricultural   commodity   cultivated   by   local 
communities  within  the  project  area. Existing palm  oil  plantations  have  been  identified  and 
excluded  from  the  eligible  area  for  ARR  activities,  ensuring  that  communities  can  continue 
managing this land without disruption. Additionally, fuelwood is not cultivated or commercialized 
but  is  sourced  informally  from  naturally  fallen  trunks  and  existing  branches. As  no  leakage  is 
expected,  no  mitigation  measures  are  required.  Nonetheless,  a  monitoring  plan  will  be 
implemented to observe and document any potential leakage throughout the project duration.

3.1.3 Project Boundary (VCS, 3.12) 
3.1.3.1 Land Cover Classification 
Satellite imagery was utilized to delineate the project area based on vegetation types, structural 
characteristics, and other land cover features. The project applies Verra’s methodology VM0007 
and module VMD0016 to conduct stratification of Land Use and Land Cover (LULC) for 2023 and 
2013 images. To align with national standards, the classification approach follows the land cover 
classification  system  established  by  the  Ministry  of  Environment  and  Forestry  (KLHK),  which 
defines 23 distinct land cover classes
19
. These classes span a wide range of cover types, including 
multiple forest categories, shrublands, plantations, settlements, water bodies, and bare lands. An 
overview of the high-level processing steps is presented in the diagram below. 
 
Figure 16: Flow Chart Land Cover Classification 
 
The  2013  image  was created  by  combining Landsat-8  and  SPOT5  images to  minimize  cloud 
coverage. The 2023  image  was  processed  on  Google  Earth  Engine by compositing data  from 
Sentinel-2  imagery  with  additional  cloud  probability  and  classification  information.  It  excluded 
those  with  high  cloud  coverage (>90%). Remaining  images  were  filtered  using  pixel-level  cloud 
probability  estimates (<15%) and  scene  classification  labels  to  identify  and  mask  clouds  and 
shadows.  After  applying  these  masks  to  all  images,  it  compiled the  remaining  clear  pixels  into  a 
single median composite image. The result is a clean, cloud-minimized visual representation of the 
area. 
 
19
 Direktorat Inventarisasi Gas Rumah kaca dan Monitoring Pelaporan Verifikasi. 2023. Kerangka Metodologi Penghitungan pengurangan 
Emisi/Peningkatan Serapan Gas Rumah Kaca (GRK) Sektor Forestry and Other Land Use (FOLU).  Direktorat Jenderal Pengendalian 
Perubahan Iklim, Kementerian Lingkungan Hidup dan Kehutanan 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
122 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The  project  utilized  Sentinel-2  satellite  images,  Landsat-8  and  SPOT5  images to  conduct LULC 
analysis. Images were composited to minimize cloud cover and enhance classification accuracy. 
Table 14: Satellite Images for LULC Analysis 
Period Sources Resolution Date 
2013 Landsat-8 Pan-sharpened 15m 26 June 2013 
Landsat-8 Pan-sharpened 15m 13 August 2013 
SPOT-5 Pan-sharpened 5m 23-June-2013 
2023 Sentinel-2A 10m January – July 2023 
Following image preparation, multiple distinct objects were identified within the imagery to develop 
a  training  dataset  for  supervised  classification.  This  interpretation  was  conducted  manually  by 
visually  assessing  the  spatial  characteristics  of  each  object  to  assign  an  appropriate  land  cover 
category. Within the PT RAL concession area, the following land cover types were identified: 
•
 
Secondary  Swamp  Forest
: Forest  on  wetland  area that  regenerates  naturally  after  being 
disturbed by human or natural activities. 
• 
Wet  Shrubland
: Shrub-dominated  areas  typically  occurring  in  unmanaged  wetlands  or 
swamp environments. 
• 
Bare Land
: Areas lacking significant vegetation cover, often resulting from human activity or 
land-use change. 
• 
Estate Crop
: Existing cultivated land with oil palm or other agricultural crops. 
• 
Water  Bodies
: Rivers,  canals,  and  ditches  were  mapped  using  data  from  Indonesia’s  RBI 
map
20
. Based on measurements, the Kaliwang River has an approximate width of 16 meters; 
therefore, an 8-meter buffer was applied on each side. Only primary canals and ditches that 
are wide enough is taken into consideration on the map. Canals and ditches are assumed to 
have an average width of 3 meters, with a 1.5-meter buffer applied on each side. 
A supervised classification was then performed using the Random Forest algorithm to generalize 
LULC  classes  across  the  imagery.  Random  Forest  proved  effective  in  producing  accurate 
classifications,  reducing  noise,  optimizing  the  use  of  training  data,  and  identifying  the  most 
important predictor variables. The final result of LULC is in below figures and tables.

Accuracy Assessment 
The  accuracy  assessment  evaluates  how  closely  the  land  cover  classification  results  represent 
actual  on-the-ground  conditions.  This  evaluation  is  conducted  by  comparing  the  land  cover 
classifications derived from satellite imagery with ground truth data collected through direct field 
surveys. In this project, 67 sample plots were surveyed. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
125 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
A confusion matrix was used to perform the accuracy assessment. This statistical tool compares 
the  predicted  land  cover  classes  with  the  observed  classes  to  determine  the  classification’s 
performance.  From  the  confusion  matrix,  several  accuracy  metrics  were  calculated,  including 
overall accuracy, producer’s accuracy, user’s accuracy, and the kappa coefficient. 
According to established quality standards, land use classifications with an accuracy value greater 
than 85% are considered suitable for further analysis.

The overall classification accuracy was 93%, exceeding the 85% quality threshold. This indicates 
that the classified data closely matches the actual land cover conditions, demonstrating that the 
model  is  both  precise  and  reliable  for  further  analysis.  The Kappa  statistic,  which  measures  the 
level of agreement between the classification results and the reference data, was 0.81. A Kappa 
value of this magnitude represents an “almost perfect” level of agreement according to standard 
interpretation guidelines
21
. 
3.1.3.2 Peat and Non-Peat Stratification 
The stratification of peat and non-peat areas is determined using three main sources: the Ministry 
of Forestry’s indicative map, field surveys, and river identification. In accordance with VCS Verra’s 
VMD0016  (X-STR)  Module  Version  1.3,  soils  containing  peat  layers  less  than  50  cm  thick  are 
classified as mineral soils. The figure below presents a high-level process for delineating peat areas 
and developing a peat depth map.

3.1.3.3 Stratification of Peatland based on Peat Thickness and Peat Depletion Time (PDT) 
Drained peatlands are vulnerable to microbial decomposition under baseline scenario. As a result, 
peat in certain parts of the project area may be depleted before the end of the project period. To 
estimate the peat depletion time (PDT)—the period it would take for peat in the project area to be 
completely lost without the project—we used the following parameters: 
•
 
Initial peat thickness 
•
 
Drainability elevation limit 
•
 
Surface elevation 
•
 
Subsidence caused by microbial decomposition and burning 
Peat Thickness 
PT RAL is bordered by two major rivers: the Mangkutup River and the Kahayan River. The Kaliwang 
River  traverses  the  area  from  northwest  to  southeast.  To  collect  peat  data,  13  transects  were 
established  across  the  PT  RAL  concession,  covering  multiple  locations.  The  transects  were 
positioned perpendicular to the two bordering rivers, following the predicted contours of the peat 
dome.  Each  transect  comprised  five  sampling  points  arranged  from  west  to  east.  The  sampling 
locations will be made available upon request. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
128 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The resulting data was processed in ArcGIS for further analysis. Peat interpolation modelling was 
performed  using  the  Topo  to  Raster  tool,  following  the  Director  General  of  Pollution  and 
Environmental  Damage  Control  Regulation  No.  18/PPKL/PKG/PKL.0/11/2019 on  Technical 
Guidelines   for   Spatial   Data   Processing   of   Peat   Ecosystem   Characteristics. Developed   by 
Hutchinson  (1989),  Topo  to  Raster  is  a  hydrologically  sound  interpolation  method  that  applies  a 
thin  plate  smoothing  spline  algorithm,  integrated  with  topographic  and  hydrological  constraints. 
This  method  is  particularly  well-suited  for  peat  thickness  mapping,  as  it  produces  smooth, 
continuous surfaces that realistically represent features such as peat domes
22
. It also minimizes 
interpolation  artifacts,  such  as  the  “bullseye  effect,”  which  are  common  in  methods  like  Inverse 
Distance Weighting (IDW) or standard spline. 
The generated raster was then filtered to retain only peat thickness values of ≥ 50 cm. The resulting 
output  served  both  as  the  final  peat  thickness  map  and  as  the  basis  for  peat  thickness 
stratification. 
Table 19: Justification for peat stratification requirements 
Requirements per VM0007 module X-STR Findings Conclusion

Drainability Elevation Limit 
It was conservatively assumed that in the baseline scenario, mechanical pumping is not used to 
drain the peat. The thickness of the peat layer that’s available for microbial decomposition and the 
potential  risk  of  peat  burning is  limited  by  Drainability  Elevation  Limit  (DEL) – the  threshold 
elevation at which natural gravity drainage is no longer effective. 
The  determination  of  the  lowest  elevation  that  can  be  naturally  drained  is  based  on  the 
fundamental  principle  that  surface  water  flows  by  gravity  towards  the  nearest  water  body.  To 
establish this limit, water levels in the main receiving bodies—such as rivers, canals, and primary 
drainage outlets—were measured during field surveys. These measurements provided the average 
water  table  height  at  the  final  outlet  points  where  all  drainage  channels  converge.  Hydrological 
analysis  identified  the  Kaliwang  River  as the  nearest  primary  water  body  to  the  peat  areas; 
therefore, the DEL for the peat is estimated as the average water elevation, which is 11 m. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
131 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Figure 22: Drainability Elevation Limit 
 
Digital Terrain Model 
Indonesia’s National DEM, with a spatial resolution of approximately 8 meters, was integrated 
with canopy height data to produce a Digital Terrain Model that reflects the bare-earth elevation 
beneath vegetation cover. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
132 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Figure 23: Digital Terrain Model 
 
The Digital Terrain Model is combined with Drainability Elevation Limit to create a map of 
peatland subject to microbial decomposition and burning, based on the following rules: 
Peat available for microbial decomposition and burning = DEM – DEL 
Where: 
DEM – DEL ≤ Peat Thickness 
Peat Available for Microbial Decomposition and Burning = Peat Thickness 
Where: 
DEM – DEL > Peat Thickness

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4

peat before the end of the crediting period. Using the results from peat thickness, DTM and DEL 
maps and peat subsidence rate, PDT map is generated using the following formula:

3.1.3.5 Project Boundary for WRC Activities 
3.1.3.5.1 Geographical Boundaries 
For  the  WRC  activity, the  maximum  eligible  quantity  of  GHG  emission  reductions  from  the  soil 
carbon pool is limited to the difference between the remaining carbon stock in the project scenario 
and  baseline  scenarios  after  100  years.  The total  stock  approach  is  used to  demonstrate  a 
significant difference at the 100-year mark between baseline scenario and project scenario. The 
three-step approach is presented as follows: 
Step 1: Under the baseline scenario, successive changes in peat carbon stock within each stratum 
are modeled over a 100-year period. The remaining carbon stocks at year 100 are then mapped. 
The method used to calculate peat carbon stock dynamics over time is described in section 3.2.1

Step 2: Under the project scenario, successive changes in peat carbon stock within each stratum 
are modeled over a 100-year period. The remaining carbon stocks at year 100 are then mapped. 
The method used to calculate peat carbon stock dynamics overtime is described in section 3.2.2

separate  land  parcels  within  the  concession  were  designated  for  deforestation to  support 
community development through livelihood crops, specifically palm oil cultivation. Without PT IHE’s 
acquisition, implementation of this plan was expected to commence in 2023 and continue through 
2028. The map of PT RAL working plan is shown in
