3.1.4 Baseline Scenario (VCS, 3.13) 
3.1.4.1 Baseline Scenario for APD & WRC (VM0007) 
Determination of the most plausible baseline scenario builds on the outcome of the additionality 
analysis and must be consistent with the description of the conditions prior to the project start 
date.  VT0001 is used  to  assess  the  project  additionality.  The stepwise  approach is followed  in 
addition  to  VT0001  to  determine  the  most plausible  baseline  scenario. Following  is  the 
description of the stepwise approach: 
Step 1: Reuse the plausible alternative land scenarios to the REDD project activity that have been 
listed as an outcome of sub-step 1b of the additionality tool VT0001. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
151 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The  plausible  alternative  land  use  scenarios  listed  as  the  outcome  of  sub-step  1b  are  the 
following: 
§ Industrial Timber Plantation with Acacia and Eucalyptus 
§ Protected Forest without being registered as VCS AFOLU project 
§ Forest with commercial logging 
§ Unexploited Forest 
§ Smallholder Agriculture 
§ Mining 
Step  2a:  Where  the  VT0001  barrier  analysis  is  used  to  demonstrate  additionality,  apply  the 
decision tree in Figure below to the list of all alternative land use scenarios from Step 1 that are 
not prevented by any barrier. 
 
Figure 35: Barrier analysis decision tree 
 
1. Is  REDD  without  being  registered  as  a  VCS  project  activity  included  in  the  list  of  land  use 
scenarios that are not prevented by any barriers?  
Protected  Forest  without being  registered  as  the  VCS  AFOLU  project  is  included  in  the  list  
but it’s prevented by investment barrier according to barrier analysis in section 3.1.5.2.1 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
152 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
2. Does the list contain only one land use scenarios? 
According  to  section 3.1.5.2.1, industrial  timber  plantation  is  the  only  land  use  that isn’t 
prevented by any barrier. Therefore, it’s considered baseline scenario. 
3.1.4.2 Baseline Scenario for ARR (VM0047) 
Under  the  VCS  standard  and  methodology  VM0047,  the  realistic  and  credible  alternative 
scenario—i.e., the baseline scenario—for the current project area is defined as the business-as-
usual  change  in  above-ground  biomass  within  designated  control  plots.  These  control  plots 
represent areas where no project activity takes place. Changes in vegetation within these control 
plots serve as the reference for baseline conditions. The effectiveness of the reforestation project 
is then assessed by comparing vegetative cover in the project area to that in the control plots, 
using the performance benchmark approach as specified by the methodology. More details about 
the performance benchmark can be found at Appendix 4: Performance Benchmark Calculation. 
Historical analysis shows that changes in the stocking index for both project and control plots are 
closely aligned, with standardized mean difference (SMD) values below 0.25. This indicates that 
the control plots are representative of the project plots in terms of performance. The calculated 
changes in stocking index are -0.011856 for the project plots and -0.011766 for the control plots. 
In accordance with methodology VM0047, the historical ∆SI
control
 is used to establish the ex-ante 
estimate of the performance benchmark. However, if ∆SI
control
 is negative, it is set to zero.

a law, statute, or other regulatory framework, are such laws, statutes, or regulatory frameworks 
systematically enforced?

3.1.5.2 Additionality Methods (VCS, 3.14) 
3.1.5.2.1 Avoided Planned Deforestation – VM0007 
Methodology  VM007 uses  tool  VT0001 – Tool  for  the  Demonstration  and  Assessment  of 
Additionality  in  VCS  Agriculture,  Forestry  and  Other  Land  Use  (AFOLU)  Project  Activities to 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
153 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
demonstrate the additionality for all project activities except for WRC in tidal wetlands. This test is 
applicable to our project areas where avoided planned deforestation activities will be implemented 
on non-tidal wetlands. 
The four steps applied in the additionality test required in VT0001 are as follows: 
• Step 1. Identification of alternative land use scenarios to the AFOLU project activity’s 
• Step 2.  Investment  analysis  to  determine  that  the  proposed  project  activity  is  not  the  most 
economically or financially attractive of the identified land use scenarios; or 
• Step 3. Barrier analysis; and 
• Step 4. Common practice analysis. 
Step 1: Identification of alternative land use scenarios to the proposed VCS AFOLU project activity 
Sub-step 1a. Identified credible alternative land-use scenarios to the proposed project activity. 
The range of realistic and credible alternative land use scenarios that would have occurred on the 
land within the project boundary in the absence of the project are shown in 
Table 33
. These seven 
scenarios were  derived  from  the  analysis  of historical land use across the Kalimantan’s lowland 
secondary forests together with an analysis of practices and economic trends within other similar 
regions of Indonesia. 
Table 33: Alternative Land use scenario 
Land Use Scenario Description 
Industrial Timber 
Plantation  with  Acacia  and 
Eucalyptus 
Prior  to  the  acquisition  of  PT  IHE,  PT  RAL  was  designated  for 
industrial timber plantation with Acacia and Eucalyptus. In 2017, 
a Business Permit for the Utilization of Timber Forest Products 
in   Industrial   Plantation   Forests   was   issued   under   Decree 
Number 2/1/IUPHHK-HTI/PMDN/2017. And in 2019, Ministry of 
Forestry  has  approved  PHHK-HTI  Business  Work  Plan  for  a  10-
Year  Period,  2019-2028  granting  the  permission  for  PT  RAL  to 
clear  the  land  and  conduct  timber  cultivation  with  Eucalyptus 
and Acacia species. 
Industrial timber plantations are prevalent near the project area. 
Specifically,  to  the  northeast  of  PT  RAL,  PT  Industrial Forest 
Plantation has been actively clearing and converting secondary 
forest into Acacia plantations. 
These  events  confirmed  that  the  pre-project  land  use  scenario 
was designated for Industrial Timber Plantation. In the absence 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
154 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
of  intervention  by  PT  IHE,  this  land-use  scenario  would  likely 
have continued. 
Protected   Forest   without 
being   registered   as   VCS 
AFOLU project 
Although rare, forest areas designated as Production Forest can 
be legally converted into Protected Forest. This legal conversion 
falls  under  the  authority  and  discretion  of  the  Ministry  of 
Environment and Forestry, as the central government holds the 
power to designate state forest areas.  
An example of such a conversion is the Sebangau National Park, 
where   portions   of   Production   Forest   were   converted   into 
national park. 
Industrial Palm Oil 
Plantation 
Industrial oil palm plantations are a common form of agricultural 
land use in Indonesia. As of 2023, the United States Department 
of Agriculture (USDA) reported that the total oil palm plantation 
area in the country reached 13.5 million hectares
23
. Oil palm is 
cultivated  in  25–35-year  rotations  and  becomes  commercially 
harvestable after 4 to 5 years, with its fruit processed into palm 
oil.  Over  the  past  few  decades,  the  sector  has  expanded 
significantly, making palm oil one of Indonesia’s leading export 
commodities.  
However  industrial  oil  palm  expansion  has  taken  place  almost 
exclusively   in areas   legally   outside   of   the   forest   estate 
(designated as Other Land Utilization, Area Penggunaan Lain, or 
APL)   or   within   the   forest   estate   in   areas   ear-marked for 
conversion  (designated  Conversion  Production  Forest,  Hutan 
Produksi Konversi, or HPK). The project area has no area within 
its  boundaries  designated as Hutan  Produksi  (HP)  not  HPK  or 
APL. 
Forest    with    Commercial 
Logging 
Historically,   forests   in   Central   Kalimantan   were   extensively 
exploited  through  selective  logging  practices.  However,  most 
commercial  logging  operations  have  since  shifted  to  primary 
forest   areas,   where   trees   meeting   the   minimum   allowable 
diameter  of 35 cm  are  still  available.  In  contrast,  the  PT  RAL 
concession  consists  solely  of  secondary  swamp  forest,  where 
most of trees have diameters below 35 cm, rendering the area 
economically unattractive for commercial logging. 
Unexploited Forest Unexploited  forest  areas  in  Indonesia  are  increasingly  rare.  In 
Central Kalimantan, any period of non-use is typically short-lived 
and   represents   a   transitional   phase   between   cycles   of 
 
23
 U.S. Department of Agriculture. Indonesia Palm Oil: Historical Revisions Using Satellite-Derived Methodology. Commodity Intelligence 
Report. Foreign Agricultural Service, November 9, 2023. https://ipad.fas.usda.gov/highlights/2023/11/Indonesia/index.pdf. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
commercial exploitation. Over time, forests in these areas tend 
to  degrade  rapidly,  losing  their  commercial  value  in  terms  of 
standing   timber.   As   a   result,   they   are   often   targeted   for 
conversion  into  monoculture  plantations,  such  as  oil  palm  or 
Acacia. 
Smallholder Agriculture A  portion  of  the  community  residing near the  project  area  is 
engaged  in  farming,  including  smallholder  oil  palm  cultivation, 
which  currently  covers  approximately 143 hectares  within  the 
project  boundary.  In  the  absence  of  the  VCS  AFOLU  project 
activities, the expansion of smallholder agriculture would likely 
have continued. 
Mining Four  mining  concessions  recently  granted  by  the  Ministry  of 
Energy and Mineral Resources overlap with our concession area 
(see

). The earliest of these concessions was issued in

the Ministry of Forestry. Most of the overlapping concessions are 
currently in the initial stages of exploration and reservation.

Land Use Scenario Consistency Analysis 
Industrial Timber 
Plantation  with  Acacia  and 
Eucalyptus 
This land use scenario is legally permissible, as PT RAL obtained 
permit for the Utilization of Timber Forest Products in Industrial 
Plantation  Forests issued  under  Decree  Number  2/1/IUPHHK-
HTI/PMDN/2017 and PHHK-HTI  Business  Work  Plan  for  a  10-
Year Period, 2019-2028. 
Protected   Forest   without 
being   registered   as   VCS 
AFOLU project 
Under  Indonesian  law,  Production  Forest  areas  may  be  legally 
reclassified as Protected Forest or Conservation Forest. A PBPH 
(Forest   Utilization   Business   Permit)   license   for   ecosystem 
restoration  can  be  granted  to  either  non-profit  or  for-profit 
entities  involved  in  forest  conservation  efforts.  Therefore,  this 
scenario aligns with the applicable legal framework currently in 
force in Indonesia. 
Industrial Palm Oil 
Plantation 
The legal designation of the project area is production forest or 
Hutan Produksi (HP). Legally, a commercial oil palm plantation 
can  only be  established  in  an  area designated  as  Other  Land 
Utilization,    Area    Penggunaan    Lain (APL) or    Conversion 
Production Forest, Hutan Produksi Konversi (HPK).  
As a result, the scenario of commercial conversion to oil palm is 
not considered a legally viable scenario. 
Forest    with    Commercial 
Logging 
As  per  Forestry  Laws  No.  41/1999  and  No.  19/2004,  and 
Ministry of Forestry Decree No. 31/2014, commercial logging is 
a legally permissible land-use. 
Unexploited Forest The  area  could  remain  as  unexploited  forest  if  it  does  not 
become   subject   to   any license   application   for   logging   or 
conversion to industrial agriculture. Secondly, the site could be 
subject  to  an  application  for  management  as  an  ecosystem 
restoration    concession,    a    form    of    logging    concession 
permissible on production forest land as regulated and later by 
Ministry of Forestry Decree No. 31/2014. 
Therefore,  this  scenario  is  consistent  with  enforced mandatory 
applicable laws in Indonesia. 
Smallholder Agriculture Under Forestry  Laws  No.  41/1999  and  No.  19/2004,  and 
Ministry    of    Forestry Decree    No.    31/2014,    smallholder 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
157 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
agriculture   is   not   a   legally   permissible   land-use on   areas 
designated as production forest. 
Therefore,  this alternative  land  use  scenario  is  NOT  consistent 
with enforced  mandatory  applicable  laws  and  regulations  in 
Indonesia. 
Mining All  four  mining  concessions—PT  Global  Kapuas  Borneo,  PT 
Mendawai  Borneo  Sejati,  PT  Global  Samba  Sejahtera,  and  PT 
Silika  Sumber  Panel  Energi—are  relatively  new  licenses  that 
remain  in  the  early  stages  of  development (reservation  and 
exploration status). In contrast, PT RAL has held its concession 
since  receiving  initial  approval  from  the  Ministry  of  Forestry  in 
2014. Prior to commencing operations, these mining companies 
are  required  to  obtain  approval  from  the  Ministry  of  Forestry. 
Given that the Ministry has already granted concession rights to 
PT  RAL  for  the  overlapping  area,  it  is  unlikely  that  these  new 
concessions will receive the necessary approvals. 
 
As a  result  of  the  legal  analysis,  aligned  with  the  prevailing  laws  and  regulations  in  Indonesia, 
industrial  palm  oil  plantation and  mining are not legally  permissible. Smallholder  agriculture 
scenario  is  not  legally  permitted  by  laws  but  remains  a  common  practice  in  Indonesia,  so  this 
scenario is retained to discuss further. 
Sub-step 1c. Selection of the baseline scenario  
This step is described in section 3.1.4. Industrial timber plantation is considered baseline scenario 
as a result of this analysis. 
Step 2. Investment analysis 
Tool VT0001 provides the option of either conducting barrier analysis or investment analysis. This 
project uses barrier analysis instead of investment analysis. 
Step 3. Barrier analysis 
Land Use Scenario Barrier Analysis 
Industrial Timber 
Plantation  with  Acacia  and 
Eucalyptus 
There  are  no  barriers  to  the  establishment  of  an  industrial 
plantation. The concession area is within production forest area 
and has been granted permission by Indonesian government to 
conduct Acacia and Eucalyptus plantation. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
158 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Protected   Forest   without 
being   registered   as   VCS 
AFOLU project 
Legally converting the land status to fully protected would likely 
receive   little   local   support,   as   it   would   impose   additional 
financial   and   management   responsibilities   on   the   local 
government without contributing to state revenue. 
Forest     with     commercial 
logging 
The  main  barrier  to  commercial  logging  in  the  forest  is  the 
absence of commercially viable timber in the secondary swamp 
forest, making conversion economically unfeasible. 
Unexploited Forest In  the  absence  of  potential  revenue  from  carbon  offset  sales, 
there  are  significant  barriers  to  maintaining  the  forest  in  its 
intact state, as the area would generate no income for either the 
state or local communities. 
Smallholder Agriculture Expansion of smallholder agriculture within areas designated as 
production forest is uncommon and typically occurs only in the 
absence of an active management license. Given that there are 
no    barriers    to    commercial Industrial    Forest    Plantation 
development in the project area, and the likelihood of the forest 
remaining unutilized over the long term is low, the expansion of 
smallholder agriculture in this area is not considered a credible 
scenario. 
In conclusion, substantial barriers hinder the feasibility of all previously considered land-use 
scenarios, leaving industrial timber plantation as the only credible alternative. 
Step 4. Common practice analysis 
Establishing protected forests on land legally designated for production is uncommon in Indonesia. 
Outside of officially recognized protected areas—and without the potential for revenue from carbon 
financing—there are very few such initiatives. Existing examples are typically small-scale projects 
supported by stable philanthropic donors. Even in these cases, such efforts often face resistance 
from local governments or communities, as the areas are seen as contributing little or no financial 
benefit to local budgets, despite being legally classified for production use. 
Conclusion 
It can be concluded that the most likely and plausible scenario for the project area is conversion to 
Industrial Timber Plantation. Therefore, the project is considered additional.

law, statute, or other regulatory framework.

Step 3. Investment barrier 
The  ARR project  activity  requires  significant  amount  of  upfront  investment  for  land  preparation, 
human resources, seed procurement and maintenance. The project uses investment comparison 
analysis to demonstrate the that the project activity is less financially attractive in the absence of 
carbon credits. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
161 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The ARR activity primarily focuses on the plantation of native species, with a portion of the area 
designated for agroforestry. Neither the native species plantation nor the agroforestry component 
is  expected  to  generate  revenue.  The  agroforestry  activities  are  designed  to  benefit  local 
communities,  allowing  villagers  to  harvest  fruit  products,  while  PT  RAL  does  not  anticipate  any 
income from these activities. As a result, the project’s Internal Rate of Return (IRR) is expected to 
be  zero,  and  the  cash  flow is  projected  to  remain  negative  during  the  initial  five-year  plantation 
period due to operational expenses. Therefore, the ARR project qualifies as additional, as it does 
not meet the required financial benchmarks without revenues from carbon credits. A more detailed 
financial analysis can be provided to Validation and Verification bodies upon request. 
3.1.6 Methodology Deviations (VCS, 3.20) 
There are no methodological deviations in this project. 
3.2 Quantification of Estimated GHG Emission Reductions and Removals 
3.2.1 Baseline Emissions (VCS, 3.15) 
3.2.1.1 General Procedures and Assumptions 
The project considers the following change in carbon stock to assess baseline scenario: 
• Aboveground  biomass  carbon  stock  changes  due  to Industrial  Timber  Plantation  and 
Community Crops 
• Peat microbial decompositions 
Peat burning is conservatively excluded from the baseline scenario. Emission from water bodies on 
peatlands is omitted because the contribution is less than 5% of total emissions. 
The  project  assumes  that  no  rewetting  events  occur.  Natural  events  that  are  excluded  from  our 
analysis include canal collapses, progressive peat subsidence that could alter relative water table 
depths,  and  reductions  in  CO₂ emissions  due  to  the  gradual  thinning  of  aerobic  soil  layers.  It  is 
further  assumed  that  baseline  agents  carry  out  routine  canal  maintenance  for  drainage  and 
transportation purposes. However, due to limited information regarding the volume and frequency 
of  dredging  activities,  emissions associated  with  dredging—specifically,  greenhouse  gas  (GHG) 
emissions resulting from peat exposure to aerobic decomposition during the spreading or piling of 
material  during  canal  establishment  or  maintenance—are  conservatively  excluded  from  the 
baseline  calculations.  This  exclusion  results  in  an  underestimation  of  emissions,  making  the 
baseline scenario highly conservative. 
In the baseline scenario, CO
2
 and CH
4
 are accounted for in the baseline, while N
2
O emissions were 
conservatively omitted.

Soil  type  analysis: 
Soil  types  in  both  the  proxy  and  project  areas  were  analyzed  using  the  global 
SoilGrids database
28
. Only two soil types were identified: Histosols and Nitisols. Nitisols are well-
drained  and  have  high  nutrient-holding  capacity,  making  them  naturally  suitable  for  industrial 
timber  plantations.  Histosols,  composed  primarily  of  organic  material,  typically  exhibit  poor 
drainage. However, in Indonesia, it is common practice to drain these soils and apply fertilizers to 
improve their suitability for plantation use. As a result, both soil types are considered suitable for 
timber plantations in both the proxy and project areas.

Indonesian DEM data is obtained to identify the slope classes for both project area and proxy areas. 
Around 98.97% of the project area is gentle slope and 1.03% is steep slope. All proxy areas have 
more than 80% of gentle slope and less than 20% of steep slope, which meets the requirement of 
±20% similar ratio requirement set by module BL-PL. Detailed result of slope analysis is explained 
in the table below:

company, identified as an agent of deforestation. Forest cover data for the years 2013 and 2023 
were obtained from the Global Forest Watch database and used for this assessment
29
. The results 
confirmed  that none  of proxy  areas  were  abandoned,  as  deforestation  activity  was  observed

3.2.1.3 Baseline scenario map 
The project area was stratified into multiple strata according to land use, emission profiles, and 
the timing of land conversion. The mapping process was carried out through the following steps: 
• Using Forest Management Plan (RKU) to delineate the annual working blocks for Industrial 
Timber Plantation (Acacia and Eucalyptus plantation) and Community Crops. 
•
 
Delineating exclusion area, which remains unaffected by deforestation agent and unchanged 
throughout the duration of the project. 
• Excluding protected areas as required by regulations including 50m river buffer, Germplasm 
Conservation Area, Wildlife Protection Areas. 
• Delineating of forest vs non-forest area at the start of the project 
•
 
Delineating infrastructure development  areas  including  roads,  basecamps. Roads  are 
assumed to have width of 5m. Basecamps have 100m buffer. 
•
 
Delineating water bodies: 
o
 
Natural   water   channels,   such   as   the   Kaliwang   River,   will   remain   unchanged 
throughout the entire project period.  
o
 
Secondary canals (7 m wide) are constructed within each working block to discharge 
water into natural channels and serve as the main outlets for tertiary canals. 
o
 
Tertiary canals (2 m wide) are constructed perpendicular to the secondary canals. 
•
 
Delineating drained vs undrained areas: 300m buffer and 150m buffer around both side 
of the secondary and tertiary canals are subjected to drainage. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
173 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
•
 
Delineating the  available  planting  zones: The  remaining Industrial  Plantation area  after 
deducting conservation area, water bodies and infrastructure. 
• Assigning the canal construction year according to the annual working schedule. 
• Assigning  infrastructure  construction  (roads,  basecamps)  in  accordance  with  annual 
working schedule. 
• Once the planting blocks are deforested, it’s planted immediately. 
The baseline scenario map is shown below. 
Figure 43: Baseline Scenario Map 
 
3.2.1.4 Wetland Restoration and Conservation (WRC) 
This  section  applies  VCS  methodology  VM0007  REDD+  MF  and  modules  BL-PEAT  to  model 
emission  from  baseline  scenario  for WRC  project  activities  on  peatland  that’s  expected  to  be 
drained in the absence of the project activity.

3.2.1.4.2 Baseline emissions from microbial decomposition of peat, peat burning, and water bodies. 
Spatial and Temporal variability 
GHG emissions  from  microbial  decomposition  of  peat were  quantified  using  a  spatially  and 
temporally  explicit  approach.  Each  baseline  stratum  was  divided  into  uniform  units  defined  by 
combinations of spatial variables, with each unit assigned specific GHG emission factors. As peat 
burning is conservatively excluded from the baseline scenario, all related fire variables have been 
omitted from the analysis. The table below outlines the spatial variables used in the quantification 
process. 
Table 42: List of spatial and temporal variables used for quantification of GHG emissions from 
microbial decomposition of peat, and water bodies 
Variables Description 
(A) Spatial Variables 
(A1) Soil Type Peat  vs  non-peat soil  stratification.  All  non-peat  soil  is  excluded 
from the GHG calculation. 98.31% of the project area is non-peat. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
177 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
(A2) Initial peat thickness 
available for microbial 
decomposition 
Derived from DTM, DEL and Peat Thickness maps as described in 
section 3.1.3.3. These maps are used to set the initial conditions 
for subsequent calculations of the remaining peat layer available 
for microbial decomposition. 
(A3) Initial stratum Stratum of the corresponding parcel at the project start date. This 
is   used   to   determine   the   emission   factor   (EF)   for   the 
corresponding  parcel for  the  duration  before  B1(in  this  table, 
below) take effect. 
(B) Temporal Variables 
(B1) Year of 
conversion/planting of 
the baseline land cover 
Determines the onset of land conversion from the initial stratum 
to   a   converted   or   planted   stratum.   Drainage   activities   are 
assumed to occur in the same year as the conversion or planting. 
This  variable  governs  the  assignment  of  all  relevant  drainage-
related  parameters,  including  the  selection  of  the  appropriate 
emission factor. 
(B2) Remaining peat 
thickness available    for 
microbial decompositions 
and burnings 
Used to determine whether PDT in the baseline scenario has been 
reached for the corresponding parcel at the corresponding year. If 
the  remaining  peat available  for microbial decomposition  in a 
given  stratum has  been  reduced  to  20  cm all  GHG  emissions  in 
that stratum are set to zero. 
Based on the soil type stratification (variable A1), only emission from peat microbial decomposition 
is considered in this calculation.

Subsidence related to initial compression, microbial decomposition and burning of peat 
The initial peat thickness in the baseline scenario is assumed equal to the initial peat thickness as 
mapped at the project start date. 
Peat loss was estimated based on microbial decomposition, using stratification of the project area 
by initial drainage conditions, year of land conversion, and specific subsidence rates that account 
for  compaction  following  drainage.  As  peat  burning  is  conservatively  excluded  from  the  baseline 
scenario, peat loss is assumed to result solely from microbial decomposition. The annual rate of 
peat and carbon stock loss is quantified using emission rates from microbial decomposition (CO₂ 
and CH₄), the bulk density of peat above the water table, and a defined carbon content value, as 
expressed in the following equation:

By tracking annual peat thickness in the baseline scenario, it is ensured that no GHG emissions 
are accounted for in any parcel within each stratum once the available carbon stock—or peat—has 
been  fully  depleted.  Conservatively,  peat  is  considered  depleted when  the  remaining  thickness 
available for decomposition falls below 20 cm.

Emission from peat burning 
Peat  burning  is  conservatively  assumed  to  be  absent  in  the  baseline  scenario.  Therefore, GHG 
emission from peat burning is set as zero. 
Emission from water bodies 
Based on the calculations, greenhouse gas (GHG) emission from water bodies on peatlands is 360 
tCO
2
 which contribute less than 5% of total emissions and are therefore considered negligible. As 
such, these emissions are conservatively treated as zero. 
3.2.1.5 Avoided Planned Deforestation (APD) 
This section applies VCS methodology VM0007 REDD+ MF and modules BL-PL to model emission 
from baseline scenario for avoided planned deforestation. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
187 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
3.2.1.5.1 Baseline carbon stock change 
According  to  module  VMD0006,  the  net  carbon  stock  changes  in  the  baseline  is  equal  to  the 
baseline pre-deforestation stock minus the long-term carbon stock after deforestation and minus 
the baseline stock that enters the wood products pool at the time of deforestation. In the context 
of PT RAL, the pre-deforestation stock refers to the carbon stored in secondary swamp forest, while 
the post-deforestation carbon stock includes: 
• 
Industrial  Timber  Plantation
: Average long-term carbon  stored  in  Acacia  and  Eucalyptus 
plantations managed under a 5-year harvesting cycle. 
• 
Estate Crops
: Average long-term carbon in Palm Oil plantation managed under 30-year cycle. 
•
 
Infrastructure
: Roads are made to facilitate transportation within the plantation areas. Carbon 
stock of infrastructure is assumed to be zero. 
•
 
Water Bodies
: Secondary and  tertiary canals are  made to  drain  water on  secondary swamp 
forest before plantation. Carbon stock of water bodies is assumed to be zero.

Biomass  sampling  was  conducted  in  May  2025  across 52 plots,  during  which  data  from  1,965 
individual trees were collected. The project uses the allometric model developed by Chave et al. 
(2014)
30
 to estimate aboveground biomass. This model was chosen for its broad acceptance and 
strong statistical performance. Chave’s equation demonstrated a high coefficient of determination 
(R² = 0.95) and a low root mean square error (RMSE) of 0.356 on the logarithmic scale.

By  applying  the  default  carbon  fraction  of  0.47,  the  average  aboveground  biomass  (AGB)  of  the 
secondary  swamp  forest  was  estimated  at 74.6 (tC/ha).  The  belowground  biomass  (BGB)  was 
estimated using the root-to-shoot ratio method as outlined in Indonesia’s Second Forest Reference 
Emission  Level  (FREL)  submission  to  the  UNFCCC.  In  accordance  with  the  2nd  FREL
31
 (KLHK, 
2020), a standardized and conservative BGB-to-AGB ratio of 0.22 is applied across all forest types. 
Using this ratio, the baseline belowground biomass is estimated at 16.41 (tC/ha).

The calculation only considers carbon stock from standing deadwood and carbon stock from lying 
deadwood is conservatively assumed as zero. 
Standing dead trees shall be measured using the same criteria used for measuring live trees and 
was categorized into four criteria (D1-D4): 
• Class 1: Tree with branches and twigs that resembles a live tree (except for leaves) (D1) 
• Class 2: Tree  with signs  of  decomposition  (other than loss of  leaves)  including  loss  of  twigs, 
branches, or crown. This class is further divided into three criteria: 
o D2 (Sound): Minimal decomposition, wood still hard 
o D3 (Intermediate): Initial decay, porosity increasing 
o D4 (Rotten): Advanced decay, soft and many voids 
Biomass  estimation  for  D1  was  conducted  using  the  allometric  model  from  Chave  et  al.  (2014), 
same as living tree, incorporating class-specific default wood density. For D2 to D4, biomass was 
estimated  using  defined  wood  density  values  provided  in 
Table 49
,  and  calculated  using  the 
following formula:

Baseline carbon stock change 
In the baseline scenario, changes in carbon stocks from litter, soil organic carbon (SOC) and non-
woody  biomass are  conservatively  excluded.  Additionally,  as  the  management  plan  does  not 
include harvesting wood from deforestation for use in wood products, the carbon pool associated 
with harvested wood products is omitted. 
Stock  changes  in  aboveground  biomass  and  litter  are  emitted  at  the  time  of  deforestation. 
Following deforestation, emissions from belowground biomass, dead wood, soil and wood products 
take place gradually over time. Stock changes in belowground biomass and dead wood are emitted 
at an annual rate of 1/10 of the stock change for 10 years. The baseline carbon stock change for 
the project is estimated using the following equation:

3.2.1.5.2 Greenhouse gas emissions 
Emissions  from  nitrogen  fertilizer  use  in  Acacia,  Eucalyptus,  and  palm  oil  plantations  are 
conservatively assumed to be zero. Emissions from biomass burning and fossil fuel use are also 
excluded, based on the assumption that deforestation by fire is not practiced. This is in line with 
Indonesian Forestry Law No. 41 of 1999, which explicitly prohibits the use of fire for land clearing 
activities. 
Therefore, net GHG emissions as a result of deforestation activities in the baseline scenario is 
conservatively set to 0.

3.2.1.5.4 Frequency of baseline renewal 
The baseline will be revised every ten years for ongoing planned deforestation in accordance with 
module VMD0006 and VCS Standard.

3.2.1.6 Afforestation, Reforestation and Revegetation (ARR) 
ARR activity in the project is developed using area-based approach in methodology VM0047. In the 
baseline  scenario,  carbon  stock  changes are  accounted  for  by  applying  the  crediting baseline 
performance benchmark value. The baseline scenario is the business-as-usual  growth  of  carbon 
stocks  in  aboveground biomass,  as  observed  on  representative  remotely  sensed  control  plots 
located outside of any registered AFOLU project area. 
The   business-as-usual   changes   in   vegetation   cover   are   represented   by   the   performance 
benchmark, which is equal to the ratio of average change in stocking index (SI) of control plots to 
project plots.  
Detailed calculation of stocking index (SI) and performance benchmark is explained in Appendix 
4: Performance Benchmark Calculation. 
3.2.2 Project Emissions (VCS, 3.15) 
3.2.2.1 General Procedures and Assumptions 
The  actual  emissions  and  change  in  carbon  stocks  will  be  determined  in  the  future  using  site 
specific  data. The  following  procedures  are applied  to project future  emissions  under project 
scenario. 
The project considers the following sources of emissions under project scenario: 
•
 
Aboveground biomass carbon stock changes due to REDD and ARR activities. 
• Peat microbial decompositions 
The  planned  project  activities  related  to  climate  are described  in  section 2.1.17 including 
conservation  of undrained  peatland,  reforestation,  assisted  natural  regeneration, avoidance  of 
deforestation and forest degradation and forest fire control and prevention. 
As  forest  fire  suppression  and  prevention  are  integral  components  of  the  project  activities,  the 
occurrence   of   uncontrolled   burning   within   the   project   area   is   assumed   to   be   negligible. 
Consequently, no greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions from such events are expected. 
Emission from water bodies on peatlands is omitted because the contribution is less than 5% of 
total emissions. 
On   peatland,   CO2   and   CH4 emissions are   accounted   for,   while   N2O   emissions   were 
conservatively omitted. 
3.2.2.2 Project scenario map 
The  project  area  is  stratified based  on four land  cover  classes  (secondary  swamp  forest,  wet 
shrubland, open land, estate crops), two peat statuses (peat and non-peat) and one water body 
class. The mapping process involved the following steps: 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
196 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
• Delineation  of different  types  of  land  cover at  the  project  start  date. This  process  is 
described in section 3.1.3.1. 
• Delineation of water bodies that existed prior to the project start date and will not change 
for the entire project period. 
• Delineation of peat vs non-peat areas at the start of the project. 
During  the  field  survey, no  drainage  canals  were  observed  in  the  vicinity  of  the  peat  areas; 
therefore, all peat is assumed to be in an undrained condition. 
The project scenario map is shown in the figure below: 
Figure 47: Master project scenario map

The  project  area  comprises  both  peat  and  non-peat  lands.  However,  only  emissions  from  peat 
areas  are  considered  under  the  WRC  activity.  At  the  start  of  the  project,  the  peat  area  is  in 
undrained condition. The project aims to conserve this area to prevent from drainage. As a result, 
the extent of the peat area is expected to remain unchanged throughout the crediting period. The 
following table  and figure  illustrate  the  anticipated peatland strata changes under  the  project

Site-specific data such as peat depth and water table level will be collected along with secondary 
data  from  IPCC to  measure  the  GHG  emissions  under  the  project  scenario. Land  cover 
classification and change detection using remote sensing imagery will be used to  update  land 
stratification,  specifically  to  distinguish  and  re-stratify  forest  and  non-forest  areas during 
monitoring period.

By tracking annual peat thickness in the project scenario, it is ensured that no GHG emissions are 
accounted for in any parcel within each stratum once the available carbon stock—or peat—has been 
fully depleted. Conservatively, peat is considered depleted when the remaining thickness available 
for decomposition falls below 20 cm.

During the monitoring period, the peatland within the project area is expected to remain unaffected 
by  drainage  or  deforestation. Therefore,  Tier  1  IPCC  default  emission  factors  will  continue  to  be 
applied  to  estimate  carbon  and  greenhouse  gas  (GHG)  emissions  resulting  from  peat  microbial 
decomposition under the project scenario. 
The project will conduct annual monitoring of land cover changes and gather field reports on any 
observed deforestation or canal construction. If such activities are detected near the peatland area, 
site-specific  measurements—such  as  water table  depth or  soil  subsidence—will  be undertaken. 
These measurements will support the development of a more accurate, project-specific dataset for 
emission calculations.

For  ex-ante  calculation,  greenhouse  gas  (GHG)  emission  from  water  bodies  on  peatlands in  the 
project  scenario is estimated to contribute  less  than  5%  of  total  emissions  and  are  therefore 
considered negligible. As such, these emissions are conservatively treated as zero. 
During the monitoring period, area of water bodies on peatland will be monitored and changes will 
be taken into account if it significantly influences project GHG emissions. 
Emission from uncontrolled burning 
Forest  fire control  and  prevention  are  implemented  as  project activities; therefore, uncontrolled 
burning is assumed to be omitted in the ex-ante calculation under project scenario. 
NASA’s FIRMS tool is employed to receive real-time update on potential hotspots. Once the forest 
fire is detected, the burnt area will be mapped and site measurements will be conducted to record 
fire scar, burn scar depth for accurate estimation of GHG emissions. 
Following module VMD0013 (E-BPB), GHG emissions from uncontrolled peat burning is estimated

3.2.2.4.1 Emission from deforestation 
In the project scenario, no planned deforestation is expected to occur, as the project is specifically 
designed  to  conserve  and  protect  the  forest. For  the  purpose  of  ex-ante  projections, zero 
deforestation  is  assumed  in  the  project  scenario. However,  there  remains  a  risk  of  unplanned 
deforestation  caused  by  external  factors  beyond  the  project’s  control. Project  will  continuously 
monitor and report deforestation in every monitoring event. The net carbon stock change as a result 
of deforestation is estimated using the following formula:

The  ex-ante  calculation  conservatively  assumes  no  wood  products  are  produced  so  the carbon 
stock sequestered in wood products is zero. The baseline carbon stock is considered equivalent to 
the sampled carbon stock, while the carbon stock in post-deforestation land use is assumed to be 
zero. Site-specific data will be collected through direct measurements during the monitoring period. 
Ex-ante total GHG emissions as a result of deforestation in the project area within the project period 
is estimated to be 3,360 tCO

3.2.2.4.1 Emission from forest degradation 
According  to  module  VMD0015,  forest  degradation  may  result  from  either  (i)  selective  logging 
within  forest  management  areas  holding  an  FSC  certificate,  or  (ii)  illegal  extraction  of  trees  for 
timber, fuelwood, or charcoal. For the purpose of ex-ante estimation, illegal extraction of trees is 
assumed  to  be  zero.  As  required  by  the  module,  actual  degradation  will  be  assessed  through 
participatory rural appraisals (PRA), conducted every two years during the monitoring period. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
211 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Since  the  project  holds  concession  rights  to  operate  and  conserve  the  forest,  no  permits  will  be 
granted for selective logging within the project area. Consequently, emission from selective logging 
is considered to be zero. 
3.2.2.4.2 Emission from natural disturbance 
According to InaRisk
35
 geoportal data, the risk of earthquake in the project area is relatively low. 
Topographic  analysis  shows  that  98.97%  of  the  project  area  consists  of  gentle  slopes  (<15%), 
making the risk of landslides negligible. There are no active volcanoes in Kalimantan, Indonesia, 
so the risk of volcanic hazard is zero.  
Forest fires are common in Indonesia, particularly during the dry season. To address this risk, the 
project has established Fire Response Units (FRUs) in collaboration with local communities. These 
units  are  permanently  stationed  within  the  project  area  to ensure  timely  suppression  of  any  fire 
outbreaks. As a result, the risk of large-scale forest fires is considered negligible. 
For ex-ante estimation, the net carbon stock change from natural disturbances is assumed to be 
zero.  During  the  monitoring  period,  any  incidents  will  be  documented  on  the  ground  and 
supplemented with remote sensing data, as well as secondary data from external sources such as 
NASA’s FIRMS, to quantify emissions resulting from natural disturbances. 
3.2.2.4.3 Project emissions 
In accordance with Indonesia’s laws and regulations, land clearance by slash-and-burn practices is 
strictly prohibited. To reinforce this, the project conducts regular patrols and deploys Fire Response 
Units (FRUs) to ensure timely suppression of any uncontrolled fires. Consequently, emissions from 
biomass burning are assumed to be zero. 
Additionally, since project activities to protect and conserve the forest do not involve the application 
of fertilizers, emissions from fertilizer use are excluded. 
For  ex-ante  estimation,  GHG  emissions  in  the  project  scenario  are  assumed  to  be  zero.  Any fire 
incidents or emissions that occur during the monitoring period will be recorded and accounted for. 
3.2.2.5 
36
Emissions from ARR activity under the project scenario 
Reforestation activities will be implemented on land that has not been forested in the past ten 
years, through a combination of forest restoration and agroforestry development. 
A total of 220 hectares located in the western part of the project area, adjacent to the residential 
zone,  will  be  designated  for  agroforestry.  Tree species are  selected  to  enhance both  local 
livelihoods  and  environmental  benefits including Havea  brasiliensis, Mangifera  spp (Mango), 
Durio zibethinus (Durian), Macaranga tanarius, Shorea leprosula.

3.2.2.5.1 Non-woody Biomass Carbon Stock Change 
According to the methodology VM0047, v1.1, non-woody biomass is optional so it’s conservative 
to  exclude  it  from  the  calculation.  Therefore,  the non-woody  biomass  carbon  stock  change  is 
considered zero.

trees. For the purpose of ex-ante estimation, the project assumes a 60% survival rate five years 
after planting. The biomass of trees that do not survive is classified as deadwood biomass. The

3.2.3 Leakage Emissions (VCS 2.5, 3.2, 3.6, 3.15, 4.3) 
3.2.3.1 Leakage Emission from REDD+ Activities 
According  to  the  VCS  methodology  VM0007  REDD+  MF,  the  applicable  leakage  modules  are 
determined  based  on  the  specific  project  activities.  As  this  project  involves  a  combination  of 
Avoiding  Planned  Deforestation  (APD)  and  Conservation  of  Undrained  and  Partially  Drained 
Peatland (CUPP), the following leakage modules relevant to these activities are applied: 
•
 
Estimation of emissions from activity shifting for avoiding planned deforestation and planned 
degradation (LK-ASP) 
•
 
Estimation of emissions from market-effects (LK-ME) 
•
 
Estimation of emissions from ecological leakage (LK-ECO) 
3.2.3.1.1 Activity Shifting Leakage 
According to module LK-ASP, where the specific deforestation agent can be identified, leakage does 
not need to be considered where it can be demonstrated that the management plans and/or land-

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
223 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
use designations of the deforestation agent’s other lands have not materially changed as a result 
of  the  project. In  the  context  of  the  KPCP  project,  PT  RAL  was  identified  as  the  primary  agent 
responsible  for  deforestation.  The  company  does  not  hold  any  other  designated  concessions  for 
industrial timber plantations outside of the project area. Following the full acquisition of PT RAL by 
PT IHE, PT IHE has committed to ensuring that no additional timber concessions will be granted to 
or operated by PT RAL in the future. 
Therefore, GHG leakage emissions due to activity shifting (∆퐶
LK-AS,planned
) is set to zero. 
3.2.3.1.2 Market Effects Leakage 
Based  on  the  Industrial  Timber  Plantation’s  management  plan,  PT  RAL  planned  to use Acacia 
and Eucalyptus for wood chips production. Therefore, the project doesn’t reduce the production 
of timber, fuelwood or charcoal at the country level.  
GHG leakage emissions due to market-effects (∆퐶
LK-ME
) is set to zero. 
3.2.3.1.3 Ecological Leakage 
Under Module LK-ECO, it is required that ecological leakage affecting the soil (peat) carbon pool 
does not occur. The project aims to conserve the existing undrained peat areas and prevent any 
degradation,  and  therefore  no  ecological  leakage  is  expected  under  the  project  scenario. 
Throughout the crediting period, ecological leakage will be monitored by systematically observing 
and recording water levels within the project area, as well as tracking land-use changes. 
3.2.3.1.4 Net GHG Emission due to leakage from REDD Activities 
In  summary, it’s  expected  that  no  leakage  related  to  the  implementation  of  Avoiding  Planned 
Deforestation and Conservation of Undrained and Partially Drained Peatland activities will occur. 
Therefore, the ∆퐶
퐿퐾
−
푅퐸퐷퐷	
is set to zero. 
3.2.3.2 Leakage Emission from ARR Activities 
According  to  the  VCS  methodology VM0047,  v1.1, GHG  emissions  due  to  leakage  from  ARR 
activities  must  be  estimated  using  module  VMD0054.  The  project  follows  stepwise  approach 
outlined in the module to determine the leakage emission. 
Step 1: Determine Foregone 
Production in Project Area 
Module VMD0054 requires that the baseline agricultural and 
fuelwood  commodity  production  in  the  project  area  must  be 
demonstrated  using  historical  production  records  for  each 
commodity during the chosen reference period. 
The   project conducted Participatory   Mapping   with   local 
communities and analyzed satellite imagery to delineate land 
use related to commodity cultivation within the project area. 
The  results  confirm  that  palm  oil  is  the  sole  agricultural 
commodity  cultivated  by  local  communities.  Satellite  data 
from 2023 estimates  that  approximately 143 hectares  of 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
224 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
existing palm oil plantations are present within the area. This 
land  has  been  excluded  from  the  eligible  area  for  ARR 
activities,  and  the  project  will  allow  local  communities  to 
continue managing it. 
Additionally, fuelwood is neither cultivated nor 
commercialized. It is sourced from naturally fallen trunks and 
existing   tree   branches.   As   there   is   no   formal   fuelwood 
production,   no   displacement   of   fuelwood   resources   is 
anticipated. 
Therefore,   no   pre-project   agricultural   activities   will   be 
displaced as a result of the ARR project activities. 
Step 2: Determine the Impact 
of Leakage Mitigation Activities 
As  there  is  no  displacement  of agricultural  activities due  to 
the project, no mitigation activities are needed to implement. 
Step 3: Determine Amount of 
New Land Brought into 
Production 
No  new  land  is  expected  to be  brought into  production 
because there is no displacement of agricultural activities due 
to the project. 
Step 4: Determine Change in 
Carbon Stocks in New Lands 
Brought into Production 
As no new land is expected to be brought into production, this 
step is not applicable. 
Step 5: Determine Leakage 
Emissions 
As  no  new  land  is  expected  to  be  brought  into  production, 
there is no leakage emissions. 
Based  on  the  above  analysis,  there  will  be  no  GHG  emissions  resulting  from  leakage  of  ARR 
activities. During the implementation, the project plans to follow the monitoring plan to account 
for any possible agricultural displacement.

3.2.4.1.3 Total REDD and WRC Uncertainty 
Total  project  uncer tainty  is  equal  to  the  combined  uncer tainty  in  baseline  and project estimates 
for the REDD and WRC activities. The allowable uncertainty in this methodology REDD+ MF is +/- 
15% of NER
REDD+
 at the 95% confidence level. Where this precision level is met then no deduction 
should result for uncertainty. Where uncertainty exceeds 15% of NER
REDD+
 at the 95% confidence 
level then the deduction must be equal to the amount that the uncertainty exceeds the allowable 
level. All cumulative uncertainty within 30-year project period is less than 15% so no deduction 
is required. The table below shows the cumulative uncertainty:

The combined  non-permanence  risk  buffer  for  the  project  is  calculated  using  the  AFOLU Non-
Permanence  risk  assessment  tool  v4.2.  It  is  estimated  that  the  overall  risk  rating  is 11.43%. 
However,  according  to the  tool’s  requirement, the  minimum  risk  buffer  must  be  set  at  12% so 
12% is used for risk buffer withholding.

3.2.4.6 Calculation of Verified Carbon Units (VCUs)

uncertainty adjusted net emission reductions for each project activity. The following table shows

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
251 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
and procedures to be 
applied 
The PRA shall evaluate whether the following activities may be 
occurring in the project area: 
• harvesting of fuel wood 
• harvesting of wood for charcoal production 
• timber harvest 
If ≥ 10% of those interviewed/surveyed believe that degradation 
may be occurring within the project boundary then the limited on-
the-ground degradation survey shall be triggered. 
An additional output of the PRA shall be a depth of penetration 
of degradation pressure. A maximum distance shall be recorded 
for penetration into the forest from access points (such as roads, 
rivers, already cleared areas) for the purpose of harvesting fuel 
wood, charcoal and/or timber. It is likely that differing distances 
shall exist for each degradation pressure. If multiple pressures 
exist in the same stratum the deepest depth of penetration shall 
be used to define 퐴
deg,i
 
Frequency of 
monitoring/recording 
Every 2 years

Data / parameter 
C
DegW,I,t
 
Data unit 
t CO
2
-e 
Description 
Biomass carbon of trees cut and removed through illegal logging 
and fuelwood and charcoal extraction degradation process from 
plots measured in stratum i in year t. 
Source of data 
Field measurement 
Description of 
measurement methods 
and procedures to be 
applied 
The diameter of all tree stumps is the designated plots will be 
measured and conservatively assumed to be the same as the 
DBH. If the stump is a large buttress, identify several individuals 
of the same species nearby and determine a ratio of the 
diameter at DBH to the diameter of buttress at the same height 
above ground as the measured stumps. This ratio will be applied 
to the measured stumps to estimate the likely DBH of the cut 
tree. The above and below ground carbon stock of each 
harvested tree must be estimated using the same allometric 
regression equation and root to shoot ratio used in the module 
for estimating the carbon pool in trees (CP-AB) in the baseline 
scenario. See detailed guidance in module CP-AB for 
aboveground biomass estimation. 
Frequency of 
monitoring/recording 
Must be monitored at least every 5 years or if verification occurs 
on a frequency of less than every 5 years examination must 
occur prior to any verification event.

3.3.3 Monitoring Plan (VCS, 3.16, 3.20) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
263 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
3.3.3.1 REDD Monitoring Plan 
The monitoring  plan  for  REDD  activity  is  developed  based  on procedures  from  methodology 
VM0007  and  module  M-REDD  which outline  approach  to  track land  cover changes  and  carbon 
stock changes. 
 
3.3.3.1.1 Spatial Data 
Satellite imagery will be obtained annually to monitor land cover change. The data will be processed 
in 3-step approach as outlined in module M-REDD to calculate relevant parameters: 
Step 1: Selection and analyses of sources of land-use and land-cover (LU/LC) change data 
Medium resolution remotely sensed spatial data (10m x 10m Sentinel-2) covering the project area 
will  be  used. If  remotely  sensed  data  have  become  available  from  new  and  higher resolution 
sources, then  the  source  will  be  changed  to  provide  better  accuracy. Pre-processing including 
geometric  correction  and  geo-referencing  and  cloud  and  shadow  detection and  removal will  be 
conducted before analysis. 
Step 2: Interpretation and analyses 
Remote  sensing  imagery  is  interpreted  using  standardized practices.  To  quantify  deforestation, 
forest  cover  change  is  assessed  between  two  distinct  timeframes. The  accuracy  of  the  Land 
Use/Land  Cover  (LULC)  classification  will  be  validated  using  a  confusion  matrix,  supported  by 
ground-truth  field  assessments.  This  process  will  be  used  to  calculate  overall  classification 
accuracy and the Kappa coefficient. Detailed processing procedure is explained at Annex 2: Land 
Use/Land Cover processing pipeline. 
The deforested area is then calculated by subtracting the forest cover of the later period from that 
of the earlier one. Subsequently, carbon emissions resulting from deforestation are estimated by 
multiplying the deforested area by the average change in carbon stock per hectare. 
According to module M-REDD, forest degradation must be monitored by conducting participatory 
rural appraisal (PRA) of the communities inside and surrounding the project area. The PRA will be 
repeated every 2 years. If the survey result indicates a potential illegal logging, field check will be 
implemented to delineate the degradation area and quantify the biomass loss. Emissions due to 
forest  degradation are  estimated multiplying  the degraded area  by  the  average carbon  loss per 
hectare. 
NASA’s FIRMS tool is used to provide near real-time updates on potential fire activity in the project 
area. All  fire  events  and  natural  disturbances  will  be  documented  and  verified  through  ground 
truthing conducted by the field team. Affected areas will be clearly delineated and incorporated into 
the overall land cover change analysis. 
Step 3: Documentation 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
264 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The sources, processing workflow and output data in Step 1 and Step 2 will be documented clearly 
and archived  in  the  project’s  central  database  prior  to  verification  event.  Data  will  be  made 
available to all involved parties and VCS auditors upon request. 
3.3.3.1.2 Carbon Stock Monitoring 
Changes in carbon stocks of tree biomass will be monitored through field measurements using a 
transect sampling method. Tree diameter, height, and wood density will be recorded and applied 
to allometric equations to estimate biomass. Monitoring of REDD activities will be conducted in all 
permanent  sample  plots  previously  established  for  baseline  development.  These  monitoring 
activities will be carried out at five-year intervals. 
3.3.3.2 WRC Monitoring Plan 
3.3.3.2.1 GHG Emissions from Microbial Decomposition 
The project's peatland areas (defined as having peat depths greater than 50 cm) are situated deep 
within the concession and are characterized by good vegetation cover and relatively stable water 
table  levels (undrained  peat  conditions).  Continuous  monitoring  will  be  conducted  to  assess  the 
condition  of  forest  cover  and  water  table  depths  in  surrounding  areas,  enabling  verification  of 
annual forest integrity and drainage status. Forest cover will be monitored using a combination of 
remote sensing imagery and routine land patrols. Drainage status will be assessed by monitoring 
water levels at key outlet points. Based on annual data on water table depth and forest cover, the 
condition  of  each  stratum  will  be  evaluated.  If  significant  changes  are  detected  in  either  forest 
cover or drainage status, the affected area will be re-stratified accordingly. A new emission factor 
will be developed to reflect the revised characteristics of the updated stratum. 
3.3.3.2.2 GHG Emissions from Peat Burning 
NASA’s FIRMS tool is used to receive near real-time alerts for any potential forest fires. If the forest 
fire is detected within the peatland area, field check will be conducted to delineate  the  affected 
areas, measure burnt scar depth and relevant parameters to calculate the emission. 
3.3.3.2.3 Peat Characteristics 
The  peat’s  inventory  data  including  peat  depth, bulk  density, carbon  content and  relevant 
hydrological parameters will be surveyed and conducted upon baseline scenario update which is 
10-year period. Primary data collected during the survey will be complemented by information from 
relevant scientific literature and research. A new peat depth map and hydrological modeling will be 
developed from these data. 
3.3.3.3 ARR Monitoring Plan 
3.3.3.3.1 Remote Sensing Data 
The project adopts an area-based quantification approach, whereby remote sensing data is used 
to measure stocking indices for both project and control plots. A dedicated database is established 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
265 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
and maintained to support ongoing monitoring. Stocking index data will be updated in the database 
prior to each verification event or every 2 years. The database will include the following information: 
• 
Stocking index description
: Enhanced Vegetation Index (EVI) is used throughout the project 
crediting  period. The  data  is  derived  by  combining  multiple  Sentinel-2’s  bands.  Detailed 
about the EVI calculation is explained at Appendix 4: Performance Benchmark Calculation. 
• 
Project  plots
:  A  list  of  chosen  project  plots including  unique  IDs,  locations,  size,  historical 
stocking index values and stocking index value from t = 0 to time t. 
• 
Control  plots
:  A  list  of  chosen control plots  including  unique  IDs,  locations,  size,  weights, 
historical stocking index values and stocking index value from t = 0 to time t. 
• 
Remote sensing datasets: 
All Sentinel-2 images with captured date used for EVI processing 
are archived.
 
•
 
Plantation data: 
The coordinates of the planted areas are recorded using a GPS device and 
stored in the database.
 
Detailed procedure to monitor and calculate the slopes of stocking indexes follows Step 4, Step 5, 
Step 6 in the Appendix 1: Performance method of methodology VM0047, v1.1. 
3.3.3.3.2 Carbon Stock Monitoring 
In agroforestry and native restoration area, annual plantation activity will be tracked and delineated 
with  GPS  equipment. Prior  to  any  verification  event  or  every  2  years,  the  project  conduct  carbon 
stock measurement  using  point  sampling  method  to  record  tree’s  specifications  (diameter  at 
breast  height,  height, wood  density)  and  apply allometric  equation to estimate the biomass. The 
number of plots will be determined using a systematic approach to ensure accurate representation 
of project activities.  
3.3.3.4 Data Management Process 
The project’s data management process consists of three main stages: data collection and 
storage, data analysis and report. The detailed process is shown in the figure below: 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
266 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Figure 48: Data management process 
 
•
 
Collect   field   data: 
Field   data   measurements   are   recorded   on printed   datasheet. 
Supplementary data such as GPS locations and photos are captured with digital device. 
• 
Process data in digital format
: Data from datasheet is organized and entered into the digital 
worksheet. Hard copies are archived in field office for future reference. 
•
 
Conduct  QC  on  data
: Staff  from  the  head  office  conduct  a  comprehensive  review  of  all 
recorded data. Any missing or inconsistent entries are flagged and reported back to the field 
team  for  verification.  The  field  team  is  responsible  for  correcting  and  updating  the  data  to 
ensure accuracy and completeness. 
•
 
Upload data to central database
: Once the data passes the quality control, it will be uploaded 
into a central database in the cloud. 
•
 
Browse  and  query  data
: Data  analyst  from  the  head office filters  and  pulls data  from cloud 
storage to prepare for analysis and modelling. 
•
 
Aggregate  and  analyse  data
: Inventory  data  is  aggregated  and analysed across  multiple 
dimensions  to  generate  insights  into  forest  structure,  species  distribution,  and  hydrological 
patterns, etc. 
•
 
Carbon stock modelling
: Tree’s measurement data is combined with allometric equations to 
estimate carbon stocks for different stratum within project area. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
•
 
Generate  analytic  reports
: Results  from  the  analytical  stage  are  compiled  and  provided  as 
ground-truth data to support monitoring reports and inform decision-making. 
3.3.3.5 Quality Assurance and Quality Control Process 
Data  quality  and  integrity  are  upheld  through  the  implementation  of  Standard  Operating 
Procedures  (SOPs),  as  well  as  through  independent  review  and  approval  processes.  To  further 
minimize risks that may compromise data quality, the project enforces the following measures: 
• 
Field Work Procedures
 
o Standardized  Datasheets:  All  datasheets  are  formatted  consistently  with  clear  labels, 
defined units, and step-by-step instructions to reduce ambiguity. 
o Instrument   Calibration:   Field   instruments   (e.g.,   GPS   units,   calipers)   are   regularly 
calibrated to ensure precise and reliable measurements. 
o
 
Staff Training: Field personnel receive thorough training to ensure they fully understand 
and correctly apply all SOPs. 
o
 
Pre-Field  Checklists:  Checklists  are  used  to  confirm  that  all  required  equipment  and 
reference materials are prepared before field deployment. 
• 
Digital Data Processing 
o
 
Automated  Validation:  Systems  automatically  detect  errors  such  as  duplicated entries 
and out-of-range values to ensure initial data accuracy. 
o Independent  Review:  Data  collected  in  the  field  is  independently  reviewed  by  data 
analysts at the head office to identify and correct any discrepancies. 
o Approval Process: All analytical results and reports undergo review and formal approval 
by the technical project lead, general manager, and senior officers. 
• 
Issue Management and Continuous Improvement 
o All data-related issues identified are documented in a dedicated QA/QC logbook. 
o Corrective  actions  are  implemented  promptly  to  address  and  rectify  any  identified 
problems. 
o Lessons  learned  are  systematically  used  to  update  SOPs  and  improve  the  overall  data 
management pipeline. 
o When necessary, retraining is provided to field and processing staff to prevent recurrence 
of past issues. 
3.3.3.6 Organizational Structure for Monitoring Plan 
The list of personnel and their respective responsibilities for the implementation and monitoring of 
project activities is presented in section 2.4.1 and 2.4.2.

3.4 Optional Criterion: Climate Change Adaptation Benefits  
3.4.1 Regional Climate Change Scenarios (CCB, GL1.1) 
Global  climate  change  is  intensifying  atmospheric  instability,  leading  to  more  frequent,  longer-
lasting, and more severe extreme weather events across Indonesia. This has resulted in a marked 
rise in hydrometeorological disasters with significant social and economic consequences. 
Temperature 
Annual  average  temperature  rise  for  Indonesia is  expected  to  be  3.4°C under  the  RCP8.5 
emissions  pathway  by  the  2080s. Projections  suggest  that  ambient  temperatures,  currently 
averaging around 26.5 °C, will rise to approximately 29–30 °C. This warming trend is expected 
to  substantially  increase  the  frequency  of  days  with  maximum  temperatures  exceeding  30  °C. 
The  rate  of  warming  is  likely  to  be  more  pronounced  in  Indonesia’s  inland  regions.  In  Central 
Kalimantan,  where  KPCP  is  located,  temperatures  under  the  RCP8.5  scenario  are  projected  to 
rise by nearly 4 °C by the end of the century
38
. 
Figure 43: Historic and projected average annual temperature in Indonesia under two emissions 
pathways estimated by the model ensemble 
 
 
38
 World Bank Group, and Asian Development Bank. Climate Risk Country Profile: Indonesia. 
https://climateknowledgeportal.worldbank.org/sites/default/files/2021-05/15504-Indonesia%20Country%20Profile-WEB_0.pdf 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4

Blue  depicts  RCP2.6.  The  solid  line  represents  the  median  of  the  model  ensemble  with  the 
shaded areas showing the 10th–90th percentile. 
Rainfall 
Indonesia’s rainfall regime is governed by its equatorial position, which produces two dominant 
monsoon seasons: the dry season (from June to August) and the rainy season (from December 
to  February).  In  Central  Kalimantan,  rainfall  trends  from  1991–2020  show  a  predominantly 
increasing pattern, with annual precipitation increase by up to 25 mm/year across much of the 
province
39
. 
Figure 43: 1991-2020 rainfall trend, with details: (a) annual trends (b) seasonal trends 
(December-January-February), and (c) seasonal trends (June-July-August). Rate of change is 
depicted with gradient color. 
 
Natural hazards  
Indonesia  faces  a  growing  range  of  climate-related  hazards  that  are  expected  to  intensify 
significantly throughout the 21
st
 century due to climate change. One of the most pressing threats 
is  the  rise  in  extreme  heatwaves.  With  an  already  high  baseline  of  temperatures — averaging 
monthly maximums around 30.6°C — the country is projected to see a dramatic increase in the 
 
39
 BMKG. 2024. KLIMA: At the Front Line of Climate Action. Edisi X 2024. Jakarta: Deputi Bidang Klimatologi BMKG 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
270 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
number of dangerously hot days. Under high emissions scenarios such as RCP8.5, days with a 
Heat Index exceeding 35°C—a level considered hazardous to human health—could occur almost 
every day by the end of the century
40
. Studies suggest that Indonesia could experience extreme 
heatwaves as frequently as once every two years by 2100, posing serious risks to public health, 
productivity, and infrastructure
41
. 
In addition to heat, drought and fire risk are expected to worsen. Indonesia currently faces a 4% 
annual probability of severe meteorological drought (as defined by the SPEI index), but this could 
double  to  around  9%  under  RCP2.6  and  RCP8.5  pathways.  These droughts  may  become  more 
intense and frequent due to the rising incidence of El Niño events, which are known to reduce 
rainfall and increase temperature extremes
42
. 
Flooding  is  another  escalating  hazard.  As  of  2010,  river  flooding  already  affected  about 6.1 
million  people  annually  and  caused  $4.9 billion  in  urban  damage.  Climate  change  and  rapid 
urban  development  are  expected  to  increase  these  figures  substantially.  By  the  2030s,  the 
number of people affected could rise to  8.7 million people annually, with urban damage costs 
increasing to $22 billion under high emissions scenarios
43
. 
Potential land use change 
Climate change is expected to significantly reshape land-use patterns across Indonesia, including 
the  project  area.  Rising  temperatures  and  shifting  rainfall  patterns  are  likely  to  prolong  dry 
seasons and intensify droughts, leading to the drying of peatlands and reduced forest moisture. 
These conditions will make forests increasingly susceptible to fires. As peat fires become more 
frequent  and  severe,  forest  degradation  may  accelerate, whereas, some  currently  productive 
lands could become unsuitable due to water scarcity and ecosystem decline. At the same time, 
more  frequent  flooding  may  render  certain  agricultural  lands  less  viable,  driving  a  transition 
toward crops that are better adapted to flood conditions. 
3.4.2 Climate Change Impacts (CCB, GL1.2) 
Community wellbeing 
Agricultural systems in Central Kalimantan are highly vulnerable to climate variability and long-
term climatic change. Observations and projections indicate that shifts in rainfall patterns, rising 
temperatures, and more frequent extremes are already undermining productivity, food security, 
and  rural  livelihoods. Spatial  vulnerability  assessments  identify  at  least  14  districts  across 
Kalimantan  as  having  “High”  to  “Very  High”  levels  of  agricultural  vulnerability  under  climate

Figure 49: Map of food farming vulnerability in Kalimantan 
 
Communities  in  Central  Kalimantan including  the  project  area face  overlapping  health  and 
livelihood   pressures   as   climate   change   intensifies   environmental   hazards   and   economic 
uncertainty. Increasingly frequent and severe peatland and forest fires exemplify this dual threat. 
These fires disrupt economic activity, reduce agricultural output, and impose high health costs 
on  households.  The  catastrophic  fire  season  of  2019  forced  airport  closures,  disrupted  trade, 
and diminished tourism, while the resulting haze was estimated to cause more than 3,200 excess 
deaths in Central Kalimantan alone and over 87,000 nationwide due to fine particulate (PM₂.₅) 
exposure
45
. Such recurrent fire disasters are expected to worsen under prolonged droughts and 
rising temperatures, directly undermining both rural economies and public health
46
. 
The  health  burden  of  climate  change  extends  beyond  haze-related  mortality.  Fire  seasons  are 
consistently associated with spikes in hospital admissions for respiratory infections, asthma, and 
cardiovascular complications, with infants, children, and the elderly particularly vulnerable
47
. In 
addition  to  acute  illness,  haze  exposure  contributes  to  long-term  reductions  in  life  expectancy. 
Climate  variability  also  influences  infectious  disease  dynamics,  as  warmer  temperatures, 
humidity, and erratic rainfall create favorable conditions for mosquito breeding and expand the

risks of vector-borne diseases such as dengue and malaria. Studies in Kalimantan confirm that 
dengue  incidence  is  closely  tied  to  climate  anomalies,  while  pneumonia  cases  have  also  been 
correlated with fluctuations in temperature and humidity
48
 
49
. 
Without the project’s activities to restore ecosystems, support alternative livelihoods, and prevent 
fire  hazards,  communities  within  the  project  zone  would  experience  worsening  impacts  from 
climate  change.  Shifts  in  rainfall  patterns,  rising  temperatures, and  more  frequent  extreme 
weathers would  continue  to  undermine  agriculture,  leading  to  more  frequent  crop  failures, 
reduced food security, and declining household incomes. In the absence of fire prevention and 
ecosystem management, prolonged droughts would leave peat soils highly flammable, resulting 
in uncontrolled fires that destroy ecosystem, disrupt local economies, and create hazardous haze. 
Such  events  would  increase  health  risks  for  residents,  with  greater  incidence  of  respiratory 
illness, cardiovascular complications, and vulnerability among children and the elderly. 
Biodiversity conservation status 
Climate change is placing growing pressure on biodiversity across Indonesia. Central Kalimantan, 
where  the  project  is  located,  is  particularly  vulnerable  due  to  its  vast  peatlands  and  lowland 
rainforests, which are highly sensitive to climate-related impacts. Rising temperatures combined 
with declining wet-season rainfall and more frequent extreme events, are altering the ecological 
balance  of  key  habitats. Drying  peat  soils  and  prolonged  droughts  accelerate  peat  subsidence 
and increase the likelihood of fires, which are among the most destructive threats to biodiversity 
in  the  region.  The  catastrophic  El  Niño  droughts  demonstrated  the  scale  of  this  risk:  peatland 
fires  burned  vast  areas  of  forest,  killed  thousands  of  animals,  and  caused  lasting  ecosystem 
degradation
50
 
51
. 
Rainfall variability and altered temperature regimes are disrupting flowering and fruiting cycles 
in   dipterocarp   forests,   with   consequences   for   food-dependent   fauna   such   as Bornean 
Orangutans,   hornbills,   and   gibbons
52
.   Amphibians   and   reptiles,   which   are   sensitive   to 
hydrological  changes,  are  at  particular  risk  as  wetlands  dry  and  disease  pressures  increase
53
. 
Climate projections suggest that by 2050, suitable climate envelopes for many lowland species 
will  shrink  drastically,  with  more  than  60  percent  of  lowland  protected  areas  in  Borneo  losing 
their  current  climatic  conditions
54
.  Fragmentation  from  agriculture  and  plantations  prevents 
species from shifting their ranges, thereby increasing extinction risk. Globally significant species 
(critically  endangered),  such  as  the Bornean Orangutan  (Pongo  pygmaeus),  Sunda  Pangolin

Without  the  project’s presence,  biodiversity  within  the  project  zone  would  face  accelerating 
decline  under  intensifying  climate  change.  Rising  temperatures,  declining  wet-season  rainfall, 
and more frequent droughts would dry out peat soils and alter natural ecosystem, leaving them 
increasingly prone to uncontrolled fires. In the absence of intervention, such fires would destroy 
habitat,  prevent  natural  regeneration,  and  convert  forests  into  degraded shrubland with  much 
lower  biodiversity.  These  conditions  would  place  severe  stress  on  the  project  zone’s  critically 
endangered  species  by  reducing  available  food  resources,  fragmenting  their  habitats,  and 
heightening direct mortality risk. 
3.4.3 Measures Needed and Designed for Adaptation (CCB, GL1.3) 
The project area and surrounding zone are highly vulnerable to the potential impacts of climate 
change, which pose significant risks to both community wellbeing and biodiversity conservation. 
To  address  these  challenges,  the  project  implements  a  range  of  targeted  activities (details  in 
section 2.1.17) aimed  at  climate  change  mitigation.  A  summary  of  the  anticipated  climate 
benefits is provided below.
