4 COMMUNITY 
4.1 Without-Project Community Scenario  
4.1.1 Descriptions of Communities at Project Start (CCB, CM1.1) 
The project area for KPCP carbon project is located in Central Kalimantan Province, encompassing 
parts of Kapuas Regency and Pulang Pisau Regency. The project area covered eight villages that 
are within PT Ramang Agro Lestari's concession boundary: Humbang Raya, Hanua, Hurung, Lawang 
Uru,  Manen  Kaleka,  Manen  Paduran,  Bereng  Rambang,  and  Parahangan.  These  villages  are 
considered  key  social  units  due  to  their  geographic  proximity,  historical  land  use,  and  socio-
economic interdependence with the surrounding forest ecosystems.  
A   comprehensive   community   socio-economic   assessment,   social   baseline   study and   High 
Conservation Value (HCV) assessment were conducted to collect relevant data. The study adopted 
a  mixed-methods  approach,  combining  primary  and  secondary  sources,  observations,  map 
analysis, and expert consultations, and integrating both qualitative and quantitative information. 
Ethical  principles  guided  all  stages  of  the  assessment,  with  emphasis  on  informed  consent, 
confidentiality and data protection, cultural sensitivity, avoidance of harm, and transparency at the 
community level. 
The information presented in this section is derived from these assessments.  
4.1.1.1 Demographic 
Gender and Age Distribution 
The total population across the eight villages is 6,036 individuals, consiting of 3,161 male (52.4%) 
and 2,875 female (47.6%) 
55
. Overall, the male population of the project zone slightly outnumbers 
the female population; however, the gender ratio remains fairly balanced, with the male-to-female 
ratio close to 1.0. 
Based on the social assessments, the community is largely dominated by the productive age group 
(19 to 60 years). The prevalence of the productive age group serves as a key indicator for planning 
social and economic interventions, highlighting a strong potential for empowerment through skills 
training, business support, and active participation in community activities and local institutional 
strengthening.

Household Composition 
The average household consists of about four people, a moderate size for rural or semi-rural areas 
like  KPCP’s  operational  zone.  This  reflects  the  dominance  of  nuclear  families,  though  extended 
members  such  as  grandparents  or  other  relatives  may  also  live together.  Household  sizes  vary: 
33% have four members, the largest group; 22% have three; 19% have five; 16% have two; and 
10% have either 1, 6, or 7 members. Most households (74%) fall within the range of three to five 
members. While nuclear families are predominant, the presence of extended family members, such 
as grandparents or other relatives living in the same household, is still possible. 
4.1.1.2 Community Characteristic 
Ethnicity and Language 
The population in the eight villages (Humbang Raya, Hanua, Hurung, Lawang Uru, Manen Kaleka, 
Manen Paduran, Bereng Rambang, and Parahangan) predominantly consists of indigenous Dayak 
Ngaju communities. Over time, other ethnic groups, such as Javanese, Madurese, and Banjar, have 
settled  in  Kalimantan  and  become  closely  integrated  with  the  Dayak  community's  values  and 
culture. The Dayak Ngaju language is generally used as the primary means of communication in 
daily life, including both informal interactions and formal gatherings. 
Religion and Spiritual Practices 
The communities in the eight villages primarily adhere to various religions, with a strong presence 
of  Protestant  Christianity,  specifically  affiliated  with  the  Evangelical  Church  of  Kalimantan.  A 
smaller portion of the community practices Islam, Roman Catholicism, and Hinduism. This religious 
diversity is accompanied by a high level of religious tolerance and respect for different faiths among 
the Dayak Ngaju community.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

The villagers continue to uphold various traditional rituals rooted in Kaharingan beliefs, which play 
an important role in their spiritual and communal life. The occurrence of these ceremonies varies, 
depending on the specific needs and circumstances of each Dayak Kaharingan community.

Traditional Authority 
The traditional authority of the villagers is deeply rooted in the indigenous Dayak Ngaju community's 
cultural  practices  and  the  Kaharingan  belief  system.  Traditional  authority  in  the  community  is 
upheld  through  customary  institutions  and  influential  leaders  such  as  mantir  and  damang,  who 
guide decision-making and play a central role in governance. Village elders are respected for their 
knowledge,  particularly  regarding  the  forest,  and  contribute  to  preserving  traditional  values. 
Customary  law  continues  to regulate  aspects  of  land ownership,  with  initiatives  underway  to 
integrate it into legal literacy programs. 
The Kaharingan religion remains central to spiritual life, with rituals such as Tiwah, Pakanan Sahur 
Lewu,  Mamapas  Lewu,  Menyanggar,  and  Badewa  practiced  widely  as  expressions  of  ancestral 
heritage.  Sacred  sites,  including  Sandung,  Patahu,  and  Pasah  Kamanthuhu,  hold  cultural  and 
spiritual significance as spaces for rituals, protection, and interaction with guardian spirits. These 
practices  and  institutions  collectively  reinforce  the  community’s  cultural  identity,  governance 
structures, and connection to their ancestral traditions. 
Overall,  traditional  authority,  manifested  through  customary  institutions,  respected  leaders, 
traditional laws, and spiritual practices, remains a fundamental component of the social fabric in 
these Dayak Ngaju villages.

CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
 
Figure 51: Sandung in Manen Kaleka (left) and in Bereng Rambang (right) 
 
4.1.1.3 Wellbeing 
Environment and Forest Dependence 
The Dayak Ngaju, like other Dayak communities, maintain a deep cultural and spiritual bond with 
nature,  relying  heavily  on  natural  resources  for  their  livelihoods.  Nature  is  valued  not  only  for  its 
economic benefits but as a heritage to be preserved, ensuring the balance between humans, the 
environment, and spiritual beliefs. Forest protection is regarded as essential to sustaining life, as 
cultural traditions and ancestral values are rooted in the natural world. The forest is considered a 
vital  stabilizing force, supporting  human  well-being,  preserving  culture,  providing  oxygen,  and 
serving as a key source of livelihood. 
Livelihood 
The most prevalent livelihood across the villages within the KPCP project zone is traditional gold 
mining, involving more than 30% of the population and 67.9% of household heads according to our 
social baseline survey. This sector is dominant in areas like Bereng Rambang (55%), Parahangan 
(47%), and Hanua (36%). This represents a transition from historical mainstays like rubber tapping 
and smallholder agriculture, which began in the early 1990s due to falling rubber prices. While gold 
mining  can  offer  substantial  short-term  financial  gains  (up  to  50  grams  per  drum  of  processed 
material initially), it exposes households to long-term risks like income volatility and environmental 
degradation.  
Aside gold mining, agriculture and plantation cultivation are also a popular way of livelihood. This 
includes rubber tapping (historically primary, now supplementary due to low prices) and emerging 
independent   oil   palm; some   households   also   grow   fruit   trees   and   horticultural   crops   for 
subsistence.  Pig  farming  was  once  a  common source of  household  income  but  collapsed  during 
the COVID-19 pandemic from 2020 to 2022 due to widespread livestock deaths. Only families who 
still have sufficient capital have been able to continue their pig farming activities. Recently, there 
is strong potential for pig farming development. This is supported by the regional governments of 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
281 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Pulang  Pisau  and  Kapuas,  who  plan  to  develop  the  area  as  one  of  the  pork  supply  centers  for 
Palangka Raya. 
Other informal occupations include sand miners (22% in Humbang Raya), carpenters, and various 
Micro, Small, and Medium Enterprises (MSMEs) like trading businesses and food stalls. A smaller 
percentage   of   residents   work   as   civil   servants   (e.g.,   teachers,   healthcare   staff,   village 
administration   personnel),   concentrated   in   Lawang   Uru   (20%)   and   Manen   Kaleka   (20%). 
Meanwhile, the presence of palm oil and industrial timber plantation workers are relatively small 
in comparison to other occupations.  
A  notable  proportion  of  female  respondents,  particularly  in  Lawang  Uru  (56%),  identified  as  full-
time  housewives,  indicating  low  female  labor  force  participation  and  heavy  reliance  on  the 
household head for income. Some wives engage in secondary jobs like agriculture or small-scale 
trading to supplement household income.  
Amongst all, unemployment is also an issue with numerous residences, where 9% of the population 
generally reported having no employment. Manen Kaleka shows the highest unemployment rate at 
27%.  The  heavy  reliance  on  seasonal  and  informal  sectors  like  small-scale  gold  mining,  which 
depends  on  dwindling  natural  resources,  creates  significant  economic  fragility  and  limited  job 
security. Although secondary occupations exist (farming, fishing, small-scale trade), they are often 
small-scale and informal, making the employment structure largely vulnerable. 
Economy
 
The average monthly household income across seven villages is Rp3,000,000, with a range from 
Rp2,500,000  to  Rp7,000,000.  This  average  income  generally  falls  below  the  average  monthly 
household expenditure of approximately Rp3,562,953, creating a monthly deficit for most villages. 
Bereng  Rambang  is  an  exception,  reporting  a  higher  average  income  (Rp7,000,000)  than 
expenditure (Rp4,875,449). Over 70% of surveyed households across all villages earn below their 
respective  Regional  Minimum  Wage  (UMK),  placing  the  majority  of  rural  families  in  a  state  of 
working  poverty.  In  vulnerable  locations  such  as  Parahangan,  more  than 94%  of  households  fall 
under the UMK threshold, with 26% reporting no income and 53% relying on unstable sources of 
income.  The  share  of  households  earning  above  Rp4.5  million/month  is  minimal,  with  very  few 
reporting earnings above Rp6.5 million, underscoring limited access to formal, high-paying sectors. 
This widespread income shortfall, combined with high volatility and minimal access to the upper 
income tiers, reflects a structurally fragile rural economy. 
Traditional  gold  mining  is  the  primary  source  of  income  for  most  household  heads,  with  67.9% 
identifying  it  as  their  main  occupation.  Average  monthly  income  for  a  gold  miner  (based  on  20 
working  days)  is  estimated  between  Rp2,000,000 – Rp3,500,000  per  worker. While  historically 
offering  substantial  short-term  gains  (e.g.,  up  to  50  grams  of  gold  per  drum),  yields  are  now 
declining and becoming more unpredictable, leading to unstable income for 39.42% of gold miners. 
Rubber tapping, though once primary, now provides supplementary income, estimated at around 
Rp3,000,000 per month for 300 kg. Low rubber prices have even led to land being sold or leased 
for gold mining. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
282 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The economic contribution of spouses, primarily wives, remains limited, with the majority (69.2%) 
serving  as  full-time  housewives  without  involvement  in  income-generating  sectors.  Specifically, 
22%  of  female  respondents  were  housewives  without  income. In  Lawang  Uru,  over  50%  of 
respondents identified as full-time housewives, pointing to low female labor force participation and 
potentially entrenched gender norms. Some wives do engage in secondary jobs like agriculture or 
small-scale trading to supplement income (approximately 8% of spouses). This imbalance means 
the  family's  economic  contribution  heavily  relies  on  the  household  head,  narrowing  the  family’s 
economic adaptability when the primary income is disrupted.  
Education 
Educational attainment across the project area varies widely, with access limited by economic and 
geographic barriers. Most villagers have completed junior or senior high school, though some have 
not finished primary education. Senior high school graduation is the most common level achieved 
(19.48% of both men and women), while 7.45% of men and 14.90% of women completed junior 
high,  and  14.3%  of  men  and  10.32%  of  women  finished  only  elementary  school.  Hanua  Village 
records  the  highest  proportion  of  senior  high  graduates  (57%),  while  Bereng  Rambang  has  the 
highest share of residents with only primary (23%) or junior high education (33%). Higher education 
is rare, with 4% of men and 2% of women completing university, and an additional 1% of men and 
3%  of  women holding  diplomas. A small  proportion  (up  to  13%  in  some  villages)  has no  formal 
education. 
While elementary schools are present in all villages, junior high schools exist only in Hanua, Lawang 
Uru, and Humbang Raya, and senior high school only in Hanua. Students from other villages, such 
as  Manen  Kaleka,  Manen  Paduran,  Bereng  Rambang,  and  Parahangan,  often  need  to  travel  to 
Lawang  Uru  or  Hanua  for  junior  high;  and  students  from  the  other  seven  villages  must  travel  to 
Hanua to access senior high school education. The long distance to school systematically creates 
barriers  to  continuing education, especially for those from  low-income  households  or  those  with 
limited transportation options. These challenges, combined with economic hardship, lead many to 
leave school early, often for informal work such as gold mining. The absence of local vocational or 
alternative education options heightens dropout risks for students with low academic engagement 
or economic pressure to work. Early marriage, found among 28% of women before age 19, further 
disrupts  education,  steering  young  women  toward  domestic  roles  and  reducing  future  economic 
and community participation opportunities. 
Health and Medical Condition 
Overall, the health condition of residents reflects a relatively low incidence of seasonal illnesses 
such as acute respiratory infections and diarrhea, though certain diseases remain common. Among 
adults, high blood pressure is the most prevalent condition, affecting 35% of respondents in our 
survey, with the highest incidence in Lawang Uru, Manen Paduran, and Humbang Raya. Diarrhea 
follows  closely,  reported  by  31%  of  adults,  particularly  in  Lawang  Uru  and  Humbang  Raya,  while 
back  pain  and  osteoporosis  account  for  11%  of  cases,  most  notably  in  Bereng  Rambang.  Other 
adult health issues include asthma and upper respiratory tract infections (7% each), skin diseases 
(3%), and a small proportion of other conditions such as diabetes, kidney stones, and gastric acid 
problems.  Among  children  (aged  7–21),  diarrhea  emerges  as  the  most  serious  health  concern, 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
283 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
affecting 75% of respondents, followed by asthma (10%), high blood pressure (8%), and smaller 
proportions with skin diseases, and back pain. 
Healthcare facilities are generally available across villages, including Posyandu Balita (integrated 
health  services  for  toddlers),  Posyandu  Lansia  (for  the  elderly),  auxiliary  health  centers  (PUSTU), 
and  community  health  centers  (Puskesmas),  with  most  PUSTUs  staffed  by  a  nurse  and  midwife. 
Access,  however,  varies  by  location: 58%  of the  respondents (including those  in Lawang  Uru, 
Humbang Raya, Bereng Rambang, Hanua, and Manen Paduran) report in our baseline survey that 
they  have relatively  easy  access to  healthcare  facilities, though some forest-edge  settlements  in 
Humbang Raya still face certain challenges. About 29% of the respondents (particularly those in 
Parahangan) have  moderate  access,  while 13%,  especially  those  in Manen  Kaleka,  experienced 
difficult  access,  as  residents  in  riverbank  areas  must  cross  the  river  to  reach  facilities  near  the 
provincial road. 
  
Figure 52: Health facilities in Humbang Raya Village (left), and Manen Paduran Village (right) 
 
  
General Health & Environmental Sanitation 
Villages  in  the  project  area  generally  demonstrate  organized  settlement  patterns,  with  orderly 
housing  layouts,  spacious  yards,  and  well-ventilated  homes  that  contribute  to  healthier  living 
conditions.  Most  households  have  access  to  toilets,  an  important  indicator  of  public  health,  but 
sanitation   challenges   remain   in   non-permanent   housing.   In   parts   of   Humbang   Raya   and 
Parahangan, some households still practice open defecation in rivers or rely on makeshift shared 
river  toilets,  practices  linked  to  poverty  and  limited  infrastructure  that  increase  the  risk  of 
waterborne diseases. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
284 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Figure 53: Condition of the settlement area in Humbang Raya Village 
 
 
Figure 54: Houses in Hanua Village (left) and Humbang Raya Village (right) 
 
 
Access to clean water is relatively good, with drilled and shallow wells serving as the primary sources 
for  daily activities in  most  villages.  These  are  the  primary  sources  of  clean  water  for  consumption 
and  sanitation  (bathing,  washing,  and  toilet  use)  in  most  villages.  Parahangan  Village  also  utilizes 
shallow wells and gallon-sized bottled water for these purposes. Some communities, such as those 
in Bereng Basuran Hamlet (part of Humbang Raya Village), still use rivers and rainwater harvesting 
for their water needs. In Hanua Village, some residents continue to rely on river water for bathing, 
washing,  and  sanitation  purposes.  While  drilled  wells  provide  a  strong  foundation  for  safe  water 
access,  the  continued  reliance  on  rivers  for  daily  needs  and  the  persistence  of  open  defecation 
highlight gaps in achieving universal access to safe water and sanitation across all villages. 
4.1.2 Interactions between Communities and Community Groups (VCS, 3.19; CCB, 
CM1.1) 
Community  interaction  in  the  project  area  reflects  strong  traditional  cohesion,  evolving  social 
structures shaped by economic change, and varied responses to external influences. The population 
is  predominantly  Dayak  Ngaju,  with  the  Dayak  Ngaju  language  widely  spoken  and  reinforcing  a 
shared cultural identity. Despite religious diversity, including Protestant Christianity, Islam, Roman 
Catholicism, and Hinduism, relations remain tolerant and respectful, supported by the presence of 
worship facilities that foster harmonious coexistence. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
285 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Cultural  identity  is  actively  sustained  through  Kaharingan-based  rituals  such  as  Tiwah,  Pakanan 
Sahur  Lewu,  Mamapas  Lewu,  Menyanggar,  and  Badewa,  which  link  spirituality,  nature,  and 
community  well-being.  These  practices  strengthen  social  bonds  while  reflecting  the  Dayak  Ngaju 
worldview that regards forests not merely as economic resources but as integral to culture, livelihood, 
and resilience. 
Traditional  authority  continues  to  play  a  central  role  in  governance  and  social  organization. 
Customary laws and practices, guided by leaders (such as mantir and damang) provide frameworks 
for  land  management,  conflict  resolution,  and  community  decision-making.  Village  heads  and 
informal  leaders  are  widely  respected  for  mediating  disputes  and  guiding  collective  choices, 
reflecting a reliance on customary institutions and participatory mechanisms to maintain harmony 
and accountability. Together, these cultural traditions, spiritual practices, and governance structures 
form the foundation of social interaction and collective resilience in the community. 
4.1.3 High Conservation Values (CCB, CM1.2) 
KPCP aims to protect and enhance the quality of High Conservation Values (HCVs) that support 
community well-being within the project zone. Based on field surveys, community consultations, 
and spatial analysis following the Common Guidance for the Identification of High Conservation 
Values  (HCVN,  2019
56
) and the HCV-HCSA Assessment Manual (2023
57
), the project identified 
areas that provide vital ecosystem services to communities (HCV 4), resources that sustain local 
livelihoods  (HCV 5),  and culturally  significant  sites  that  uphold  the  Dayak  Ngaju  people’s 
traditional identity (HCV 6). Further  details  about  these  HCVs  are  presented  in  the  subsections 
below. 
4.1.3.1 HCV 4 - Ecosystem services 
 
High Conservation 
Value 
HCV 4 – Ecosystem Services  
Qualifying Attribute 
Areas  that  are  important  for  water  catchment  protection,  flood 
regulation,  erosion  control,  fire  prevention,  and  pollination. In  the 
project areas, these include ecosystems or forests that: 
• Prevent destructive floods and fires 
• Protect vulnerable land and fisheries 
• Provide  windbreaks  and  maintain  moisture,  rainfall,  and 
other climatic conditions 
• Ensure clean water availability 
• Support pollination processes

4.1.3.2 HCV 5 – Community needs 
 
High Conservation 
Value 
HCV 5 – Community needs  
Qualifying Attribute 
Sites and resources that are essential for meeting the basic needs of 
local   communities,   particularly   in   situations   where   no   realistic 
alternatives exist. In the project area, rivers and surrounding habitats 
serve  as  primary  sources  of  fish  (a  key  protein  source). These 
resources   are   critical   for   food   security,   livelihoods,   and   daily 
subsistence. 
Focal Area 
Within KPCP’s project area, a 50-meter buffer zone on both sides of 
Mangkutub, Hai, and Kaliwang Rivers
 
has been designated as HCV 5 
(see 
Figure 60
).  These  riparian  zones  are  vital  for  sustaining  fish 
populations, protecting water quality, and maintaining the ecosystem 
services that support community nutrition and livelihoods, especially 
for residents of Humbang Raya Village and surrounding settlements.

4.1.3.3 HCV 6 – Cultural values 
 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
290 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
High Conservation 
Value 
HCV 6 – Cultural values  
Qualifying Attribute 
Areas  of  critical  cultural,  spiritual,  or  historical  importance  to  local 
communities,  particularly  those  linked  to  traditional  beliefs  and 
practices. In the project area, this includes sacred cultural heritage 
sites  of  the  Dayak  Ngaju  people,  such  as  Sandung  (bone  storage 
structures  used  in  the  Tiwah  ceremony),  Patahu  (ritual  houses  or 
sacred stones used for offerings), and Pasah Kamantuhu (places of 
worship  believed  to  possess  spiritual  power).  These  sites  embody 
deep ancestral connections, spiritual significance, and play a central 
role in community identity and traditional customs. 
Focal Area 
The focal areas for HCV 6 are the designated sacred sites within and 
around the PT RAL concession, located across 8 villages, with a 100-
meter buffer zone surrounding each site (see 
Figure 61
).  These  buffers  ensure  the  protection  of  both  the  physical 
structures   and   their   surrounding   sacred   spaces,   preserving   the 
cultural integrity and spiritual functions of the sites.
 
 
Figure 61: HCV 6 – Cultural values 
 
4.1.4 Without-Project Scenario: Community (CCB, CM1.3) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
291 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
In  the  absence  of  the  project,  communities  within  the  concession would  have  been  directly 
affected by the planned conversion of the concession into an Industrial Timber Plantation (HTI), 
as authorized by the 2017 IUPHHK-HTI decree and the 2019–2028 Business Work Plan. Since 
this plan permitted large-scale clearing for timber plantations, alongside allocations for palm oil 
and  other  estate  crops, it would  progressively  reduce  community  access  to  forests  and  non-
timber forest products. In the long run, this would not only undermine traditional livelihoods but 
also weaken cultural and spiritual connections tied to customary lands. 
Economically, while some  households  may  have  found  short-term  employment  in  plantation 
operations  such  as  land  clearing,  planting,  and  maintenance,  these  jobs  would  have  offered 
limited skills development, low wages, and no pathway for long-term livelihood security. Benefits 
would  also  have  been  concentrated  in  villages  near  plantation  sites,  exacerbating  inequalities 
across the project area. In addition, many households would have continued to rely on traditional 
gold mining, which has become increasingly unstable and unsustainable due to declining yields 
and high-income volatility. Gender disparities would likely persist, with women concentrated in 
unpaid domestic roles or informal, low-income activities, leaving households heavily dependent 
on male breadwinners. 
Social  services  would  also  have  remained  stagnant  under  the Industrial  Timber  Plantation 
scenario.  The  original  management  plan  contained  no  provisions  for  improving  access  to 
education  or  healthcare.  High  school  dropout  rates—already  widespread  due  to  financial 
constraints and travel distances—would likely have persisted, with few opportunities for youth to 
pursue  higher  education  or  vocational  skills.  Health  conditions,  marked  by the  prevalence  of 
preventable illnesses and unequal access to healthcare facilities, would similarly have seen little 
improvement, while economic pressures could deepen risks of malnutrition, child marriage, and 
other vulnerabilities. 
Environmental degradation would have compounded these challenges. Clearing and drainage of 
peatlands  for  timber  plantations  and  estate  crops  would  have  heightened  fire  and  flood  risks, 
reduced water quality, and undermined local food security by damaging fisheries and small-scale 
agriculture.  Meanwhile,  traditional  gold  mining  activities  would  have  continued  contributing  to 
land and water degradation, increasing health risks for households reliant on these resources. 
Culturally, without protection, customary lands and sacred sites would face progressive erosion 
from land conversion and external pressures. This would weaken the transmission of traditional 
knowledge, diminish local authority of customary leaders, and erode social cohesion. 
4.2 Net Positive Community Impacts  
4.2.1 Expected Community Impacts (CCB, CM2.1) 
As described in section 4.1.1.2 above, the project area is predominantly inhabited by the Dayak 
Ngaju  community,  with  broadly  shared  cultural  values  and  traditions.  However,  within  this 
relatively  homogenous  social  fabric, there  is  a clear  differentiation  in  livelihoods,  particularly 
between households engaged in traditional gold mining, those working as smallholder farmers 
and estate crop workers, and the wider village population in general that depends on community-

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
292 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
level   services   and   environmental   stability. The   identification   of   these   groups   and   their 
perspectives  was  informed  through  household  surveys, in-depth  interviews,  and Focus  Group 
Discussions (FGDs) conducted during the baseline and FPIC processes. 
As three community groups above represent  the  main  socioeconomic  structures  within  the 
concession  area,  they  will  be  analyzed  in  detail  in  this  section. Each  group  is expected  to 
experience distinct types of impacts from the proposed project activities,

Change in well-being 
Overall, the project is expected to generate positive and lasting 
well-being  impacts  for  households  currently  reliant  on  gold 
mining.   While   short-term   income   may   decline   as   mining 
activities are reduced, the combination of alternative livelihood 
options and targeted   financial   support   is   expected   to 
strengthen    economic    security    and    reduce    vulnerability. 
Employment  opportunities  in  project  activities  will  provide 
immediate cash income, while agroforestry systems will create 
long-term   revenue   streams.   In   addition,   improvements   in 
environmental  conditions  and  health  (from  reduced  exposure 
to   mercury   and   degraded   water   sources)   will   enhance 
household well-being beyond financial aspects.  
Over  the  project  lifetime, Participatory  Rural  Appraisal  (PRA) 
activities will be conducted to monitor the project’s impacts and 
gather feedback from the community.

outcome  will  be  more  sustainable,  reliable,  and  profitable

reduced poverty among agricultural households.

Change in well-being 
For the wider community, the project is expected to bring broad-
based  improvements  in  quality  of  life.  Enhanced  education 
opportunities  will  expand  prospects  for  younger  generations, 
while  community  grants  will  strengthen  social  infrastructure 
and  cohesion.  The  protection  of  forests  and  peatlands  will 
secure vital ecosystem services that sustain water, air, and soil 
quality,   directly   supporting   public   health   and   household 
security. Besides,  the  prevalence  of  preventable  diseases  is 
expected to be reduced through the health outreach program. 
Employment  opportunities  will  increase  household  income 
across   villages,   helping   to   reduce   poverty   and   economic 
inequality. Cultural and spiritual well-being will be reinforced as 
customary  lands  and  sacred  sites  are  preserved.  While  some 
short-term  grievances  may  arise  due  to  land-use  restrictions, 
the net impact on the wider population will be strongly positive. 
Over  the  project  lifetime, PRA  activities  will  be  conducted  to 
monitor  the  project’s  impacts and  gather  feedback  from  the 
community. 
 
4.2.2 Negative Community Impact Mitigation (VCS, 3.19; CCB, CM2.2) 
The  project  is  designed  to  deliver  net  positive  benefits  to  all  community  groups  in  the  project 
zone.  Nonetheless,  certain  project  activities, such  as  restrictions  on  unsustainable  practices, 
increased  human  presence  in  forests,  and  livelihood  transitions, may  lead  to  short-term  or 
unintended  negative  impacts  on  community  well-being.  Consistent  with  the  precautionary 
principle,  the  project  will  identify  these  risks  early,  monitor  them  regularly,  and  implement 
proactive  measures  to  prevent  or  minimize  harm, particularly  for  women,  children,  and 
marginalized groups. 
The table below summarizes potential negative impacts, their underlying factors, and mitigation 
measures adopted by the project.

4.2.3 Net Positive Community Well-Being (VCS, 3.19; CCB, CM2.3, GL1.4) 
The  project’s  interventions  are  designed  to  deliver  net  positive  well-being  impacts  across  all 
identified  community  groups: traditional  gold  miners,  smallholder  farmers  and  estate  crop 
workers, and the wider village population. By reducing reliance on extractive activities, restoring 
degraded  peatlands,  and  promoting  sustainable  livelihood  alternatives,  the  project  improves 
income stability and health outcomes while strengthening local institutions and environmental 
stewardship.   Traditional   gold   miners   benefit   from   alternative   employment   and   training 
opportunities that reduce exposure to mercury and hazardous working conditions. Smallholder 
farmers  and  estate  crop  workers  gain  from  agroforestry  programs  and  improved  ecosystem 
services  that  enhance  soil  fertility,  water  availability,  and  productivity.  Meanwhile,  the  broader 
village population experiences improved social cohesion, community infrastructure, and access 
to  education and healthcare.  Collectively,

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template

300 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
As discussed  in section 3.4, the  project  zone  is  highly  vulnerable  to  the  adverse  impacts  of 
climate  change,  particularly  shifting  rainfall  patterns,  prolonged  dry  seasons,  and  increased 
frequency of floods and fires on degraded forests. Without intervention, these dynamics threaten 
essential ecosystem services, reduce agricultural productivity, and undermine community health 
through recurring haze events and loss of clean water sources. Communities that already depend 
on  unstable  livelihoods  such  as  artisanal  gold  mining  or  plantation  wage  labor  would  face 
heightened risks from climate-induced shocks, lacking the institutional and financial capacity to 
adapt on their own. 
Through its integrated conservation and restoration strategy, KPCP aims to meet the Gold Level 
for  climate  change  adaptation  benefits by directly  strengthening the  adaptive  capacity  of  local 
communities to  probable  impacts  of  climate  change. Wetland protection and  fire  prevention 
measures will reduce the risk of catastrophic forest and peat fires, thereby safeguarding public 
health  and  protecting  food  and  water  resources.  Agroforestry  systems  combining  native  trees 
with locally  preferred crops will provide  climate-resilient  income  opportunities  while  improving 
soil fertility and water retention, buffering households against droughts and floods. In parallel, 
training  in  sustainable  non-timber  forest  product  (NTFP)  management  and  employment  in 
restoration, patrolling, and monitoring diversify household revenues, decreasing dependence on 
climate-sensitive and extractive activities. 
In  addition,  the  project  enhances  community  resilience  through  participatory  governance  and 
early-warning  preparedness.  Community  patrols,  supported  by  training  and  equipment,  act  as 
the  first  line  of  defense  against  fire  outbreaks  during  prolonged  dry  seasons. To  strengthen 
monitoring capacity, the project employs NASA’s FIRMS hotspot detection system to provide near-
real-time alerts of potential fire activity, enabling rapid local response before fires escalate into 
large-scale  events. Engagement  with  schools  through  eco-clubs  and  curriculum  integration 
ensures that younger generations understand climate risks and adaptive land-use practices. By 
maintaining  critical  ecosystem  services,  diversifying  livelihood  sources,  and  empowering  local 
institutions, the project provides a robust foundation for long-term  adaptation.  These  activities 
demonstrate  measurable  climate  adaptation  benefits  for  both  communities  and  biodiversity, 
meeting the requirements for Gold Level under the CCB Standards. 
4.2.4 High Conservation Values Protected (CCB, CM2.4) 
The project will ensure that none of the High Conservation Values (HCVs) related to community 
well-being  are  negatively  affected  by  project  activities.  Protection  and  restoration  efforts  will 
maintain the integrity of HCV 4 areas, including peatlands, riparian forests, erosion control zones, 
and pollination habitats, which provide vital ecosystem services such as water regulation, flood 
prevention,  erosion  control,  and  fire  risk  reduction.  These  areas  will  be  safeguarded  through 
forest patrols, rehabilitation of degraded lands with native species, and careful management of 
riparian buffer zones. 
For  HCV  5  resources,  such  as  rivers  that  supply  fish,  household  water,  and  other  subsistence 
needs, the project will maintain unrestricted community access and actively monitor water quality 
to ensure safety and long-term availability. Project activities, including reforestation and improved 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
301 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
land  management,  will  help  stabilize  watersheds  and  sustain  fish  populations  critical  to  local 
needs. 
Regarding HCV  6  cultural  values,  the  project  will  fully  respect  sacred  sites  of  the  Dayak  Ngaju 
communities, including Sandung, Patahu, and Pasah Kamantuhu. No project activities will take 
place within these designated areas, and buffer zones will be maintained to ensure their cultural 
and spiritual functions remain undisturbed. The project recognizes the customary importance of 
these sites and will uphold community rights to practice traditional rituals. 
If  new  HCV  areas  of  community  importance  are  identified  during  implementation,  they  will  be 
incorporated into project management plans and safeguarded to avoid any negative impact. 
4.3 Other Stakeholder Impacts  
4.3.1 Impacts on Other Stakeholders (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, CM3.1) 
The  project  activities  are  not  expected  to  generate  negative  impacts  on  other  stakeholders, 
including residents living outside the project area. Instead, they stand to benefit from improved 
landscape-level management of natural resources, enhanced protection of ecosystem services 
such as water and soil regulation and strengthened forest conservation efforts that contribute to 
regional  environmental  stability.  In  addition,  the  project’s  emphasis  on  sustainable  land-use 
practices  and  diversified  livelihood  opportunities  is  likely  to  support  broader  community 
resilience and economic stability beyond the project boundaries. 
4.3.2 Mitigation of Negative Impacts on Other Stakeholders (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, 
CM3.2) 
As  discussed  above,  negative  impacts  on  stakeholders  outside  the  project  area  are  not 
anticipated,  and  such  risks  are  considered  highly  unlikely.  Accordingly,  no  specific  mitigation 
measures are deemed necessary. 
4.3.3 Net Impacts on Other Stakeholders (VCS, 3.18, 3.19; CCB, CM3.3) 
The project activities are not expected to cause harm to other stakeholders and instead provide 
opportunities for broader benefits. 
4.4 Community Impact Monitoring  
4.4.1 Community Monitoring Plan (CCB, CM4.1, CM4.2, GL1.4, GL2.2, GL2.3, GL2.5) 
The  project  will  implement  a  structured  community  monitoring  plan  to  track  how  project 
interventions affect local households, community groups, and other stakeholders over time. This 
plan is designed to measure progress toward project objectives, verify the delivery of intended 
social benefits, and ensure that community well-being is maintained or enhanced. The results of 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
302 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
monitoring  will  directly  inform  adaptive  management,  enabling  the  project  team  to  adjust 
strategies where needed to maximize positive outcomes. 
A  combination  of  qualitative  and  quantitative  methods  will be  applied,  including  household 
surveys, key informant interviews, focus group discussions (FGDs), and participatory monitoring 
approaches. Secondary  data  sources,  such  as  government  statistics,  academic  studies,  and 
publicly available reports, will also be used where relevant to complement primary data collection 
and strengthen the robustness of findings. These methods will ensure that the perspectives of 
different social groups—particularly women, youth, and vulnerable households—are represented 
and integrated into project evaluation. Where relevant, data will be disaggregated by gender and 
other demographic factors to capture differentiated impacts. 
Monitoring  results  will  be  regularly  analyzed  and  shared  with  community  members  and 
stakeholders  through  feedback  sessions  and  village  meetings,  creating  a  cycle  of  continuous 
learning   and   adaptive   management.   This   participatory   approach   not   only   strengthens 
accountability  but  also  empowers  local  communities  to  be  active  partners  in  shaping  project 
outcomes.  The  key  categories,  indicators,  frequency,  and  data  sources  for  monitoring  are

4.4.2 Monitoring Plan Dissemination (CCB, CM4.3) 
The  project  will  ensure  that  the  monitoring  plan  and  the  results  from  its  implementation  are 
disseminated  in  a  transparent  and  accessible  manner  to  both  local  communities  and  external 
stakeholders.  All  official  monitoring  reports  will  be  made  available  on the  Verra  Registry  to 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
307 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
guarantee  public  access.  Summaries  of  key  findings  will  be  prepared  in  Bahasa  Indonesia  to 
ensure broad comprehension. 
At  the  community  level,  the  project  team  will  actively  communicate  results  through  village 
meetings,  participatory  forums,  and  workshops.  By  combining  digital,  written,  and  face-to-face 
dissemination  methods,  the  project  will  ensure  that  information  is  accessible  across  different 
literacy levels and communication preferences. These will allow communities and stakeholders 
to  ask  questions,  provide  feedback,  and  suggest  improvements,  reinforcing  transparency  and 
mutual accountability throughout the project's lifetime. 
Furthermore,  the  project  will  provide  annual  update  sessions  where  monitoring  results  and 
lessons learned are presented, discussed, and linked to adaptive management decisions. These 
sessions  will  allow  communities  and  stakeholders  to  ask  questions,  provide  feedback,  and 
suggest  improvements,  reinforcing  transparency  and  mutual  accountability  throughout  the 
project’s lifetime. 
4.5 Optional Criterion: Exceptional Community Benefits  
4.5.1 Exceptional Community Criteria (CCB, GL2.1) 
Data from the Central Bureau of Statistics of Indonesia (BPS) in March 2025
58
 indicate that the 
national  poverty  line  is IDR 609,160  per  capita  per  month.  With  the  average  poor  household 
consisting  of  about  4.72  members,  this  translates  into  a  household  poverty  threshold  of 
approximately IDR 2,875,235  per  month.  When  compared  with  the  socio-economic  baseline 
survey  conducted  in  the  project  area,  the  results  demonstrate  that  a  large  proportion  of 
households  fall  below  or  very  close  to  this  poverty  threshold.  Specifically, in 6 out  7  villages 
surveyed (Humbang   Raya, Hanua, Lawang   Uru, Manen   Keleka, Manen   Paduran,   and 
Parahangan), the majority of households reported monthly incomes between IDR 1,500,000 and 
IDR 2,500,000 million, while another significant share earns less than IDR 1,500,000 million. 
Meanwhile, the proportion of households reporting no income at all is remarkably high in certain 
villages,  including  Manen  Kaleka,  Parahangan,  and  Lawang Uru. It  is also notable  that  a 
significant proportion of respondents fall into the irregular income category due to deep reliance 
on  informal  and  uncertain  sources  of  livelihood  such  as  artisanal  gold  mining,  seasonal  wage 
labor, and small-scale trading. Only a very small share of households reported stable earnings 
above IDR 5,500,000 per month. 
Taken  together,  this  income  distribution  shows  that  more  than  half  of  the  households surveyed 
are  living  below or  close  to the  nationally  defined  poverty  line.  This  finding  supports  the 
conclusion that the project area qualifies under the Exceptional Community Criteria, as set out 
in  the  CCB  Standards.  By  targeting  these underdeveloped communities,  the  project  aims  to 
create  sustainable  livelihood  improvements  that  raise  incomes  beyond  the  poverty  threshold 
through  a  combination  of  livelihood  diversification,  strengthened  forest  management,  and

improved   access   to   social   benefits.   In   addition,   the   project’s   long-term   environmental 
interventions, such as restoration activities and fire risk reduction, are expected to generate co-
benefits for local households, further supporting their resilience and overall well-being. 
4.5.2 Short-term and Long-term Community Benefits (CCB, GL2.2) 
The project is designed to deliver tangible well-being improvements for local communities in both 
the  immediate  and  long-term  future.  In  the  short  term,  benefits  will  focus  on  improving 
livelihoods, safety, and participation opportunities, while in the long term, the project will build 
resilience, strengthen governance capacity, and sustain the economic and ecological foundations 
upon which community well-being depends. The following benefits are anticipated: 
Short-term benefits (first 1–5 years) 
• Employment  opportunities through  the  recruitment  of  local  community  members  for 
restoration  work,  patrols,  fire  response  units,  nursery  management,  and  monitoring 
teams. 
• Skill development and training in sustainable land management, fire prevention, first aid, 
and nursery techniques. 
• Diversified income and increased income through agroforestry practices and NFTPs.  
• Improved  higher  education  attainment rate,  especially  among  girls,  through  targeted 
scholarship program. 
• Increased environmental awareness among youth through partnership with student eco-
clubs. 
• Increased  healthcare  access  and  awareness through healthcare  workshops which 
address preventable illnesses and promote basic emergency care. 
• Preserved cultural values and traditional customs through community support grants 
• Inclusive participation of women, youth, and marginalized groups in decision-making and 
project activities, fostering equity and representation. 
Long-term benefits (5+ years and beyond) 
• Improved ecosystem  services through  forest  protection  and  restoration activities  that 
safeguard water resources, reduce fire and flood risks, and help regulate local climate 
conditions. 
• Stronger community resilience through education, health, and livelihood programs that 
build adaptive capacity and improve the long-term well-being of households.

• Increased overall literacy  levels  across  generations through scholarship programs that 
broaden access to higher education opportunities, strengthening resilience to economic 
and environmental shocks. 
• Inclusive   and   sustainable economic development through   diversified   low-carbon 
livelihoods and strengthened governance that ensure equitable economic opportunities 
and intergenerational benefits. 
4.5.3 Community Participation Risks (CCB, GL2.3) 
Community participation risks were identified through participatory consultations, baseline social 
surveys,  and  the  FPIC  process.  This  inclusive  approach  emphasized  open  dialogue,  equal 
representation, and transparent decision-making. Village meetings, focus group discussions, and 
household  interviews  created  opportunities  for  all  groups  to  share  potential  concerns,  while  a 
grievance mechanism was established to allow community members to raise issues at any time. 
Through  this  process,  the  following  risks  were  identified,  along  with  measures  designed  to 
manage them: 
Crop failure in agroforestry systems 
Agroforestry  systems  are  unfamiliar  to  many  households,  and  the  transition  from  traditional 
farming  can  be  challenging.  Crop  failure  may  occur  due  to  poor  species  selection,  pest 
infestations, or extreme weather events that are increasingly common in the project area. 
To  address  this,  agroforestr y  systems  will  be  co-designed  with  communities  and  agricultural 
experts, ensuring that species and planting systems are adapted to local conditions. Training will 
cover    sustainable    farming,    pest    management,    and    diversification    techniques,    while 
demonstration  plots  will  showcase  best  practices.  This  participatory  and  evidence-based 
approach minimizes crop failure risks and strengthens farmer ownership. 
Lack of participation 
Certain groups, including women, youth, migrants, or residents of remote settlements, may face 
barriers to joining project activities due to distance, time constraints, or social norms that limit 
their involvement in community decision-making. If not addressed, these barriers could lead to 
unequal access to project benefits. 
The   project   will   maintain   participation   through   regular   village   meetings,   transparent 
communication,  and  participatory  planning  processes  guided  by  FPIC.  Special  measures  will 
target women, youth, and marginalized groups. For example, women-only meetings will be held 
to  gather  their  input on  the  implementation  of  certain  project  activities  such  as agroforestry 
species choices  and the  selection  of  healthcare  outreach  topics. Meeting  notes  and  decisions 
will be disseminated through village noticeboards, and community bulletins to ensure that those 
unable to attend are still informed and able to provide input later. Finally, accessible grievance 
mechanisms will ensure concerns are promptly addressed, reinforcing trust and inclusiveness. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
310 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Safety risks during project activities 
Project  activities  such  as  forest  patrols,  firefighting,  and  large-scale  tree  planting  involve 
physically  demanding  tasks  that  expose  participants  to  risks  such  as  heat  stress,  injuries,  or 
accidents  in  remote  areas.  These  risks  may  discourage  participation  or  lead  to  harm  if  not 
properly managed. 
To reduce risks, all patrol and fire team members will be trained in occupational health and safety 
(OHS)  and  equipped  with  protective  clothing,  GPS  devices,  radios,  and  fire  suppression  tools. 
Standard  operating  procedures  will  be  developed  and  enforced, ensuring  consistent  safety 
practices  in  the  field.  Tree  planting  and  nursery  work  will  also  follow  clear  safety  protocols, 
including first aid preparedness, to protect all participants. 
Poor management of project activities 
If activities are poorly managed due to limited technical capacity, resource constraints, or weak 
oversight, communities may lose confidence in the project, and benefits may not be distributed 
fairly. This could undermine participation and long-term support. 
To  address  this,  the  project  will  establish  robust  monitoring  and  accountability  mechanisms. 
Activity implementation and community fund allocations will be regularly reviewed to ensure that 
resources  are  appropriately  used  and  that  marginalized  households are  not  disadvantaged.  At 
the  same  time,  targeted  training  and  mentoring  will  be  provided  to  build  the  capacity  of  local 
facilitators, enabling them to manage activities effectively and equitably. Qualified personnel with 
relevant  experience  will  also  be  recruited  to  oversee  technically  demanding  activities,  such  as 
nursery operations and restoration planning, ensuring that management standards remain high. 
By integrating a community-based adaptive management approach, the project will introduce a 
system  of  checks  and  balances  that  enforces  rules,  strengthens  democratic  governance  of 
natural resources, and prevents inefficiencies or mismanagement from taking root.  
Elite capture 
Power  imbalances  within  communities  may  allow  influential  individuals  to  dominate  decision-
making  or  control  access  to  project  resources,  leading  to  unequal  benefits  and  exclusion  of 
vulnerable households. This dynamic has been observed in other rural development projects and 
represents a real risk here if not carefully managed. 
To  avoid  this,  the  project  will strengthen  inclusive  representation  and  transparent  decision-
making. Participation in decision-making process will be structured to include a balanced mix of 
members—such as women, youth, migrants, and poorer households—so that no single group can 
dominate. All project-related meetings will keep written records of attendance, discussions, and 
decisions, which will be shared publicly through village information boards and communication 
with  village  governments.  This  openness  creates  accountability  and  places  natural  checks  on 
decision-making. Meanwhile, transparent  benefit-sharing mechanisms will be  subjected to 
community  review  as  well  as  project oversight. Grievance  channels  will  provide  an additional 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
311 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
safeguard to  challenge  unfair  practices  and ensure  that  resources  reach  all households 
equitably.  
By  embedding  these  safeguards  in  project  design,  the  risks  of  participation  are  addressed 
proactively,  ensuring  that  all  community  members, particularly  vulnerable  groups, can  benefit 
from the project in a safe, inclusive, and equitable manner. 
4.5.4 Marginalized and/or Vulnerable Community Groups (CCB, GL2.4) 
The project has identified two primary community groups that are marginalized and/or vulnerable 
within the project zone: (i) women, and (ii) poor households. Both groups have historically had 
limited influence in village-level decision-making processes and are disproportionately exposed 
to livelihood and health risks stemming from forest degradation, climate variability, and economic 
marginalization.  The  project  will  ensure  that  activities  are  designed  to  generate  net  positive 
impacts for these groups by enhancing their participation, strengthening livelihood security, and 
putting safeguards in place to reduce the risk of exclusion or harm.

4.5.5 Net Impacts on Women (CCB, GL2.5) 
Baseline assessments show that women are central to household food security and family well-
being but remain underrepresented in formal decision-making. They are also disproportionately 
affected by forest degradation, climate variability, and land fires, as they depend on forest plants 
such as Kalakai (Stenochlaena  palustris) for nutrition, manage subsistence farming, and carry 
primary  responsibility  for  family  health.  These  pressures subsequently increase  their  daily 
workload, heighten health risks, and reduce available productive time, underscoring the need for 
project interventions that directly improve women’s well-being and empowerment. 
The  project  is  committed  to  creating  meaningful  opportunities  for  women  to  participate  in, 
influence, and benefit from project activities. These measures include: 
• Inclusive  participation  in  governance  and  FPIC  processes:  Ensuring  that  women  are 
actively  represented  in  village  forums,  consultations,  and  project  decision-making 
platforms,  with  specific  facilitation  to  amplify  their  voices  where  they  have  historically 
been excluded. 
• Livelihood  diversification  and  training:  Providing  women  with  skills  and  resources  for 
sustainable agroforestry, improved subsistence farming, and NTFP enterprises, thereby 
reducing reliance on degraded forest resources and enhancing economic security. 
• Employment   opportunities:   Recruiting   women   into   project-based   roles—including 
restoration,  nursery  management,  monitoring,  and  administration—with  a  target  to 
increase their share of formal employment within the project. 
• Health  and  safety  programs:  Delivering  health  awareness  workshops,  first  aid  and 
emergency  response  training,  and  improving  access  to  information  on  women’s  and 
children’s health, particularly in relation to preventable illnesses. 
• Education   and   youth   empowerment:   Promoting   scholarships   and   environmental 
education  programs  with  a  commitment  to  gender  balance,  ensuring  that  girls  benefit 
equally from educational opportunities.

Through these initiatives, the project will not only reduce the disproportionate burdens women 
face but also strengthen their agency in shaping community development. By embedding gender-
responsive approaches across project activities and promoting women’s leadership, the project 
ensures  that  women  are  not  passive  beneficiaries  but  active  drivers  of  conservation  and 
livelihood  outcomes.  The  cumulative  effect  of  these  measures  will  be  improved  well-being, 
increased  economic  independence,  and  enhanced  participation  of  women  in  community 
governance, demonstrating clear net positive impacts.

4.5.7 Benefits, Costs, and Risks Communication (VCS, 3.18; CCB, GL2.7) 
The project team has taken proactive steps to ensure that smallholders and community members 
clearly understand the predicted and actual benefits, costs, and risks associated with the project. 
During  the  initial  round  of  consultations  in  all  eight  villages  within  the  project  zone,  dedicated 
sessions were organized to present this information in plain language, with translation into local 
Dayak dialects when necessary. Visual aids such as posters and maps were used to explain how 
activities  like  agroforestry,  fire  prevention,  and  forest  patrols  generate  benefits,  while  also 
discussing the labor requirements, opportunity costs, and potential risks. 
To suppor t comprehension and informed decision-making, the project team developed brochures 
and   handouts   summarizing   the   project   objectives,   implementation   steps,   benefit-sharing 
mechanisms,  and  grievance  redress  channels.  These  materials  were  distributed in  Indonesian 
language. Special emphasis was placed on inclusivity: women, youth, and marginalized groups 
were actively encouraged to participate in discussions and ask questions. Feedback from these 
sessions  indicated  that  communities  appreciated  the  clarity  of  information  and  valued  the 
opportunity to openly discuss both benefits and risks before giving consent. 
Following each consultation, official records of the meetings were prepared, reviewed collectively 
in   village   forums,   and   signed   by   community   representatives   as   confirmation   of   their 
understanding. These records are archived and made accessible for reference, and summaries 
are posted on village notice boards for transparency. The project will continue to communicate 
updates  on  realized  benefits,  emerging  costs,  and  unforeseen  risks  through  village  meetings, 
and written circulars, ensuring that communities remain informed throughout the lifetime of the 
project. 
4.5.8 Governance and Implementation Structures (CCB, GL2.8) 
The  project  is  governed  through  a  multi-layered  structure  that  integrates  project  management, 
local government authorities, and customary Dayak institutions to ensure both accountability and 
inclusivity. At the community level, project activities are coordinated through village forums and 
sub-village  groups,  which  serve  as  platforms  for  participatory  decision-making.  These  forums 
allow smallholders, women, youth, and marginalized groups to voice their perspectives, ensuring 
that project design and implementation reflect community priorities. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
315 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
To  suppor t  implementation,  community  members  are  engaged  as  field  staf f  in  restoration, 
agroforestry, and forest protection activities. Recruitment prioritizes local labor, enabling direct 
benefits while strengthening ownership of project outcomes. Selected community members are 
trained  and  appointed  as  coordinators  or  team  leaders,  bridging  communication  between  the 
project proponent and villagers. This structure provides pathways for community members not 
only to participate in operational roles but also to assume middle-management responsibilities 
in line with their skills and aspirations. 
The  governance  system  is  further  reinforced  through  participatory  monitoring  and  evaluation, 
where community representatives are involved in tracking progress and reporting outcomes. A 
grievance redress mechanism is in place to safeguard transparency and accountability, ensuring 
that concerns raised by smallholders or other stakeholders are promptly addressed. The project’s 
reliance on Free, Prior and Informed Consent (FPIC) principles throughout all stages, from design 
to  long-term  monitoring, ensures  that participation  is  voluntary,  informed,  and  equitable. 
Collectively, these governance and implementation structures are designed to embed community 
self-governance capacities, foster collaboration across stakeholder groups, and enable the full 
and effective participation of smallholders in decision-making and project execution. 
4.5.9 Smallholders/Community Members Capacity Development (CCB, GL2.9) 
Capacity  development  is  a  central  strategy  of  the  project,  recognizing  that  long-term  success 
depends  on  the  ability  of  smallholders,  local  communities,  and  institutions  to  participate 
effectively in project design, implementation, and management. Training programs are designed 
not only to build technical skills but also to strengthen organizational and leadership capacities 
at the village level, ensuring that benefits are sustained beyond the project’s lifespan. 
The project will implement a broad range of capacity development activities, including: 
• Sustainable land management and agroforestry training: Hands-on training for farmers 
on species selection, planting design, soil and water conservation, and integrated pest 
management, with particular emphasis on resilience to climate variability. 
• Forest protection and fire management: Training and equipping community patrol units 
and Fire Response Units (FRUs), including modules on early warning systems, safe fire 
suppression techniques, and biodiversity monitoring. 
• Livelihood  diversification:  Programs  to  support  community  members  in  strengthening 
livestock  management,  developing  non-timber  forest  product  (NTFP)  enterprises,  and 
exploring market opportunities for sustainable commodities. 
• Community governance and financial management: Strengthening the capacity of village 
institutions  and  cooperatives  to  operate  transparent  benefit-sharing mechanisms  and 
lead participatory planning processes. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
316 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
• Environmental education and awareness: School-based environmental workshops, youth 
engagement  activities,  and  scholarships  to  encourage  the  next  generation  to  become 
active stewards of the forest. 
• Health and safety training: Training in workplace safety standards and basic first aid for 
all community members involved in field activities. 
Special  attention  will  be  given  to  disadvantaged  groups,  including  women  and  youth,  whose 
participation is often limited in formal decision-making structures. By deliberately targeting these 
groups, the project seeks to expand opportunities, promote gender equality, and strengthen the 
social fabric of participating villages. 
All training activities will be monitored and evaluated through feedback surveys and participatory 
assessments  to  measure  knowledge  uptake  and  practical  application.  Lessons  learned  will  be 
incorporated into subsequent training cycles, ensuring continuous improvement and alignment 
with community needs. Through this approach, the project builds both individual and institutional 
capacity,  embedding  the  skills  and  systems  required  for  effective,  community-driven  forest 
conservation and sustainable development.

5 BIODIVERSITY 
5.1 Without-Project Biodiversity Scenario 
5.1.1 Existing Conditions (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B1.1) 
The  KPCP  concession  area  is  a  Forest  Utilization  Business  Permit  (PBPH)  holder,  spanning 
approximately  13,580 hectares  in  Central  Kalimantan  Province.  The  project  area  lies  within  a 
tropical rainforest climate (Köppen Af), making it highly suitable for ecosystem restoration. The 
region  experiences  consistently  high  rainfall  year-round,  with  an  annual  average  ranging  from 
3,093  to  3,387  mm;  in  2024,  the  total  reached  3,305  mm.  Temperatures  remain  stable  at 
around 27.37 °C with little seasonal variation, supporting the growth of diverse tropical rainforest 
ecosystems. 
Geologically, the concession is underlain by the Dahor Formation and Alluvial Deposits, forming 
a flat to gently undulating landscape situated within the Barito and Kahayan River Basins, both 
of which are vital for hydrological regulation and ecological resilience. The largest land cover of 
the project’s concession area is Secondary Swamp Forest, spanning 10,687 hectares (78.7%), 
consisting  of  degraded  yet  recoverable  forests dominated  by  swamp  vegetation,  including 
Dipterocarpaceae  species.  This  habitat  demonstrates  high  flora  biodiversity  with Shorea 
teysmanniana (Meranti  lilin)  identified  as  the  most  dominant  species  across  all  regeneration 
phases. Peatlands, though smaller in extent (229 hectares), are ecologically significant as carbon 
storage  systems,  while  riparian  zones  along  the  Mangkutub,  Hai,  and  Kaliwang  Rivers  provide 
critical habitats and essential ecosystem services. 
Non-forest areas within the project concession are divided into three main land cover types: wet 
shrubland, estate crops, and bare ground. Wet shrubland is the largest, spanning 2,607 hectares 
(19.2% of the area), and consists of unmanaged wetlands dominated by shrub vegetation. These 
areas  are  ecologically  vulnerable,  particularly  to  forest  fires  along  degraded  peatlands  or 
disturbed  secondary  growth  zones,  as  evidenced  by  remnants  of  charred  wood. Estate crops, 
mainly  community-managed  oil  palm  plantations,  cover 143 hectares  (1.05%).  While  these 
contribute to local livelihoods, their expansion—especially in the western part of the concession—
poses  a  significant  threat  to  remaining  forest  habitats  critical  for  Rare,  Threatened,  or 
Endangered  (RTE)  species,  highlighting  the  need  for stricter  land-use  regulations  and  forest 
buffers.  Finally, bare ground  accounts  for 106 hectares (0.8%)  and  represents  areas  cleared 
through logging or road development. With little to no vegetation, these lands face heightened 
risks of soil erosion, further degrading the ecological balance of the area. 
5.1.1.1 Flora diversity 
The flora diversity study within the concession applied a stratified sampling approach based on 
land cover representation. Thirteen transects, each 2 km in length, were established, with 0.25-
hectare sample plots containing five nested subplots to capture different tree size classes such 
as seedlings,  saplings,  poles,  small  trees,  and  large  trees,  categorized  by  Diameter  at  Breast 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
318 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Height (DBH). Data collected included species identification, DBH measurements, and individual 
counts across size classes.  
Analysis  focused  on  key  ecological  indices  such  as  Importance  Value  Index  (IVI),  and  the 
Shannon-Wiener Diversity Index (H'). The Importance Value Index (IVI) is a composite metric used 
to quantify the ecological significance of plant species by integrating measures of their relative 
density (KR), relative frequency (FR), and relative dominance (DR) within a community. Higher IVI 
values  indicate  species  that  play  a  structurally  or  functionally  dominant  role  in  the  ecosystem. 
The  Shannon–Wiener  Diversity  Index  (H′)  is  a  quantitative  metric  used  to  evaluate  community 
biodiversity by integrating both species richness and species evenness into a single value. It is 
derived  from  information  theory,  where  the  uncertainty  of  predicting  the  species  identity  of  a 
randomly selected individual increases with greater diversity. 
The flora assessment revealed a significant diversity of plant life, with specific dominant species 
and notable conservation values. A total of 124 plant species from 40 families were found from 
the field data collection. The Dipterocarpaceae family was the most common, with 21 species, 
which is consistent with Kalimantan's rich biodiversity for this family, both in terms of abundance 
and species diversity. The concession forest of KPCP, which is a swamp forest, has 20 species 
that  are  classified  as  endangered  according  to  the  IUCN  (
Table 83
).  Species  such  as Shorea 
teysmanniana and Gonystylus bancanus listed in 
Table 83
 are classified as Critically Endangered 
by the IUCN, indicating that they are at very high risk of extinction unless significant conservation 
efforts are made. Similarly, species such as Agathis borneensis, which is listed as Endangered, 
are highly vulnerable to existing threats from human activities and climate change. Gonystylus 
bancanus is also one of the endemic tree species in swamp forests whose timber trade is listed 
in CITES Appendix II due to logging.

Data  analysis  of  species  dominance  was  conducted  using  the  Importance  Value  Index  (INP) 
across regeneration strata (
Table 84
). At the seedling level, Vatica  rassak was  most  dominant, 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
320 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
followed  by Tristaniopsis  merguensis and Shorea  teysmanniana.  At  the  sapling  stage, Shorea 
teysmanniana showed  clear  dominance,  accompanied  by Shorea  uliginosa and Vatica  rassak, 
indicating  its  strong  persistence  across  early  regeneration  phases.  At  the  pole  stage, Shorea 
teysmanniana again ranked highest, followed by Shorea uliginosa and Vatica rassak, reflecting 
their  adaptability  to  mid-regeneration  growth.  In  the  small  tree  phase, Shorea  teysmanniana 
maintained superiority, with Vatica rassak and Tristaniopsis merguensis contributing significantly 
to  stand  structure.  Finally,  at  the  large  tree  level, Shorea  uliginosa exhibited  exceptional 
dominance,  shaping  canopy  structure  and  biomass,  while Shorea  teysmanniana and Vatica 
rassak remained  important  components.  These  results  demonstrate  the  consistent  ecological 
role  of  Dipterocarp  species,  particularly Shorea teysmanniana and Shorea uliginosa, in driving 
forest regeneration and structural formation.

The KPCP concession forest consists of two land cover types: secondary swamp forest and wet 
shrubland.  Swamp  forests  are  critical  ecosystems  that  support  high  biodiversity  and  play  an 
essential  role  in  maintaining  ecological  stability,  particularly  through  the  presence  of  endemic 
tree species. Secondary swamp forest refers to areas previously disturbed, such as by logging, 
that have undergone natural regeneration, while wet shrubland is characterized by open wetlands 
dominated by shrubs and bushes. Species richness and diversity differ markedly between these 
two habitats, with secondary swamp forest supporting 120 species compared to only 24 in wet 
shrubland.  Diversity  indices  further  highlight  this  contrast,  with  secondary  swamp  forest 
recording  a  higher  value  (H’  =  3.40)  than wet  shrubland (H’  =  2.30).  Specific comparison of 
diversity  in  each  land  cover  type  is  reflected in 
Table 85
.  The  greater  species  richness  and 
evenness  in  secondary  swamp  forest  indicate  a  more  balanced  and  stable  ecosystem,  likely 
supported  by  higher  vegetation  density,  greater  resource  availability,  and  improved  habitat 
stability. In contrast, wet shrubland’s limited structure and openness result in lower diversity and 
fewer tree populations.

5.1.1.2 Fauna diversity 
Herpetofauna 
Herpetofauna  surveys  were  conducted  in  the  project  area  using  the  Visual  Encounter  Survey 
(VES)  method along  thirteen  transects, each  extending  2  km in  length,  with  observations 
performed   at   50-meter intervals between   successive   segments where microhabitats of 
amphibians  and  reptiles such  as fallen  logs,  leaf  litter,  water  margins,  tree  bark,  tree  cavities, 
and  remnant  vegetation  structures are  likely  to  be  found. The  surveys  were carried  out  during 
both daytime  hours (09:00–12:00)  and,  on  selected  transects, night  hours (19:00–22:00)  to 
capture  both  diurnal  and  nocturnal  species,  with  each  encounter  systematically  documenting 
species identity and abundance.  
A total of 25 species (147 individual observations) from 15 families were recorded, of which two 
were  identified  as threatened under  IUCN  Red  List  categories (
Table 86
).  The  calculated 
Shannon-Wiener diversity index (H′ = 2.42) indicates a moderately high level of species diversity, 
and an Evenness index (E = 0.79) reflects a relatively balanced distribution of individuals across 
species, signifying stable community structure within the surveyed habitats (
Table 87
). 
Mammals 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
322 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
The  mammal  diversity  assessment  was  conducted  using  three  complementary  methods  to 
enhance detection across different species and activity patterns. 
• 
Line transects:
 Conducted along thirteen transects, each approximately 2 kilometers in 
length,  systematically  distributed  across  the  Assessment  Area.  This  method  targeted 
both diurnal and crepuscular species in a structured and repeatable manner. 
• 
Reconnaissance  walks  (recce  walks
): Involved  walking  routes  through  various  habitat 
types  to  opportunistically  observe  mammals  that  might  be  missed  during  structured 
transects, especially in less accessible or more varied terrain. 
• 
Camera  traps:
 Four  units  deployed  for  about one  month  at  random  but  purposively 
selected  locations  within  the  study  area.  Site  selection  considered  signs  of  mammal 
activity, habitat types, and landscape features likely to be used by wildlife. 
Mammal surveys recorded 24 species (132 individual observations) representing 18 families, of 
which 12 species were identified as threatened under IUCN Red List categories. This assemblage 
included four endangered species, six vulnerable species, and two critically endangered species 
(
Table 86
).  Notably, one  of  the  large  mammals  observed  was Bornean Orangutan (Pongo 
pygmaeus), a species classified as Critically Endangered by the IUCN (2016)
59
 and afforded full 
protection under Indonesian national law (Government Regulation No. 106 of 2018
60
). Diversity 
analysis produced a Shannon-Wiener index (H′) of 2.85, approaching the threshold of 3 generally 
indicative of a rich and diverse community, while the high Evenness index (E = 0.90) reflects a 
balanced species distribution and strong ecosystem stability across the study area (
Table 87
). 
Wildlife  monitoring  was  further  complemented using camera  traps  deployed  at  four  locations 
within  the  study  area.  This  method provides valuable  supplementary  data,  particularly  for 
mammal species that are elusive or primarily nocturnal and therefore less likely to be detected 
through  direct  observation.  The  camera  traps  recorded  three  species:  the  Lesser  Mouse-deer 
(Tragulus  kanchil),  the  Sun  Bear  (Helarctos  malayanus),  and  the  Bearded  Pig  (Sus  barbatus), 
contributing  to  a  more  comprehensive  understanding  of  mammalian  presence  within  the 
landscape.
