Avifauna (Birds) 
The bird survey was conducted using two complementary methods. These methods were used to 
maximize the detectability of different bird species across varied habitats and activity patterns. 
• 
Point Count Method (Point Transect):
 Observers stood at fixed points within each habitat 
type  and  recorded  all  bird  encounters  during  a  specific  time  interval.  Surveys  were 
conducted  in  the  early  morning  (06:00–10:00  local  time),  which  coincides  with  peak 
avian  activity.  Each  transect  was  approximately  2,000  meters  long,  with  10  fixed 
observation points spaced 100-150  meters  apart.  At  each  point,  observers  paused  for 
15 minutes including a 5-minute settling time and 10 minutes of recording. Observations 
were aided by binoculars and digital zoom cameras. All birds observed or heard within a 
100-meter radius were recorded, including species name, individual count, and time of 
detection. 
• 
Reconnaissance Walks (Recce Walks):
 Birds were recorded visually or acoustically using 
the  same  equipment  and  references  as  in  the  point  count  method.  Recce  walks  were 
particularly  useful  for  documenting  habitat  specialists  and  species  with  irregular 
movement patterns. 
Avifaunal surveys conducted across thirteen transects documented a total of 144 species (1,424 
individuals)  representing  44  families,  including  the  migratory  Oriental  Honey-buzzard  (Pernis 
ptilorhynchus).  Of  the  recorded  species, 11 were  classified  as  threatened,  comprising of 10 
vulnerable and one endangered species, based on IUCN Red List categories (
Table 86
). Diversity 
analysis  yielded  a  Shannon-Wiener  index  (H′)  of  4.37  and  an  Evenness  value  (E)  of  0.88, 
indicating  a  highly  diverse  avian  community  with  near-uniform  species  distribution  (
Table 87
). 
These values are comparatively high for tropical forest ecosystems, reflecting favorable habitat 
conditions capable of supporting a wide range of bird guilds and ecological functions
61
. 
The  bioacoustics  survey was employed  as  an  addition,  using  the  SwiftOne  recording  device 
developed  by  the  Cornell  Lab  of  Ornithology,  a  specialized  instrument  designed  for  long-term, 
passive monitoring of wildlife through acoustic detection. This approach facilitated non-intrusive 
data  collection  in  dense  and  remote  forest  habitats  where  direct  visual  observation  is  often 
 
61
 Bibby, C. J., et al. (2000). Bird Census Techniques (2nd ed.). Academic Press. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
324 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
limited. Analysis of the recordings confirmed the presence of several key species, including the 
White-bearded  Gibbon  (Hylobates  albibarbis),  as  well  as  notable  avifauna  such  as  the  Black 
Hornbill (Anthracoceros malayanus), Banded Kingfisher (Lacedo pulchella), Red-headed Trogon 
(Harpactes erythrocephalus), and Chestnut-winged Babbler (Cyanoderma erythropterum).

5.1.1.3 Threats to biodiversity 
Swamp  forests  within  the  KPCP  project  area  represent  critical  ecosystems  that  support  high 
biodiversity  and  provide  essential  ecological  functions,  particularly  through  the  presence  of 
endemic and threatened tree species. However, these ecosystems are under increasing pressure 
from both anthropogenic activities and climate change. Major threats include land conversion, 
habitat   degradation,   and   unsustainable   resource   exploitation,   often   driven   by   economic 
vulnerability and small-scale mining. The expansion of community-managed oil palm plantations, 
construction of logging roads, and abandonment of farmlands—have accelerated habitat loss and 
fragmentation. Additionally,  high  dependency  on  forest  resources  for  livelihoods  heightens  the 
risk of overharvesting and long-term ecosystem decline if left unmanaged. These pressures are 
particularly critical for the conservation of Rare, Threatened, and Endangered (RTE) plant species 
such as Shorea teysmanniana (CR) and Gonystylus bancanus (CR, CITES Appendix II), which rely 
heavily on the integrity of peat swamp forests for survival. 
Faunal  diversity  in  the  project  area  also  faces  multiple  and  compounding  pressures,  with 
deforestation  and  land  conversion  identified  as  the  most  severe.  Expansion  of  oil  palm 
plantations,  clearing  of  land  for  logging  roads,  and  habitat  degradation  caused by  agricultural 
activities  have  reduced  the  availability  of  suitable  habitats  for  wildlife.  Hunting  pressure, 
evidenced  by  snares  and  traps,  has  contributed  significantly  to  the  decline  of  large  mammals; 
while recurring fires in wet shrubland and peatland continue to degrade ecosystems that provide 
critical habitat for both terrestrial and aquatic fauna. Furthermore, community dependence on 
forest  resources,  combined  with  limited  economic  alternatives,  increases  the  likelihood  of 
unsustainable harvesting practices and long-term biodiversity loss. These cumulative pressures 
threaten not only common species but also vulnerable and endangered fauna, undermining the 
ecological stability of the landscape. 
Flora  and  fauna  threats  are  tightly  linked,  with  deforestation,  mining,  and  fires  simultaneously 
reducing  plant  diversity  and  disrupting  wildlife  habitats.  Together,  these  drivers  accelerate 
biodiversity  loss  and  highlight  the  need  for  integrated  conservation  measures  that  protect 
habitats, regulate resource use, and safeguard RTE species. 
Species and habitat  
The  project  will  not  adversely  impact  habitats  of  rare, 
threatened, or endangered species; instead, it strengthens 
them   through   activities   such   as   forest   conservation, 
assisted natural regeneration, and enrichment planting with 
native  species.  These  measures  restore  degraded  areas, 
maintain  forest  cover,  and  reduce  pressures  from  illegal 
logging and fire, thereby improving ecological conditions for 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
327 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
sensitive  species  like  dipterocarps,  hornbills,  and  other 
forest-dependent fauna. 
The list of RTE species can be found in 
Table 83
 and 
Table 
86
 
Areas needed for habitat 
connectivity 
The project also safeguards and enhances areas critical for 
habitat   connectivity   by   preventing   fragmentation   and 
maintaining  continuous  forested  landscapes  across  the 
concession.  Restoration  efforts,  including  replanting  and 
assisted natural  regeneration  in  strategic  zones,  reinforce 
ecological corridors that allow species movement between 
remaining forest patches and neighboring protected areas, 
ensuring long-term genetic exchange and resilience of local 
biodiversity. 
The list of RTE species can be found in

 and

5.1.2 High Conservation Values (CCB, B1.2) 
The project area harbors several High Conservation Values (HCVs) that are critical for biodiversity 
conservation. Based  on  field  surveys,  community  consultations,  and  spatial  analysis  following 
the Common Guidance for the Identification of High Conservation Values (HCVN, 2019
62
) and the 
HCV-HCSA  Assessment  Manual  (2023
63
),  the  project  identified habitats that support a  wide 
range of species, including rare, threatened, and endangered species (HCV 1); relatively natural 
peat swamp forest ecosystems that maintain ecological connectivity and habitats for key predator 
species with large  range  requirements (HCV  2);  and secondary  swamp  forests  qualify  as 
threatened  ecosystems that  require protection (HCV  3). Further  details  about  these  HCVs  are 
presented in the subsections below. 
5.1.2.1 HCV 1 – Species diversity 
 
High conservation value 
HCV 1 – Species diversity 
Qualifying attribute 
Areas  that  contain  concentrations  of  species  of  conservation 
significance,  including  rare,  threatened,  endangered  (RTE),  or 
endemic  species,  as  well  as  unique  species  assemblages. In 
KPCP project area, based on the baseline survey, there are: 
• 124 species of flora

5.1.2.2 HCV 2 – Landscape-level ecosystems and mosaics 
 
High conservation value 
HCV 2 – Landscape-level ecosystems and mosaics 
Qualifying attribute 
The project area retains relatively natural and intact peat swamp 
forest  ecosystems,  as  evidenced  by  land  cover  history  showing 
limited  conversion  to  non-forest  uses.  Its  forest  cover  remains 
connected with surrounding peat swamp forests, forming part of 
a functioning   hydrological   unit   that   supports   ecological 
continuity  across  the  landscape.  When  compared  objectively 
with  other  ecosystem  types  within  the  same  landscape  and 
hydrological system, the project area represents one of the more 
natural and less disturbed forest blocks, thereby strengthening 
its ecological significance within the regional mosaic. 
 
Additionally, biodiversity surveys confirmed the presence of the 
Sunda  Clouded  Leopard  (Neofelis  diardi),  a  key  predator  and 
umbrella species that requires extensive habitat with sufficient 
prey and cover. The occurrence of such a species indicates that 
the  project  area  provides  functional  habitat  connectivity  and 
supports  ecological  processes  critical  for sustaining  viable 
populations of wide-ranging fauna. Thus, even though the area 
is fragmented, it can be qualified for HCV 2 as it plays a crucial 
role   in   maintaining   ecological   diversity   and   predator-prey 
dynamics in a broader landscape. 
Focal area 
All forested areas within the PT RAL concession and the broader 
Area  of  Interest  (a  5-km  buffer  around  the  concession),  with 
particular  importance  given  to  secondary  swamp  forests (see 
Figure 64
)

330 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Figure 64: HCV 2 – Landscape-level ecosystems and mosaics 
 
5.1.2.3 HCV 3 – Threatened or rare ecosystems 
 
High conservation value 
HCV 3 – Threatened or rare ecosystems 
Qualifying attribute 
Ecosystems  that  are  rare,  threatened,  or  endangered  due  to 
natural  limitations  or  human-induced  pressures. The  project 
area    contains    secondary    swamp    forests and    localized 
peatlands, both classified as a threatened ecosystem under the 
Indonesian  HCV  Toolkit
66
 due  to  pressures  from  drainage,  fire, 
and  land  conversion. The  secondary  swamp  forest,  though 
regenerating, retains important ecological functions and habitat 
characteristics that provide refuge for forest-dependent species 
in   an   increasingly   fragmented   landscape. Meanwhile,   for 
peatlands, despite their limited spatial coverage and moderate 
depth,   they perform   critical   ecological   functions,   including 
carbon  storage,  water  retention,  and  hydrological  connectivity 
with surrounding wetlands. 
Focal area 
The secondary swamp forests (10,687 Ha) and peatlands (229 
Ha) within the PT RAL concession boundary and the broader

5.1.3 Without-project Scenario: Biodiversity (CCB, B1.3) 
Without  the  KPCP,  the  PT  RAL  concession  would  undergo  rapid  ecological  decline  under  a 
combination  of  legal  plantation  conversion,  community-driven  land  use  change,  extractive 
activities, and unsustainable resource use. These pressures would eliminate structurally diverse 
habitats, fragment corridors, drive the loss of species populations and species diversity, as well 
as degrade  rare  ecosystems  such  as  peat  swamp  forests.  The  cumulative  impacts  would 
compromise  High  Conservation  Value  areas,  diminish  ecosystem  services,  and  transform  the 
concession into a simplified and degraded landscape unable to sustain its ecological values over 
the long term. 
The PT RAL concession was subjected to its legally approved permit, which designated the area 
for development into an Industrial Timber Plantation (HTI). The concession’s flat to gently sloping 
terrain is considered ideal for plantation activities, and the business plan explicitly outlined large-
scale forest clearance followed by monoculture planting of fast-growing commercial species such 
as  Eucalyptus  and  Acacia.  Implementation  of  this  plan  would  result  in  the  near-total  loss  of 
structurally  diverse  natural  forest  habitat.  The  replacement  of  native forests with  monoculture 
stands would eliminate vertical and horizontal complexity, erasing the diverse microhabitats that 
support a wide range of species. Wildlife adapted to mixed-species canopies, dense understory 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
332 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
vegetation, and heterogeneous forest structures would face local extirpation, while fragmentation 
of  remaining  corridors  would  isolate  populations  and  restrict  genetic  exchange,  accelerating 
declines in species richness. 
Conversion of natural swamp and peat forests to facilitate monoculture plantations would also 
threaten rare ecosystems (secondary swamp forest and peatland). These systems, which provide 
specialized  habitats  for  rare  and  threatened  species,  cannot  be  replicated  within  industrial 
plantations.  Road  construction  and  canalization  to  facilitate  HTI  planting  would  further  disrupt 
natural  hydrology,  fragment  habitats,  and  expand  access  to  previously  remote  forest  areas. 
Drainage canals in particular would lower peatland water tables, causing subsidence, peat dome 
collapse, and long-term  degradation.  These  processes  would  undermine  ecosystems,  and  over 
time,  intact  peatland  and  swamp  forests  would  risk  irreversible  transformation  into  degraded, 
open landscapes that are extremely difficult and costly to restore. 
The  HTI  model  would  also  heighten  the  risk  of  forest  fires.  Monoculture  systems  are  more 
vulnerable  to  pest  outbreaks  and  drought  stress,  which  weaken  vegetation  and  increase 
flammability. Eucalyptus species in particular shed flammable litter and contain volatile oils that 
ignite easily, creating continuous fuel loads across plantation areas. Combined with drained peat 
soils,  this  creates  a  highly  fire-prone  landscape.  Fires  have  already  been  recorded  in  wet 
shrublands,  and  forest-edge  areas,  and  without  intervention,  recurrent  burning  would  destroy 
habitats,  cause  high  mortality  among  ground-dwelling  and  slow-moving  fauna,  and  transform 
ecosystems  irreversibly.  Such  events  pose  severe  risks  to  species  diversity,  by  eliminating 
habitats  for  Rare,  Threatened,  and  Endangered  (RTE)  species,  and  to  local  landscape,  by 
damaging peatland hydrological functions, causing shrinkage, compaction, and subsidence. 
Beyond the HTI plan, additional pressures would exacerbate biodiversity loss. Community-driven 
expansion of oil palm plantations, particularly in the western landscape, poses one of the most 
immediate threats. Illegal logging adds to these pressures, particularly along the Sungai Kaliwang 
corridor,  where  intensive  and  unregulated  timber  extraction  has  already  been  observed.  Such 
activities  result  in  direct  forest  loss,  further  fragmentation,  and  the  degradation  of  habitats 
critical for RTE species. Vulnerable flora such as Shorea teysmanniana and Gonystylus bancanus 
(Critically  Endangered,  CITES  Appendix  II)  depend  on  intact  forest  cover  for  survival,  and  their 
persistence is directly threatened by continued logging. 
Wildlife   populations   are   further   jeopardized   by   widespread   hunting   and   poaching.   Field 
assessments recorded traps, firearms, and hunter shelters within the concession, confirming the 
prevalence  of  these  activities.  Species  targeted  include  wild  boar  (Sus  scrofa),  barking  deer 
(Muntiacus  spp.),  sambar  deer  (Rusa  unicolor),  mouse-deer  (Tragulus  spp.),  pangolins  (Manis 
spp.),  and  slow  lorises  (Nycticebus  spp.),  several  of  which  are  protected  or  threatened.  Large 
mammals  are  already  notably  scarce  due  to  historic  hunting,  and  the  Critically  Endangered 
Bornean Orangutan (Pongo pygmaeus), which relies on intact forests, is particularly vulnerable 
to further decline in population. Unsustainable fishing methods, including poisoning and electric 
shock   devices,   further   undermine   aquatic   biodiversity   and   water   quality,   compounding 
ecosystem degradation. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
333 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
5.2 Net Positive Biodiversity Impacts  
5.2.1 Expected Biodiversity Changes (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.1) 
The project area has not undergone any large-scale clearing or drainage of natural ecosystems 
within  the  past  ten  years.  The  dominant  habitats  remain  secondary  swamp  forest,  riparian 
corridors,  and  wet  shrubland  mosaics,  all  of  which  continue  to  support  important  ecological 
functions despite historic disturbances from selective logging and localized land clearing. These 
ecosystems retain their capacity to provide habitat, regulate hydrology, and sustain biodiversity, 
thereby  qualifying  the  project  zone  as  a viable  foundation  for  restoration  and  long-term 
conservation. The presence of these habitats also demonstrates that the project does not involve 
conversion of natural ecosystems, but rather their protection and rehabilitation. 
Building on this baseline, the project activities are expected to deliver measurable improvements 
to  biodiversity  over  the  project  lifetime.  By  preventing  deforestation,  suppressing  fire,  and 
restoring degraded areas, the project will enhance the condition of natural habitats and create 
more stable ecological niches for flora and fauna. The following analysis details the anticipated 
changes  across three biodiversity  elements:  natural  ecosystem conditions,  flora, and  wildlife 
diversity.

fire  damage,  gradually  reducing  its  ecological  value.  With  the 
project,  these  pressures  are  actively  addressed  through  legal 
designation    of    the    concession area for    conservation, 
community-based  patrols  that  deter  illegal  logging,  and  fire-
prevention teams trained to detect and control hotspots before 
they   spread.   Restoration   and   agroforestry   efforts   further 
stabilize   degraded   sites   and reduce   reliance   on   forest

Justification of change 
Without  the  project,  recurring  fires,  illegal  logging,  and  other 
human disturbances would continue to degrade forest structure. 
These  pressures  would  favor  invasive  grasses  and  shrubs, 
suppress natural regeneration, and create conditions for pests 
or  diseases  to  spread  unchecked.  In addition,  illegal  logging 
would likely target  slow-growing,  high timber-value  Dipterocarp 
species,  leading  to  long-term  loss  of  these  keystone  trees  and 
further   reduction   in   species   diversity.   With   the   project, 
restoration  through  planting  native  species,  assisted  natural 
regeneration, and especially fire prevention will directly address 
these   risks.   As   canopy   cover   recovers   and   microclimate 
stabilizes, invasive species will be less able to dominate. At the 
same   time, prevention   of illegal   logging   will   safeguard 
Dipterocarps  and  other  vulnerable  species,  allowing  them  to 
regenerate  and  maintain  their  essential  ecological  role  within 
the forest ecosystem.  
Changes in flora will be monitored by comparing the presence of 
threatened plant species over time against the baseline records. 
Regular  patrols  and  biodiversity  surveys  will  document  the 
occurrence of these species, while field teams will note any new 
invasive plants or signs of pest damage. In addition, vegetation 
monitoring will be carried out using PSPs for trees.

Increase  in  population  size,  species  richness,  and  species 
presence across the project area. 
Justification of change 
Without  the  project,  the  persistence  of  illegal  logging  and 
poaching    would    further    fragment    habitats    and    reduce 
populations  of  forest-dependent  wildlife.  Mammals  such  as 
primates, deer, and small carnivores would face increasing risk 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
335 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
from snaring and habitat loss. Birds would continue to decline 
as  fire  and  degradation  simplify  forest  structure,  leaving  only 
generalist  and  edge  species  to  dominate.  Amphibians  and 
reptiles,  highly  sensitive  to  fire  and  microclimatic  shifts,  would 
lose their breeding grounds and moist habitats. With the project, 
multiple     interventions     address     these     threats     directly. 
Community-based patrols using SMART will deter poaching and 
illegal   logging,   while   fire   prevention   efforts   reduce   the 
destruction of habitats that wildlife depend on. Restoration and 
assisted   natural   regeneration   will   gradually   rebuild   forest 
structure,    offering    more    nesting,    roosting,    and    feeding 
opportunities     for     birds,     mammals,     and     herpetofauna. 
Agroforestry near villages will decrease pressure on core forests 
by   providing   alternative   resources   and   livelihoods,   while 
awareness-raising    campaigns    reduce    hunting    incentives. 
Together, these actions are expected to increase the abundance 
and diversity of wildlife over the project lifetime. 
Wildlife  diversity  will  be  monitored  using  a  combination  of 
various  methods  such  as  camera  traps  for  mammals,  point 
counts for birds, and simple night/day transects for amphibians 
and reptiles. Patrol teams will also record signs of wildlife such 
as tracks, dung, or direct sightings during their regular routes. In 
addition, Participatory Rural Appraisal (PRA) sessions with local 
communities  will  help  track  hunting  pressure  and  changes  in 
species presence. 
 
5.2.2 Mitigation Measures (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.3) 
The project’s core interventions, forest conservation and restoration, are inherently aligned with 
biodiversity  protection  and  enhancement.  As  no  large-scale  clearing,  drainage,  or  habitat 
disturbance is  planned  under  project  activities,  there  are  no  anticipated  negative  impacts  on 
biodiversity that would require corrective mitigation. Instead, as described in section 5.2.1, the 
project  is  expected  to  deliver  significant  positive  outcomes  across  key  biodiversity  elements, 
including the protection and recovery of natural ecosystems, enrichment of floral diversity, and 
the  increase in  population  size,  species  richness,  and  species  presence of  wildlife within  the 
landscape. 
In  line  with  the  precautionary  principle,  the  project  has  nonetheless  adopted  complementary 
measures to reinforce biodiversity safeguards and ensure the maintenance and enhancement of 
High Conservation Value (HCV) attributes. These measures are not reactive responses to threats 
caused  by  the  project  itself  but  proactive  strategies  to  further  strengthen  forest  protection, 
prevent external pressures, and raise community stewardship. These measures include: 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
336 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
• Strengthening legal protection through partnership with both local and regional forestry 
and environmental authorities. 
• Raising biodiversity awareness through community engagement. 
• Enhancing forest patrols in HCV areas. 
5.2.3 Net Positive Biodiversity Impacts (CCB, B2.2, GL1.4) 
The KPCP project is expected to deliver clear net positive impacts on biodiversity compared with 
conditions under the without-project scenario. In the absence of the project, the concession was 
at  high  risk  of  being  converted  into  an  Industrial  Timber  Plantation  (HTI), along with  planned 
allocations for monoculture crops such as acacia, palm oil, and other estate commodities. Such 
conversion  would  have  resulted  in  extensive  large-scale  clearing  of  secondary  swamp  forest, 
riparian habitats, and wet shrubland mosaics. The replacement of these diverse ecosystems with 
monoculture  plantations  would  drastically  reduce  habitat  heterogeneity,  fragment  remaining 
wildlife corridors, and accelerate the decline of flora, herpetofauna, mammals, and bird diversity 
across the landscape. The loss of natural vegetation would also increase susceptibility to pests, 
invasive species, and fire, further undermining ecosystem resilience. 
By contrast, under the with-project scenario, the concession is managed for forest conservation, 
restoration,  and  sustainable  community  engagement.  Conservation  of  remaining  secondary 
swamp  forests  and  riparian  buffers  maintains  critical  ecosystem  functions,  while  enrichment 
planting  and  assisted  natural  regeneration  in  degraded  areas  enhance  structural  and  species 
diversity.  These  interventions  are  expected  to  stabilize  and  gradually  increase  populations  of 
native flora and fauna, particularly species of conservation concern such as hornbills, primates, 
and amphibians that depend on intact forest cover. In addition, suppression of illegal activities 
through  regular  patrols  and  collaboration  with  local  authorities  reduces  direct  pressure  on 
biodiversity.   Complementary   community   outreach   sessions   are also conducted   to   raise 
awareness  of  biodiversity  protection,  ensuring  that  local  residents  understand  the  ecological 
value of the project area and are empowered to actively participate in safeguarding it.  
In  the  long  run,  the  project’s  landscape-level  design will contribute significantly to  sustaining 
ecosystem  services. The  protection  of  riparian  areas will lead  to improvement  in hydrological 
regulation and water quality, benefiting both biodiversity and human communities. Reconnected 
forest patches will strengthen habitat corridors, allowing wildlife to move across the project zone 
and   into   adjoining   protected   forests,   thereby   supporting   genetic   diversity   and   long-term 
population  viability.  These  measures  ensure  that  the  project  does  not  simply  avoid  negative 
impacts but actively creates net biodiversity gains, in contrast to the widespread losses projected 
under the without-project scenario. 
Finally, the project will directly assist biodiversity in adapting to the probable impacts of climate 
change.  Anticipated  changes  in  Kalimantan  include  increased  rainfall  variability,  extended 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
337 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
drought periods, and heightened fire risk
67
, which are the factors that threaten the persistence 
of  swamp  forest  ecosystems.  Through  its  conservation  and  restoration  strategies,  the  project 
addresses these vulnerabilities: 
• 
Habitat  corridors  and  connectivity:
 By  restoring  degraded  areas  and  reinforcing  natural 
corridors,  the  project  enables  species  to  migrate  in  response  to  shifting  climatic  zones, 
ensuring continued access to food and breeding habitats. 
• 
Hydrological stabilization:
 Protection of riparian zones and peat swamp ecosystems maintain 
water storage and release functions, buffering biodiversity against floods and droughts. 
• 
Species  and  structural  diversity:
 Mixed-species  planting  and  enrichment  improve  forest 
redundancy  and  resilience,  reducing  vulnerability  to  climate-driven  pests,  diseases,  and 
invasive species. 
• 
Community-based  fire  prevention:
 Patrols,  early-warning  systems,  and  awareness-raising 
reduce the risk of catastrophic fires, one of the most severe climate-exacerbated threats to 
biodiversity in peatland ecosystems. 
5.2.4 High Conservation Values Protected (CCB, B2.4) 
The project ensures that no High Conservation Values (HCVs) related to biodiversity are negatively 
affected.  The  area  encompasses  HCV  1  habitats  that  support  endangered  and  threatened 
species; HCV 2 landscapes that are relatively natural and intact, as well as forests that serve as 
habitat for key predator species; and HCV 3 ecosystems represented by secondary swamp forests 
classified as endangered in Indonesia. Without the project, these values would be at risk from 
conversion to industrial plantations, illegal logging, and recurrent fires. Through forest protection, 
restoration,   and   community-based   monitoring,   the   project   conserves   these   habitats   and 
reinforces their ecological functions. Additional measures such as fire prevention, patrols, and 
local awareness programs further reduce threats. As a result, the project delivers clear positive 
impacts on these biodiversity-related HCVs rather than any negative effect. 
5.2.5 Species Used (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.5, B2.6) 
The  project  will  employ  a  combination  of  agroforestry  and  reforestation  approaches  to  restore 
degraded  areas  and  enhance  community  livelihoods.  For  agroforestry,  approximately  220 
hectares in the western portion of the project zone—close to residential areas—will be allocated 
to  species  that  balance  economic  value  with  ecological  benefits.  For  reforestation,  a  total  of 
1,329 hectares will be restored over a five-year implementation plan through both active planting 
and  assisted  natural  regeneration,  prioritizing  native  species  that  support  long-term  forest 
recovery.

5.2.6 Invasive Species (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.5) 
The project will not introduce invasive species into the concession area, as all species selected 
for use are native and already occur naturally within the local ecosystem. 
5.2.7 GMO Exclusion (CCB, B2.7) 
The project will not use genetically modified organisms (GMOs) in any aspect of its activities to 
generate  GHG  benefits.  Furthermore,  all  planting  efforts  within  the  project  will  rely  solely  on 
seeds sourced from natural, non-GMO origins. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
340 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
5.2.8 Inputs Justification (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.8) 
The  project’s  agroforestry  program  will  be  implemented  near  residential  areas  and  on  non-
wetland sites, integrating native tree species with locally preferred crops such as durian (Durio 
zibethinus), Rubber (Hevea  brasiliensis),  mango  (Mangifera spp.),  and  other  culturally  and 
economically important plants. This diversified approach is designed to strengthen community 
livelihoods  while  restoring  ecological  functions  through  sustainable  land  management.  By 
combining  perennial  fruit  trees  with  short- and  medium-term   crops,   the   program   offers 
communities an alternative to monoculture farming and helps reduce pressures on surrounding 
forests. 
To  ensure  productivity  and  viability,  cer tain  agricultural  inputs  may  be  required,  par ticularly 
during the establishment phase when soils need improvement and crops are more vulnerable to 
pests  and  diseases.  While  the  project  promotes  minimal  use  of  external  inputs,  targeted 
applications  of  organic  soil  amendments  such  as  compost,  manure,  and  biochar  may  be 
introduced to enhance soil fertility, support seedling growth, and improve long-term soil health. 
These practices are consistent with sustainable land management and are designed to remain 
supportive of long-term ecological and livelihood goals. 
No chemical pesticides will be used in the project to avoid risks to human health, biodiversity, 
and water quality. Instead, pest management will prioritize ecological balance through integrated 
approaches,  which  may  include  the  careful  use  of  biological  control  agents  to  suppress  pest 
populations naturally. These agents will be applied in a controlled manner to protect crops and 
young  seedlings  while  minimizing  potential  impacts  on  non-target  species.  Communities  will 
receive  training  on  the  safe  handling, application,  and  disposal  of  all  inputs  to  ensure  that 
environmental  safeguards  are  upheld.  A  summary  of  proposed  inputs,  justifications,  and 
potential adverse effects is provided in the table below.  
Table 91: Inputs justifications 
Name

5.2.9 Waste Products (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B2.9) 
The project will apply a structured waste management system that prioritizes reduction, reuse, 
and  recycling  to  minimize  environmental  impacts.  Waste  products  generated  from  project 
activities will be identified and classified as organic or inorganic at the source. Organic waste, 
such  as  vegetation  residues  from  restoration  and  agroforestry  activities,  will  be  composted  or 
naturally decomposed on-site to enrich soils. Inorganic waste, including plastics and metals, will 
be separated and directed toward existing local recycling initiatives where possible. Any residual 
non-recyclable  waste  will  be  collected  and  properly  disposed  of  through  government-managed 
facilities, ensuring compliance with local regulations and preventing pollution within the project 
area. 
5.3 Offsite Biodiversity Impacts  
5.3.1 Negative Offsite Biodiversity Impacts (CCB, B3.1) and Mitigation Measures (CCB, 
B3.2)

The  project’s  conservation  and  restoration 
activities   are   expected   to   generate   net 
positive  outcomes  for  biodiversity,  and  no 
adverse  effects  are  foreseen  either  within 
the project area or in adjacent landscapes. 
As    no    negative    biodiversity    impacts    are 
projected  beyond  the  project  boundary,  the 
implementation of additional mitigation 
measures is not required. However, the project 
will actively conduct precautionary monitoring, 
if potential threats to biodiversity are detected, 
actions will be taken immediately. 
5.3.2 Net Offsite Biodiversity Benefits (VCS, 3.19; CCB, B3.3) 
The  project’s  conservation  and  restoration  activities  are  anticipated  to  produce  net  positive 
biodiversity  outcomes  both  within  and  beyond  the  project  zone.  By  protecting  remaining  peat 
swamp forest and restoring degraded areas, the project strengthens habitat connectivity within 
the   wider Central Kalimantan   landscape,   facilitating   species   movement   and   reducing 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
342 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
fragmentation. No unmitigated negative impacts on biodiversity outside the project boundary are 
expected,  as  project  activities  do  not  involve  land  conversion  or  practices  that  could  harm 
adjacent  ecosystems.  On  the  contrary,  the  anticipated  spillover  benefits—such  as  reduced  fire 
risk,  decreased  hunting  pressure,  and  increased  habitat  quality—will  extend  into  surrounding 
areas, ensuring that the overall effect of the project on biodiversity is strongly positive. 
5.4 Biodiversity Impact Monitoring  
5.4.1 Biodiversity Monitoring Plan (CCB, B4.1, B4.2, GL1.4, GL3.4) 
The project has established a robust biodiversity monitoring framework that captures ecological 
changes  across  ecosystems,  species  groups,  and  habitats.  The  framework  is  grounded  in 
established  scientific  and  participatory  methods such as remote  sensing analysis,  permanent 
sample  plots,  transect  surveys,  camera  traps,  and  point  counts,  ensuring  consistency  with 
baseline  assessments  while  remaining  practical  for  long-term  implementation.  Monitoring 
activities  are  designed  to  track  progress  toward the expected  positive biodiversity changes 
outlined in section 5.2.1, assess the effectiveness of restoration and protection measures, and 
evaluate  outcomes  for  High  Conservation  Values  (HCV  1,  2 and  3).  In  developing  this  plan, 
considerations  were  given  to  ecological  priorities,  logistical  feasibility,  staff  and  community 
capacities,  and  alignment  with  Gold  Level  criteria  for  climate  adaptation  and  exceptional 
biodiversity benefits. The detailed indicative monitoring framework is presented in Appendix 3: 
Biodiversity Monitoring Framework. 
To   ensure   that   the   biodiversity   monitoring framework is  both  scientifically  rigorous  and 
operationally feasible, its implementation within the KPCP concession will follow a phased and 
systematic  approach.  The  first phase will  be  the  establishment  of  permanent  monitoring  sites 
and  transects.  These  include  PSPs  for  vegetation  monitoring,  transects  for  herpetofauna  and 
mammals, fixed stations for bird point counts, and designated patrol zones for natural ecosystem 
observations.  Where  necessary,  light  maintenance  of  trails  will  be  conducted  to  ensure  safe 
access  by  patrol  teams  and  monitoring  staff,  while  minimizing  disturbance  to  vegetation  and 
avoiding  the  creation  of  new  access  routes  that  could  increase  external  threats.  By  co-locating 
different  monitoring  activities  along  shared  transects  and  patrol  routes,  the  project  maximizes 
efficiency, reduces duplication, and lowers operational costs. 
The second phase is capacity building. All monitoring will be carried out by trained project staff 
in close collaboration with community members who already participate in fire prevention units 
and forest patrols. Training will cover survey protocols, species identification, data recording, data 
management, and  the  use  of  digital  tools  such  as  camera  traps, GPS  units,  and Spatial 
Monitoring   and   Reporting   Tool   (SMART).   By   embedding   monitoring   responsibilities   into 
community-based patrol structures, the project ensures both long-term  sustainability  and  local 
ownership  of  biodiversity  outcomes.  Regular  refresher  training sessions and  participatory 
analysis   sessions   will   help   maintain   quality   and   strengthen   local   capacity   for   adaptive 
management. 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
343 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
In  KPCP, the  use  of  SMART will  be  central  to  the  monitoring  system,  functioning  both  as  a 
biodiversity data platform and as a management tool for forest protection. SMART will allow patrol 
teams  to  record  georeferenced  data  on  threats  (e.g.,  evidence  of  illegal  logging,  snares,  or fire 
hotspots), patrol coverage, and wildlife observations. The system enables standardized collection 
and analysis of indicators across space and time, generating outputs such as maps, charts, and 
summary tables. These outputs serve three purposes: 
• Track the effectiveness of patrols and fire prevention teams 
• Monitor changes in threat levels through standardized threat indices 
• Contribute wildlife encounter data to broader biodiversity assessments.  
SMART  thus  creates  a  feedback  loop  where  data  directly  informs  decision-making,  from  daily 
patrol planning to annual strategy reviews. The keys steps in using SMART as a tool for adaptive 
management are as follows: 
• 
Patrol  Data  Collection
:  Field  teams  document  patrol  routes,  record  encounters  with 
threats such as illegal logging, poaching signs, or fire scars, and note wildlife sightings 
or other ecological observations. 
• 
Debriefing Sessions:
 After patrols, teams present their findings and supervisors review 
the accuracy and completeness of the recorded information. 
• 
Data  Entry:
 Collected  records  are  systematically  uploaded  into  the  SMART  database, 
creating a centralized and secure repository of patrol and biodiversity data. 
• 
Analysis and Reporting:
 The system processes this information into clear outputs—tables, 
charts,  and  geospatial  maps—that  illustrate  patrol  coverage,  threat  intensity,  and 
observed biodiversity trends. 
• 
Feedback  and  Planning:
 Results  are  discussed  in  regular  meetings  with  rangers  and 
project staff, where past performance is evaluated, adaptive strategies are developed, 
and future patrol objectives are set. 
Apart from the SMART system, the monitoring methodologies adopted by the project are effective 
because  they  integrate  rigorous  scientific  protocols  with  approaches  that  are  feasible  for  long-
term, community-led implementation. Camera traps offer an unbiased and non-invasive method 
to record medium- and large-bodied mammals, including cryptic or nocturnal species that would 
otherwise remain undetected. The standardized placement and rotation of these traps generate 
datasets  that  can  be  compared  across  time, providing  insights  into  species  richness,  relative 
abundance, and population trends. Point counts and transect surveys extend coverage to birds 
and herpetofauna, enabling the project to monitor taxa that are highly sensitive to habitat quality 
and microclimatic conditions. These methods are straightforward to implement, allowing trained 
community   members   to   contribute   meaningfully   while   maintaining   scientific   robustness. 
Permanent  Sample  Plots  (PSPs)  provide  a  foundation  for  monitoring  vegetation  dynamics, tree 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
344 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
regeneration,  and  species  composition.  By  measuring  variables  such  as  DBH  and  seedling 
density,  PSPs  enable  the  calculation  of  ecological  indices  like  Shannon–Wiener  and  IVI,  which 
track shifts in species diversity and dominance. Remote sensing and drone imagery complement 
these  ground-based  methods  by  offering  a  landscape-level  perspective  on  forest  recovery, 
fragmentation, and fire scars. Together, these tools provide a multi-scale monitoring system that 
connects  local  ecological  changes  with  broader  ecosystem  resilience,  strengthening  adaptive 
management and demonstrating tangible biodiversity outcomes. 
Monitoring of trigger species 
In addition to the broader biodiversity monitoring framework, the project has identified two trigger 
species that require specific monitoring attention, two  of  which  are  classified  as  Critically 
Endangered under the IUCN Red List and one as Endangered (see section 5.1.1.2): 
• Bornean Orangutan (Pongo pygmaeus) – Critically Endangered 
• Sunda Pangolin (Manis javanica) – Critically Endangered 
• Proboscis Monkey (Nasalis larvatus) – Endangered and endemic to Borneo 
These species were selected because of their global conservation importance, their confirmed or 
likely presence in the project zone, and their sensitivity to habitat loss and hunting. For all three 
species, the primary focus of monitoring will be on the threats they face, including deforestation, 
fires,  and  illegal  hunting  or  trade,  while  population-level  monitoring  will  be  undertaken  at 
appropriate intervals to assess long-term outcomes. 
The  core  habitats  for  these  species  are  the  swamp  and  lowland  forests  within  the  project 
concession,  which  are  directly  threatened  by  illegal  logging,  fire,  and  encroachment. In  addition, 
the rivers and peat swamp areas within the project provide suitable habitat for Proboscis monkeys, 
which depend on riparian vegetation and are relatively easier to encounter compared to the other 
trigger  species. To  address  these  threats,  habitat  monitoring  will  rely  on  spatial  analysis  of  land 
cover   change   using   satellite   imagery   and   drone   surveys,   complemented   by   field   patrol 
observations.  Camera  traps  placed  strategically  in  forest  interior  and  riparian  areas  will  provide 
additional confirmation of species presence. By conserving and restoring these natural habitats, 
the project assumes that habitat-related pressures on these trigger species will be reduced, thereby 
stabilizing or enhancing their populations. 
Monitoring of hunting and trade pressures will be conducted through Participatory Rural Appraisal 
(PRA)  surveys.  These  will  collect  information  from  communities  about  whether Orangutans, 
Pangolins,  or Proboscis  monkeys  are  being  hunted,  captured,  or  kept  as  pets,  and  whether 
conservation awareness programs and customary rules have reduced such practices. Patrol teams 
using  the  SMART  system  will  also  record  direct  evidence  of  threats,  including  snares,  poaching 
activity,  or  burrow  disturbance,  and  this  standardized  data  will  be  analyzed  over  time  to  inform 
adaptive management. 
Complementing threat and habitat monitoring, species-specific survey methods will be applied: 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
345 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
• Bornean Orangutan: Nest count surveys along permanent transects will serve as the primary 
method  for  estimating  population  density,  complemented  by  opportunistic  sightings  and 
camera-trap data. 
• Sunda Pangolin: Monitoring will combine burrow counts, spoor identification, and camera-
trap records, recognizing the species’ nocturnal and elusive behavior. 
• Proboscis  Monkey:  Population  monitoring  will  primarily  rely  on  direct  counts  and  visual 
surveys  along  riverbanks  and  swamp  margins  during  boat-based  or  ground  transects, 
supported by camera-trap data in riparian corridors. 
These  monitoring  methods  are  recognized  as  scientifically  robust  and  repeatable,  enabling  the 
project  to  evaluate  both  changes  in  population  status  and  the  effectiveness  of  conservation 
measures over time. By integrating spatial monitoring, SMART-based patrol data, community PRA 
surveys,  and  species-specific  survey  techniques,  the  project  ensures  that  the  threats  to  and 
population  dynamics  of  these  priority  species  are  systematically  tracked.  This  approach  not  only 
supports  adaptive  management  but  also  strengthens  the  project’s  capacity  to  demonstrate 
measurable progress toward achieving the CCB Gold Level for exceptional biodiversity benefits. 
5.4.2 Biodiversity Monitoring Plan Dissemination (CCB, B4.3) 
The biodiversity monitoring plan and its results will be disseminated through multiple transparent 
and accessible channels. Full reports will be made publicly available on the project’s website and 
shared with relevant government agencies such as the Forest Management Unit (KPH), BKSDA, 
and  Provincial  Forestry  Agency. Collaboration  with  the  community  development  division  will 
ensure  that  socialization  activities, such  as  consultation  and  outreach  sessions, also  include 
discussions  on HCV areas, RTE species, and  the  biodiversity  monitoring  programs  being 
implemented. In  addition,  simplified  summaries  and  visual  information  sheets  in  Bahasa 
Indonesia  will  be  distributed  through  village  offices,  posted  on  community  information  boards, 
and presented during regular meetings.  
5.5 Optional Criterion: Exceptional Biodiversity Benefits  
5.5.1 High Biodiversity Conservation Priority Status (CCB, GL3.1) 
The  project  seeks  to  deliver  exceptional  biodiversity benefits and  secure  Gold  Level  validation 
through  the  confirmed  presence  of critically  endangered  and  endangered species  within  the 
project area. In total, 45 species recorded within the project area are classified as threatened 
under the  IUCN  Red  List,  including  4  Critically  Endangered  (CR) species, 14 Endangered  (EN) 
species, and 27 Vulnerable (VU) species (section 5.1.1). In addition, 20 of the identified species 
are also included   in   the   CITES   Appendix,   highlighting   their   international   conservation 
significance. 
5.5.2 Trigger Species Population Trends (CCB, GL3.2, GL3.3) 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
346 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
Under the CCB Standards, certain species are classified as trigger species when they meet one 
of two key conditions. The first condition, vulnerability, applies when a species listed under the 
IUCN  as  Critically  Endangered,  Endangered,  or  Vulnerable is  observed regularly  in  the  project 
area. The second condition, irreplaceability, applies when the project zone holds a notable share 
(1-5%) of a species’ global population at any stage of its life cycle. 
For  the  KPCP  project,  the  initial  biodiversity  assessments, drawing  on  participatory  rural 
appraisal, field observations, and available secondary data, confirmed the presence of numerous 
threatened species. In total, 45 species found within the project area are listed by the IUCN Red 
List as threatened (CR, EN, or VU). From this group, three species have been designated as trigger 
species for focused monitoring: Bornean Orangutan (Pongo pygmaeus), Sunda Pangolin (Manis 
javanica),  and  Proboscis  monkey  (Nasalis  larvatus). These species  are  globally  classified  as 
Endangered/Critically Endangered and are experiencing sharp declines, primarily due to habitat 
destruction, illegal hunting, and trade pressures
68
 
69
. 
The  selection  of  these  species  as  trigger  species  reflects  their  ecological  importance,  high 
conservation  priority,  and  the  urgent  need  for  targeted  protection  measures  within  the  project 
zone. The Bornean Orangutan represents a flagship species for intact and connected peat swamp 
and  lowland  forest  habitats,  the  Sunda  Pangolin  highlights  the  critical  threat  posed  by  wildlife 
trafficking and poaching, while the Proboscis Monkey is an endemic species. In addition to these 
three,  other  threatened  species  identified  will  be  monitored  through  broader  biodiversity 
monitoring protocols, but Bornean Orangutan, Sunda Pangolin, Proboscis Monkey are prioritized 
as indicators of project performance in safeguarding biodiversity. 
The  following  tables  describe  the  population  trends  for  each  of  the  identified  trigger  species, 
outlining  their  status  at  the  start  of  the  project  and  providing  a  qualitative  assessment  of  the 
likely scenarios with and without the project’s intervention.

threatening long-term viability of the species in the area.

swamp  and  peatland  forest,  and  the  establishment  of

for  the  species.  Community  engagement  and  enforcement 
patrols  will  help  reduce  hunting  risks.  These  actions  are 
expected to stabilize the local population trend and improve 
conditions for group persistence within the project zone.

APPENDIX 4: PERFORMANCE

Applicability Conditions 
Condition Justification 
The project meets all applicability conditions 
detailed in Section 4 of methodology VM0047.

The project activities will produce vegetative 
cover allowing for clear spatial delineation of

years, whichever comes first.

will be updated at each verification or every five 
years, whichever comes first. 
Procedure to Define the Performance Benchmark 
Select Stocking Index (SI) 
The  project  decides  to use Enhanced  Vegetation  Index (EVI)  as  the stocking  index  for  the  performance 
benchmark. A typical EVI formula is as follows: 
퐸푉퐼=2.5⋅
(
푁퐼푅−푅퐸퐷
)
(
푁퐼푅+6⋅푅퐸퐷−7.5⋅퐵퐿푈퐸+1
)
 
Where: 
NIR = Near-Infrared reflectance 
RED = Red reflectance 
BLUE = Blue reflectance 
EVI reduces  atmospheric  and  canopy  background  noise,  making  it  more  accurate  than other common 
remote sensing indexes such as Normalized Difference Vegetation Index (NDVI) in dense vegetation areas. 
It incorporates the blue band for improved correction and retains sensitivity in high biomass regions without 
saturating. The study by Priatama et al. (2022) demonstrated that EVI is the best independent variable for 
estimating aboveground biomass (AGB) with the R
2
 of 0.72 and average of verification values of 18,10% 

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
356 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
(9–28.01%)
70
. EVI was derived from remote sensing analysis with Sentinel-2A imagery (2016–2025) using 
10 m resolution data from Bands 2 (blue), 4 (red), and 8 (NIR). To minimize cloud interference, all Sentinel-
2A images within each year were composited using cloud probability data from Google Earth Engine. Only 
pixels with a cloud probability below 15% were included in the annual composite. 
Select Stocking Index (SI) 
The entire project area was divided into equal-sized, contiguous, non-overlapping units (project plots) with 
size  of  0.25  hectares  (50m  x  50m) and  30  representative  plots  are  selected using  systematic  random 
sampling approach. All selected plots are within the wet shrubland and open land within the eligible ARR 
area. The locations of selected project plots are shown below: 
Figure 66: ARR project plots

Appendix 1 of VM0047 v1.1.

Jurisdictional

Ecoregion 
The donor pool area must exclude any

(biome level) as the project.

ecoregion. 
Policy environment

areas of the jurisdictional boundary 
(defined above) with

national  or  subnational government-
funded policy/program providing 
incentives for tree planting that differs 
from that in the project area.

Industrial Timber Plantations, 
consistent  with  the  land  use  of  the 
project  area.  These  concessions  are 
managed  by  the  private  sector,  and 
there  are  no  national  or  subnational 
government-funded policies or 
programs providing reforestation 
incentives within these areas.

project

any AFOLU projects registered under a 
carbon offset program

between   public   and private   lands.

Evaluate project and control plots. 
The donor pool area is subdivided into non-overlapping units, each measuring 0.25 hectares (50m x 50m), 
which  corresponds  to  the  size  of  each  project  plot.  From  this  pool,  approximately  220,000  units  are 
randomly selected to serve as control plots. 
To  assess  the  historical  and  baseline  conditions,  a  time  series  analysis  is  conducted  to  quantify  the 
Stocking Index (SI) for both project and control plots. In line with the VM0047 methodology, SI values for 
the  evaluation  of  multivariate  distance must  be  obtained  from  at  least  three  distinct  time  points:  one 
between t = −10 and t = −8, one between t = −8 and t = −1, and one at t = 0.  

   CCB & VCS Project Description Template  
                                                                                                CCB Version 3.0, VCS Version 4.4 
359 
CCB v3.0, VCS v4.4 
In this project, SI covariates are collected at four specific time points: t = −8, t = −6, t = −3, and t = 0. 
Match control and project plots. 
The multivariate distance between project and control plots is calculated using the Dynamic Time Warping 
(DTW) algorithm applied to the vector of covariates. DTW is a widely used technique for measuring similarity 
between time series data, particularly when sequences may differ in speed or alignment. It is also relatively 
robust to noise. To match control plots to project plots, a k-nearest neighbor optimal matching algorithm is 
employed without replacement. In this project, a 5-nearest neighbor matching strategy is used. 
After the control plots are selected, the relative weight of each control plot matching to one project plot is 
calculated using the following formula:

Ex-ante estimation 
Following the section 8.7 in methodology VM0047, v1.1, The project description must include an ex-ante 
estimation  of  carbon  dioxide  removals  to demonstrate  the  expected  difference  between  modeled 
performance  of  the  project  and  the forecasted  performance  benchmark. The  ex-ante  estimation  is 
conducted following these below steps: 
1. Projected changes in biomass from plantation activity 
Biomass  is  estimated  using  growth  and  yield  models constructed with  data  and  parameters from 
scientific research. The calculation result is shown at section 3.2.2.5.

. Based  on  the  time  series  analysis, the
